Marlex · Salesforce Programme · TargetRecruit Rebuild · Decision Record

TargetRecruit Rebuild — the Decision Record

Replacing a packaged recruitment system with a native build, inside the same live Salesforce org — captured as a set of decisions, one per page: the alternatives weighed, the choice made, and how each fails back.

Decision record · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026 · Nuvolar

What this is. The complete reasoning behind how Marlex removes TargetRecruit and stands up its own native recruitment system in its place — grounded in a full audit of the live production org, not estimates. It is a proposed strategy for peer and governance review, not a settled plan.

The core idea in one sentence: build a complete new native recruitment system — data model, Apex, flows, apps, screens, reports — inside the same live org, deployed but switched off, then activate it and move the data at a single planned cutover, with TargetRecruit kept intact underneath as the fallback until we're confident. Nobody works in the new core until the day it's switched on; only a small early trial slice (interviews) goes live ahead of it.

This set is organised as one page per decision, read in the order the argument builds. It opens with three pages that frame the work — why rebuild at all (the premise), what's in scope to rebuild, and the change management the switch demands. Then Decisions 1–5 describe what the new system is — its data model, where the standard objects (candidate and the rest) live, the matching engine it relies on, its security model, and how users' access moves to it. Decisions 6–13 describe how the old system leaves — the rebuild approach, the deployment, the cutover, the freeze, the data move, the rollback and the rehearsals. Decisions 14–17 describe running it while and after it lands — delivering to the business through the rebuild, operating the live system, its integrations, and the archive. Each page states the alternatives we considered, which one we chose and why, what could go wrong with it, and — because it differs by choice — how we get back if it does. Read them in order, or jump to the one you want to challenge.

The decision map

The decisions, each on its own page

The strategy isn't one decision — it's a set of coupled ones, bracketed by pages that define the edges of the work. Each decision was worked the same way: lay out the real alternatives, pre-mortem every one (including the chosen option, whose failure modes are the risk we still carry), then pick the option whose worst case is the most survivable.

▸

Why rebuild — the premise

Why a native rebuild beats moving to another commercial recruitment product. The deciding reason is ownership: buying re-runs the exact vendor-dependency that forced this; building ends it.

Own, don't re-rent ▸

What's in scope — defines the work

Which components get rebuilt, and which are dropped. The rule: rebuild the minimum needed to disconnect the package — the product owners define that minimum; the rest is rebuilt later.

Minimum to disconnect ▸

Change management — defines the work

The people side of a one-morning switch — training on the dormant system, native documentation, a communication plan, and concentrated go-live support. A workstream the cutover depends on.

Familiar before live 1

Target data model

The shape of the new objects — relationships, identity, history, vocabularies — designed from measured use, and engineered for a model born with 1.4 million rows and measured hot spots.

Designed from use · lookups first 2

Keep the standard objects standard

The candidate — and every standard object the package extends — stays a standard record with a new record type, not a new custom object: keeping a decade of files, history and sharing, and one identity. The candidate is the most critical case.

Standard record · re-typed 3

The matching engine

The one capability the rebuild does not reconstruct from the package — CV parsing and candidate–vacancy matching, run as an external service behind a Marlex-owned interface, with everyday search built natively beside it. Whether that service is bought or built in-house is under active re-evaluation.

Buy vs. build — open 4

Security & sharing

Who sees what in the new model — current visibility carried at cutover, then a private, country-keyed model applied as a deliberate post-cutover stage, with real owners and a designed door for the anonymous job-board feed. One condition pulls the private model forward to day one: onboarding the internal HR team at cutover.

Carry now · privatise later — unless HR onboards at cutover 5

Access & app switch

Moving every user off the old app and objects and onto the new ones at the same access level — the object, field and app permissions audited by user group, reproduced as role permission sets, and flipped at cutover.

Reproduce access · switch as sets 6

Rebuild approach

For each thing being replaced: a fresh native component switched on later, or a branch in the existing logic? What turns it on — a setting or the cutover — and what must be flagged off in the old components at the switch.

New component + switch 7

Deployment

How the switched-off build reaches production — piece by piece as it's written, or held back for one big deployment at cutover? And why timing is keyed to how fast each component changes.

Progressive · dormant 8

Activation & cutover

How each capability actually crosses from old to new — slice it off early, big-bang the shared core, or keep both live and synced? And what re-points to the new system?

Big-bang core · slice edges 9

The freeze

The pause that makes the delta clean — stopping every write source (automation, batch jobs, integrations and recruiter edits) once the bulk is already pre-loaded, timed to open right after the batch that rewrites the whole candidate pool.

Freeze all writes · timed 10

Data migration

Ten years of candidate, application and placement data into the new model. The bulk is pre-loaded into the dormant objects weeks ahead, in production, and refreshed one-way; at cutover only the small delta moves. Covers what migrates, what's dropped, and what's a business call.

Pre-load ahead · delta at cutover 11

Rollback

How we get back if go-live fails — and why the way back is different for each way a capability crosses. The reversibility argument that decides the whole shape.

Intact fallback · gated 12

Testing behaviour parity

Most of what a rebuilt feature does lives in a package whose logic can't be read. Capture that behaviour first — read the metadata, observe the running feature in a sandbox on dummy data — and write it as acceptance criteria the native build must pass.

Characterise · then test 13

Rehearsal

Refresh a full copy from production, then practise the activation and the deactivation/rollback until both are timed and proven — plus the continuous proof that keeps the dormant build trustworthy until then.

Drill activation & rollback 14

Delivering while rebuilding

The business keeps shipping features through the rebuild, with no freeze. How new and changed work is handled so the two systems don't diverge — new work built native, and touched-after-rebuilt components re-synced into their twins.

Native-first · twin-tracked 15

Operating the new system

What watches the build once it's live — error logging the org keeps, alerting with owners, go-live dashboards, technical hypercare, and the written criteria the final package removal must meet.

Native observability · gated end-game 16

Integrations & middleware

How rebuilt edges meet the outside world — every edge behind a Marlex-owned interface with a direct connection first; the integration platform adopted per edge class, never blocking the cutover.

Ports now · platform later 17

The archive

Where records that don't board the new system live — half a million never-active candidates and seventy thousand finished placements and closed jobs — and the exact path back if one is needed.

Cold store · keyed recall

In brief

The whole argument on one screen

If you read nothing else, this is the shape of it.

  • The trigger. TargetRecruit's vendor was acquired by Bullhorn in mid-2025, and support for the product as Marlex uses it ends in November 2027. Rather than move to another commercial product, the programme chose to rebuild the parts Marlex actually uses as its own native Salesforce solution, in the same org.
  • The hard part isn't building — it's removing. The package is woven into hundreds of Marlex-built components and sits directly on the standard Contact (candidate) object. Salesforce refuses to uninstall a package while anything still references it, and uninstalling destroys all data held in package fields. Removal is a one-way door that must come last.
  • The safe shape is build-ahead-dormant. The whole new system is deployed into production months early but switched off, and the data is pre-loaded into the dormant objects ahead of time too. The cutover becomes "freeze, move the small delta, switch on," not "deploy and load under pressure," and it can be rehearsed against a full copy of the org.
  • The core must cross in one go; the edges don't. The candidate, job and application form a shared core nothing can move independently of — it big-bangs, and placements migrate with it. Interviews slice off first as a trial; integrations swap behind a stable interface; a gradual placement wind-down is kept only as a fallback.
  • Keeping two live copies in sync was analysed and ruled out. Against the real production write volumes it fails — and it has no safe way back if it goes wrong. Both systems do run side by side for months, but by single ownership, not by syncing duplicate copies.
  • Rollback is what decides it. The dormant big-bang keeps the old system intact as a real fallback, with a single known moment when ownership handed over — so we know exactly what to hand back. Keeping both live and synced has no known-good state to return to. Reversibility, not build effort, is the deciding factor.
  • The new system is designed, not copied. Its model keeps the old concepts one-for-one (so the migration stays provable) but rebuilds only the fields that hold real data, engineers the measured hot spots, and is built portable — no single-country assumption baked in. Its sharing carries forward unchanged at cutover; the private, country-keyed model is tightened afterwards as a deliberate post-cutover stage, not forced into the go-live window.
  • Go-live is watched, and the end is gated. The rebuild ships its own observability — an error log the org keeps, alerts with owners, dashboards the rollback criteria read — and the final package removal has written go-criteria, so the one true one-way door is crossed on evidence, on a date.
This is a draft for review

Every decision here has a page laying out the alternatives, the choice, and what could still go wrong — including the open questions each one still carries. Challenging any of it is the point of circulating it.

Circulated by Nuvolar to the Marlex Salesforce developers and architects for review — comments and disagreement welcome on any of it. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit, which mapped every Marlex-built component that references the package to the capability it serves, alongside the programme's functional inventory and external research on the closest documented package-replacement programmes. Figures are counted from the org, not estimated, and are approximate only where shown as a range.

The running order · TargetRecruit rebuild

The rebuild as a sequence

The decision set read as one running order — what has to happen, in which order, to remove TargetRecruit and stand up the native build in its place. A sequence, not a schedule: no durations, only what must come before what. This is the technical roadmap — what to build and in what technical order, by architecture dependency; it is not a delivery plan: no committed dates, no prioritized backlog, no project manager driving it day to day. The roadmap sets direction; a project plan would operationalise it — and that plan does not yet exist.

Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit · draft for peer review

Nov 2027TargetRecruit support ends — the date the build, data move and go-live must beat; the tail is not bound by it.
632Marlex-built components coupled to the package — the surface to rebuild or re-point.
1.06MCandidates that stay on the standard Contact — re-typed in place, never re-created.
1Rehearsed window where the shared core crosses, with a real fallback held underneath.

What this page is. Every other page in this set argues one decision. This one stitches them into the order they run. The early steps build and prove a system nobody uses yet; the middle steps rehearse and then execute the one switch; the last steps close the old system down. Each step names its own hard prerequisite.

The shape in one line. Lay the foundation and draw the scope, clean the current org, settle the native model, build every capability native and switched off, rehearse the flip both ways, then switch the shared core over in one window with the old package held intact underneath — and only much later, on evidence, remove it.

The premise it rests on

Why the order matters at all

The deciding reason to rebuild rather than buy is ownership: every packaged alternative re-runs the vendor dependency that forced this, while a native build ends it (the full argument is on Why rebuild). Choosing to rebuild in the same live org is what makes the order hard. The package sits directly on the standard Contact and is woven into hundreds of Marlex components; Salesforce refuses to uninstall a package while anything still references it, and uninstalling destroys the data in its fields. Removal is a one-way door that must come last — so the whole sequence is built backwards from it.

The running order

What has to happen, in which order

Ten steps. Step 4 runs alongside steps 2–3 rather than after them; every other step follows the one before it.

0
Comes first

Foundation & the requirements gate

FoundationRequirements gate

Nothing is built until the ground is laid and the scope is drawn. This step stands up the tooling and settles the one decision that sizes everything after it — what is rebuilt, what is bought, and what is dropped.

  • Stand up Marlex-owned repositories and a deploy pipeline, branch protection, and the governance review gate — a repeatable way to push to production is a hard precondition for progressive deployment.
  • Build the functional inventory and buy-versus-build classification — the requirements gate. Product owners set the minimum to disconnect (see What's in scope); matching and CV parsing are expected to be bought.
  • Open the component register that pairs every package-coupled component with its native replacement, its activation instructions, and the behaviour to test — the real unit the rebuild is tracked by.
  • Settle the design decisions the build needs: naming, ownership boundaries, the config-driven routing pattern, build standards. No group-wide standard is pre-locked. Part of this is a census of which automations need a routing switch — where an object keeps its identity but its delegated logic is rebuilt, one entry point must route between legacy and canonical logic by configuration (Apex triggers and handlers, Platform Event subscribers, queueables and batch jobs, outbound listeners that deployment alone can't toggle), as against the components a native activate/deactivate already covers (see Rebuild approach).
Blocks → the build cannot start on an unscoped, unfrozen target. The minimum-to-disconnect line is the single decision that most shapes the programme's size and risk.
1
Precedes the build

Clean the current org first

De-riskHygiene

Whatever is dead or broken today should not be carried into the native standard. Cleanup finishes before the native model opens, so nothing inherits known problems — and it runs where the org knowledge already lives (Marlex IT / Telefónica).

  • Deprecate the Lead object logic and remove retired metadata the inventory flags, once a dependency scan confirms nothing live still needs them.
  • Fix the security findings and structural technical debt surfaced by the inventory.
  • Convert the surviving Workflow Rules and Process Builders to Flow — only the automation that survives the rebuild, since logic rebuilt natively is not converted first.
  • Free the storage the data move needs — and free it before the pre-load, not just before the switch. The record store is three-quarters full, and pre-loading the new objects holds the old records and their new copies at once for the whole dormant window, not just cutover weekend — a peak that, left unmanaged, runs to about nine-tenths of the limit for weeks. Delete the ~195,000 candidates who declined data-protection consent (no business value, counted on the live system) and confirm the never-active tail is held back from the move, bringing the peak comfortably clear. Because the recruitment records have almost no parent-child enforcement, this is a manual object-by-object sweep, not one cascade — done here so the pre-load inherits headroom rather than a storage problem (see The archive).
Runs against → the November 2027 window. Deliberately foundational, not parallel to the build — folding it into the rebuild would let it get deprioritised to the end. The storage clean-up in particular gates the pre-load: holding two copies for weeks is only safe if the archival sweep has run first.
2
The foundation

The native model & the shared foundations

Native modelRead-only bridge

The native data model is settled first and frozen — it is the real schema everything else is built against, not a wrapper over a vendor.

  • Design the model from how the system is actually used, not by copying the old shape: same concepts one-for-one so the data move stays provable, but only the fields that hold data rebuilt — on the candidate, 51 of 141 package fields carry data; 74 are empty or defaults.
  • Keep the standard objects the package extended as standard objects — the candidate stays a Contact with a new record type, preserving a decade of files, activities, sharing and history for 1.06M records (see Keep the standard objects standard).
  • Design for the measured volumes: the busiest job carries 13,151 applications, so relationships are ordinary lookups rather than the rigid form that locks a busy parent; counters compute off the save path; every record carries a key so a failed load re-runs without duplicates.
  • Stand up the shared read-only bridge to old data, the test-data generator and the switch framework — the foundation all new work is built against.
Hard prerequisite → the foundation must exist before the bulk of delivery, or new work reaches for the old package by default and re-creates the coupling.
3
The main build

Build native, switched off, one capability at a time

Native buildDeployed dormantKeeping copies in step

Each capability is built native against the frozen model, deployed to production switched off, tested, and left to wait. The default is a fresh component beside the old one; editing the live component in place is reserved for the few designed integration points.

The build set is two things, not one. It's the components Marlex built on the package and the packaged features the owners chose to keep — those native-to-the-package capabilities have no Marlex code to carry over, so each one kept is built from scratch against the new model, with its behaviour gathered fresh rather than read off existing components. Which packaged features make that list is the owners' keep/drop call on the Scope page.

  • Build capability by capability across CRM, back office and recruitment — each a self-contained unit, built to standard as it goes.
  • Build every component configuration-first and portable: business variation is a configuration record, never hardcoded — so a proven component can later become a Marlex-standard, group-shared building block reused in the Axxon org and the country rollouts by parameterising it, not rebuilding it.
  • Deploy each piece switched off as it's finished, timed to how fast it changes: rarely-touched components early (proving deployment cheaply), actively-changing ones last (so a switched-off copy waits as short a time as possible).
  • Buy and integrate the matching & CV-parsing engine — decided early because it shapes the candidate model (see The matching engine).
  • Build Marlex-owned monitoring into the rebuild, because today's visibility belongs to the package and leaves with it.
Standing risk → a fix made to a live component but not carried into its switched-off copy silently regresses at go-live. Controlled by the paired-component register, the same-change rule, and a tightening freeze — never zero.
4
Alongside 2–3

Keep delivering — without the two systems drifting apart

New work built nativeChecked continuously

There is no feature freeze. The rule that keeps the rebuild converging rather than chasing a moving target (see Delivering while rebuilding):

New work

Built native from day one

Written on the new model, reading old data only through the shared bridge — never extending the package. A feature built native needs no copy: it is the rebuild advancing, so the work still to disconnect shrinks.

Changed work

Carried into its copy, same change

A change to an already-rebuilt live component is carried into its switched-off copy in the same change — or logged as dated, owned work the tightening freeze forces to zero.

The full test suite runs nightly against the switched-off build, so anything out of step surfaces within a day — not on go-live morning.

5
Before the switch

Rehearse the switch — both directions, twice

Full-copy rehearsalBehavioural probe

Everything is deployed and dormant, so nothing about deploying is left to rehearse. Two things get proven instead: that switching on lands cleanly, and that switching back works cleanly — both timed.

  • Refresh a full-copy sandbox from production (it comes up holding the switched-off build, real data and configuration), then practise the pre-load, the delta and the activation end to end — the measured pre-load time and the far smaller delta-window time are the headline outputs.
  • Practise the switch-off and rollback — the timed flip back to the old system — turning the way back from a claim into an exercised procedure. Run at least twice, each from a clean starting point.
  • Run a cheap experiment early to learn how the unreadable package behaves — it gates the first slice, the candidate re-typing and the rollback replay (see Rehearsal).
  • Prove sharing-dependent features as each affected persona, not as one privileged account — anything whose outcome turns on sharing, org-wide defaults, role, profile or permission set can pass a single-user test and still be wrong for the role that needs it (see Testing behaviour parity). Features whose behaviour doesn't vary by user are proven once, as one user.
Gates the switch → the cutover cannot honestly be committed until a full-copy run produces a measured load time, a proven flip-back, and a passing data-match. Full-copy sandbox availability is a dependency to confirm with Marlex IT.
6
The switch

The switch — pre-loaded ahead, then freeze, delta, flip

Go / no-go gateData migrationPoint of no return

The heavy part happens before this window, not inside it. Weeks ahead, in production, the full data load runs into the dormant new objects and is refreshed one-way as the old data changes — a one-way copy nobody operates on, not the two-way sync ruled out. That lets the load be timed on real volume and let key users validate against real data well before the day. Then the one rehearsed window: interviews and the edges have already sliced off; the shared core crosses here with the old package frozen but intact underneath. Freeze all write sources, move only the delta that changed since the last refresh, then validate at a go/no-go gate — pass and flip, fail and roll back to the frozen org. The runbook below marks how reversibility narrows. Full detail on Activation & cutover, The freeze and Data migration.

Users get their new persona permission sets here, but keep their TargetRecruit license and old-object permissions — the fallback needs them able to work in the old system if a rollback comes. Reclaiming those is a later teardown, gated by the fallback expiry, not part of the switch.

01

Pre-load & refresh

Weeks before, in prod. Full extract → transform → load into the dormant new objects, refreshed one-way as the old data changes. Millions of rows moved, timed and validated ahead of the window — nobody operates on the copy, so nothing to reconcile.

02

Freeze

Stop all four write sources — automation, jobs, inbound feeds, recruiter edits — timed to open right after the batch that rewrites the whole candidate pool, so the delta is smallest.

03

Delta

Move only the records changed since the last refresh — a small, predictable set — with the same pipeline. Package-field data verified-extracted before any later uninstall.

04

Validate → GATE

Row counts match, no orphaned records, sampled field-by-field comparison, storage peak within limit — old and new records coexist, so headroom is checked, not assumed. Pass → flip. Fail → roll back to the frozen, intact org.

05

Activate

Flip record-page, app and record-type assignments and the switches; re-point every external caller. On the objects that keep their identity (the candidate stays a Contact), the screen swap rides record-page assignment — because a user holds only one profile, the switch can't be a profile edit (see Keep the standard objects standard). Still reversible — the old package sits intact underneath, so rollback here means switching back and replaying only what was created since the flip.

06

Uninstall

Separate, later gate — never go-live morning. The one true point of no return, crossed on written go-criteria once the new system has earned it.

Free to reverse — pre-load is dormant; old system frozen but intact Go / no-go gate — the last free decision Reversible at a bounded cost — old package still there, replay the delta The one true point of no return — uninstall
The whole shape → everything up to the gate rolls back at essentially no cost; after it, rollback is bounded to replaying records created since the switch. Reversibility, not build effort, is why the core big-bangs rather than syncing two live copies.
7
After the switch

Hypercare, with the fallback held and dated

HypercareDated fallback

Watch the first weeks on instruments proven in rehearsal (see Operating the new system), keep the old package locked read-only and confirm with a scheduled check that nothing writes to it, and hold written rollback-invocation criteria with a named owner and a dated fallback expiry — so the way back stays real and "later" for the uninstall never becomes "never" (see Rollback).

One part of the way back is not Marlex's to flip: a handful of third-party tools connect through their own connector, with the mapping to Salesforce objects held on their side. For those, rolling back means the tool's owner re-points its connector at the old objects — so before the fallback window opens, each such owner has agreed who re-points, within what window, on what signal, and has the substitution reference telling them which old object to point back at (see Integrations).

8
Deliberately last

Remove the package — the one true point of no return

Gated uninstall

Held separate from the switch and executed only against written go-criteria, so the final removal is a scheduled, evidenced change — boring by design, because everything dangerous was retired beforehand.

  • Nothing references it (the platform dependency check is the backstop under the rebuilt-component list), nothing still writes to it (the scheduled check shows zero writes for the agreed period), and nothing is lost (package-field data verified extracted and checksummed).
  • The new system has earned it (hypercare closed, error rates at norms) and the fallback has expired — removing the package and retiring the fallback are the same decision, taken once, in writing.
  • The user teardown runs on the same gate: reclaim the TargetRecruit user licenses and strip users' permissions on the old AVTRRT__ objects. These are kept in place at cutover so the fallback stays usable, and removed only once the fallback is retired (see Operating the new system).
9
Last, each its own stage

Finish the deferred work — each its own stage

EvolutivePrivatisationArchive

What was held back is now built on the native platform at a normal pace — each as its own stage with its own rehearsal and rollback, never crammed into go-live.

  • Build the dropped and evolutive features the owners deferred, on the now-native platform.
  • Run the privatisation stage: private defaults, a country key, one sharing rule per object — the deliberate post-cutover tightening, on quiet timing with its own rollback (see Security & sharing).
  • Execute the archive and retention sweep against the cold store — the 461K never-active candidates and terminal placements that never boarded the live system, recallable by key (see The archive).
  • Classify every finished component group-shared / Marlex-standard / country-specific — the standard defined on proven components, the catalogue other brands reuse.

The routing rule — inside steps 5 & 6

How each capability crosses

One question routes everything: can it be isolated from the shared core and the candidate? If yes, it slices off early. If no, it rides the core's single big-bang. Two live copies are never kept in sync — that was tested against real volumes and ruled out (see Activation & cutover).

Capability Records Route Why this route
Interviews 124K Slice — first A self-contained leaf, already mostly Marlex code. Moves early as the first proof the approach works, where a mistake is cheap.
Candidate · job · application 1.06M / 57K / 1.4M Big-bang core The shared backbone. Can't be sliced (one candidate record) and can't be kept in two synced copies (volume + five child records locked to a single parent). Crosses in one window.
Placements & back office ~9.4K live Big-bang Migrate with the core by default — only ~9,400 are live. Gradual run-off held as a fallback only if the rehearsal shows they can't move reliably.
Job-board feed (InfoJobs) ~27K/wk Rides the core Outbound publishing behind a switchable service can move early; inbound entry re-points with the core. The one external system that writes the old model.
ERP link (NET4) — Preserved Already behind a Marlex-owned, vendor-free service — not rebuilt; only its reads of the old model re-point.
Matching & CV parsing 1.06M profiles Bought · new edge The one capability bought, not built — behind a Marlex-owned interface, with everyday search built natively beside it. Parity is a written go/no-go criterion.

What the order is engineered against

The risks that drive the sequence

Each step is shaped by a specific failure it refuses to carry into the cutover window — the whole design works to keep that window reversible right up to the deliberately-later uninstall.

Two live copies with no way back

A two-way sync of the core leaves no trustworthy copy once the two drift, and fails outright on the ~1M-record candidate batch and the five child records locked to a single parent.

Bounded by → switching the core over in one go against an intact fallback, with a single dated handover. Moving edges by single ownership captures the gradual-transition benefit without copies to reconcile.

Copies falling out of step over months

With 600+ components switched off while the live system changes, an un-mirrored fix silently regresses at go-live — a certainty at real delivery pace.

Bounded by → building new work native, keeping each copy's wait short, the paired-component register under a same-change rule, and the nightly test run.

The package is a black box

Its reactions can only be learned by testing, never inspection — including whether replaying records into it on rollback fires hidden logic.

Bounded by → a cheap sandbox experiment run early, gating the first slice, the candidate re-typing and the rollback replay. Provisional caveats collapse the moment it clears.

The matching engine can't simply be rebuilt

Semantic matching over 1.06M profiles is the hardest capability and the wrong job for native search.

Bounded by → buying it as a service behind a Marlex-owned interface, tested on real Spanish CVs before the design freeze, with parity a written go/no-go criterion.

The deadline, and single-partner concentration

November 2027 is the planning date and most delivery sits with one partner. If capacity slips, the critical path up to go-live slips with it.

Bounded by → working the build, data move and go-live backwards from it with hypercare margin, cleaning first so nothing is rebuilt twice, and keeping a second-partner option under review.

Still to close

The decisions the sequence is waiting on

The order holds regardless of these, but each sizes or gates a step. They are owned by the business and product owners, due before the step they block.

What Nov 2027 actually obliges — stop using, or fully remove?

End-of-support means the operation must be off TargetRecruit by then. It is unconfirmed whether the licence can still be held past that date as the dated fallback, or whether the package must be uninstalled by then. If removal is forced, steps 8–9 pull back inside the deadline and the fallback design changes.

Marlex IT + vendor · confirm now

Where "minimum to disconnect" lands

The keep / drop / defer call over the candidate list — the single decision that most shapes size, risk and duration.

Product owners · before build

One go-live, or staged by domain

Whether the core crosses in one switch or activates domain by domain — informed by the independent-cuttability analysis.

Programme governance

Migrate all candidates, or archive the never-active tail

The biggest data-quality call — sets migration volume, index size and retention surface. Archiving the 461K never-active candidates is recommended.

Business + legal · before rehearsal

Which matching engine — and does it force its own objects

A service reading Marlex's objects is clean; a package that forces a two-way sync may tip the call toward a bounded build. Gates the candidate child-entity shape.

Marlex + Nuvolar · decide early

Migrate live placements, or run them off

Whether the ~9,400 live placements move in the window or wind down in the old system. The rehearsal decides.

Rehearsal outcome

This is the sequence view of the decision set — start from the overview for the decision map, or open any step's own page above. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit; counts are point-in-time and will drift.

Before the decisions · Why rebuild at all

Why rebuild, rather than move to another product

Every other page answers "how do we rebuild." This one answers the question that comes before all of them — why a native rebuild beats buying the next packaged recruitment system.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

Why this page exists. The rest of the set takes "we are rebuilding" as its starting point. A reader challenging the strategy is entitled to challenge that premise first: was moving to another commercial recruitment product seriously weighed, and why did building natively win? This page is that argument. It is the front door to everything after it.

What forced the question. The recruitment package Marlex runs on was acquired by another recruitment-software vendor in mid-2025, and the end-of-support date Marlex has been given for the product as it uses it is November 2027. Standing still is not an option — the platform Marlex's entire recruitment operation depends on has a switch-off date. Something must replace it before then.

What we chose, and why. Rebuild the parts Marlex actually uses as its own native Salesforce solution, in the same org — rather than re-implement onto another vendor's package. The deciding reason is not cost or speed: it is ownership. Every packaged option re-runs the exact risk that created this situation — a vendor's roadmap, acquisition or sunset deciding Marlex's timeline. A native build is the only option that ends that dependency instead of resetting its clock.

The forcing function

This is a replacement wave, not a Marlex peculiarity

It's worth naming that this situation is not unique to Marlex, because that context shapes the options. A vertical software vendor — one whose product is specialised for a single industry, as Marlex's is for staffing — being acquired or sunset, with a firm end-of-support date, forces every customer on it to replace the product inside a fixed window. This exact pattern has played out across several industries in the last few years, and the published playbooks agree on two things: the replacement is a re-implementation, not an upgrade (there is no gentle migration path), and experienced talent gets scarcer as the deadline nears, because every other customer is reacting to the same clock. Both facts push toward starting early and toward an option Marlex controls.

The clock, stated plainly

The end-of-support date Marlex has been given is November 2027 — a date to plan against, and one that could still move. Whatever replaces the package must be live, proven and carrying the real recruitment operation before then — with margin, not on the deadline. Every option below is judged against that date as much as against its own merits.

Alternatives considered

Four ways out of the deadline

Move to the acquiring vendor's productRejected

The path of least resistance: follow the acquisition onto the buyer's own recruitment platform. It re-implements the whole operation on a new vendor's data model and terms — a full re-implementation either way, so little is saved on effort — and it lands Marlex right back where it started: dependent on a single vendor's roadmap and commercial decisions, on a platform Marlex doesn't control, having just lived through what that dependency costs. It trades a known sunset for the same exposure under a new name.

Move to a different commercial recruitment packageRejected

Shop the market and re-implement onto another specialised product. Same re-implementation effort, same vendor-dependency risk reset to zero, plus a new integration surface and a fresh round of licensing — and it discards the substantial Marlex-built logic that already sits natively in the org around the package, which would have to be rebuilt against the new product's model regardless. Buying replaces one black box with another.

Rebuild the used parts as a native Salesforce solution, in the same orgChosen

Replace only the parts Marlex actually uses, built natively on the Salesforce platform Marlex already runs and pays for. The data model stops being a rented abstraction over a vendor and becomes Marlex's own. It ends the vendor-dependency cycle rather than resetting it; it keeps and builds on the native logic already in the org; and it removes a licence cost permanently. The cost is real: Marlex takes on building and maintaining what it used to buy, and the build must finish before the deadline. But it is the only option whose payoff is structural — the next vendor decision can't dictate Marlex's roadmap because there is no next vendor.

Do nothing until forcedRejected

Wait, and decide closer to the deadline. This isn't a strategy, it's the absence of one — and it fails on the talent-scarcity fact above: the closer to November 2027, the harder and dearer any replacement becomes, with no margin left for the rehearsals and fallback the rest of this set depends on. The deadline is fixed; the only variable is how much runway is spent before acting.

The deciding axis

Ownership, not cost or speed

It would be easy to argue the rebuild on cost — modern tooling lowers the build effort, and a licence disappears. But that is not the load-bearing reason, and pretending it is would make the decision hostage to an estimate. The load-bearing reason is that the two buy options re-create the precise failure that started this: a third party's commercial choices setting Marlex's timeline. A staffing company's recruitment platform is close to its core operation; renting the model that runs it means renting the ability to plan. Every packaged option, however good today, carries the same latent clause — the vendor can be acquired, can sunset the product, can change terms — and Marlex has just watched that clause trigger. The rebuild is the only option that removes the clause. That is worth taking on a build cost for, and it's a judgement about strategic control, not a spreadsheet.

One dependency is deliberately kept, and it's worth naming so the argument is honest: the rebuild still sits on Salesforce itself. But that is a different risk class, not the same one renamed — a hyperscale horizontal platform Marlex has already bet its whole CRM on, with an ecosystem measured in millions of customers, versus a niche vertical package whose fate one acquisition just decided. The replacement wave this page describes is a vertical-vendor pattern. Ending the vertical-package dependency while keeping the platform one is the trade being made, with eyes open.

What this page does not claim

The rebuild is not argued here as cheaper or faster than buying — that comparison depends on estimates still being worked, and it is not the deciding factor. It is argued as the option that ends the vendor-dependency risk rather than resetting it. Where a genuinely specialised capability is better bought than built — the candidate-matching and CV-parsing engine is the known example — it is bought as an external service behind a Marlex-owned interface, not re-adopted as a platform; that call is worked in full on its own page. Rebuilding the platform and buying a hard component are not in tension.

How the options compare

Rebuild versus re-buy

Do-nothing is the absence of a decision, and the two buy options share a risk profile — so the real comparison is rebuild-native against move-to-a-product (either the acquirer's or another vendor's).

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Rebuild native, in the same org Chosen

Replace the used parts with a Marlex-owned native build on the platform it already runs.

  • The vertical-vendor dependency ends — no recruitment-product acquisition or sunset can dictate Marlex's timeline again. almost certain
  • Existing native logic and the platform investment are kept and built on, and a licence cost is removed permanently. probably
  • The build runs longer or harder than hoped and eats into the margin before the deadline. possibly

Upside, and how big Structural: Marlex owns the platform that runs its core operation, permanently — the payoff compounds every year and every future vendor event that now can't touch it.

Worst case A schedule squeeze against November 2027 — which the whole rest of this strategy (dormant build, rehearsals, fallback) is engineered to bound.

Move to another commercial product Rejected

Re-implement onto the acquirer's platform or another vendor's package.

  • A supported product with a vendor's roadmap and support behind it, sooner than a build. more likely than not
  • The same vendor-dependency risk is reset to zero — the next acquisition or sunset is Marlex's problem again. almost certain
  • The effort is a full re-implementation regardless, and the native logic around the old package is rebuilt against a new model anyway. probably

Upside, and how big Speed and a vendor's shoulders to lean on — real, but bought by re-accepting the exact exposure that forced this decision.

Worst case Marlex re-implements at full cost onto a new platform it doesn't control, and finds itself here again the next time that vendor's strategy changes.

Buying is faster; owning is final. Both routes cost a comparable re-implementation effort — the buy options' speed edge comes from elapsed time, because the product already exists while the build must be written — and that edge is bought by resetting the very risk that started the programme. The rebuild's cost is a build Marlex takes on once; the buy options' cost is a dependency Marlex takes on again, indefinitely. For a platform this close to the company's core operation, ending the dependency is worth more than the time saved by renewing it. That judgement — control over convenience — is the premise every "how" decision in this set rests on.

What follows from this

Why the rest of the set is shaped the way it is

Choosing to rebuild rather than buy is what makes the rest of this strategy necessary and hard. Because the replacement is built in the same live org the business runs on, it can't be stood up beside the old one and switched over at leisure — the two share a platform, and the old package can't be removed while anything still references it. That single fact drives the shape of everything after: the minimum to disconnect, the build-ahead-dormant deployment, the one-morning cutover, the intact fallback, and the rehearsals that prove it. A buy-a-product decision would have produced a different — and in its own way harder — strategy. This one is the strategy for a rebuild.

This is the premise. Continue to What's in scope for where the rebuild's boundary is drawn, or return to the overview. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and external research on the closest documented package-replacement programmes.

Defining the work · What's in scope

What gets rebuilt — and what gets dropped

The rebuild list isn't "everything TargetRecruit does." It's the minimum needed to disconnect the package — a line the product owners will draw. What isn't on that list is dropped at cutover and rebuilt later.

Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

The decision. Before anything is built, the scope has to be drawn: which of the hundreds of package-coupled components and packaged features actually get rebuilt for cutover. Rebuild too little and the package can't be removed; rebuild everything and the programme carries years of accumulated features — many low-value — into a single high-risk cutover.

The proposed rule. The rebuild scope should be the minimum required to disconnect TargetRecruit, and the product owners are the ones who define that minimum. Four things force a component onto the list (below). Anything the owners judge not part of the minimum is deliberately dropped at cutover and reconstructed afterwards as evolutive work — a smaller, safer cutover in exchange for a known backlog. Where "minimum" lands is still an open decision for the owners, not a settled line.

What forces a rebuild

Four things put a component on the list

A component is in scope for rebuild if any of these is true. The first two are about the Marlex-built layer; the third is packaged features with no Marlex code; the fourth is the connections to the outside world.

Built on it

Anything Marlex built directly on the package's data model — a field, rule, button, flow, Apex class or screen that lives on or reads a package object. When the package leaves, these break or lose their data, so each must be rebuilt on the native model or re-pointed. This is the bulk of the coupling surface — over six hundred components in the audit.

Depends on it

Anything that references something being rebuilt. This is the part that's easy to under-count: if component A is replaced by a new native component, everything that calls or reads A has to move to the new one too — and so does whatever references those. Scope follows the dependency chain, not just the first ring of package-touching components.

Native to the package

Features that are the package's own, with no Marlex code behind them. These have nothing to migrate and no seam to strangle — they simply vanish when the package is removed. Each one Marlex still needs must be built from scratch natively (for example, the package's own opportunity-to-vacancy hand-off, which is a packaged screen with zero Marlex code).

Talks to the outside

The inbound and outbound integrations — the connections to the ERP, the job board, the training platform, the credit-check and e-signature services, and the customer data platform. Each either re-points to the new model or is rebuilt behind a stable interface, in step with cutover. These are covered in detail below.

What this does not mean — Marlex's own objects are kept, not rebuilt

The first rule is about the connection to the package, not about every object that has one. Marlex built a number of its own custom objects over the years — its own case-and-task record, its training-enrolment records, its invoicing records — and these are Marlex's, not the recruitment package's. They don't disappear when the package leaves. So they are kept and re-connected, not recreated: the object and its data stay exactly where they are, and only the link from that object to a package object — a lookup, or a piece of automation that reads the package — is re-pointed to the new native object. Recreating one of these as a brand-new object would duplicate something Marlex already owns and leave two records for one real thing. The same principle already governs the ERP connection below (preserved, not rebuilt); it applies to Marlex's own data objects too. What gets rebuilt is the seam to the package — never the Marlex object carrying it.

The transitive trap

Replacing one component pulls in the ones that use it

The second rule is the one that quietly grows scope. The set to rebuild is the dependency closure of the package-coupled components — the whole chain, not just the first ring that touches the package — which is exactly why a count taken from that first ring understates the work, and why the foundation pieces have to be built before what depends on them.

Why this matters for estimating

Counting only the components that directly reference the package understates the work. The real figure is that set plus everything downstream of it that a rebuild disturbs. This is also why the order of rebuilding matters — the shared foundation pieces (the test-data factory, the switch framework, the reference service pattern) come first, because so much else depends on them.

The integrations

Every connection to the outside world is in scope

The rebuild isn't only internal. Marlex's recruitment platform is wired to several external systems, and each has to make the crossing to the new model along with the rebuild — some re-pointed behind a stable interface, some rebuilt, some (the direct-API ones) re-pointed on the other party's side.

External systems in scope, and how each crosses
System What it does How it crosses
NET4 (ERP) Sends placements/offers into the ERP Already behind a Marlex-owned, vendor-free service — preserved, not rebuilt; its reads of the old model re-point at cutover
InfoJobs (job board) Publishes vacancies out; ingests candidates in Outbound publishing goes through a switchable service (can move early); inbound candidate feed re-points with the core at cutover
Humact / Lectio (training) Enrolments tied to placements Restructured — the enrolment link is one of a handful of tight relationships (detailed on the data-model page) that must be re-pointed before placements go native
Iberinform (credit check) Client solvency lookups Re-pointed direct at cutover; low volume, low coupling
Signaturit (e-signature) Contract signature requests and signed-event callbacks Re-pointed direct at cutover; single consumer, low coupling
Data Cloud / customer data platform Ingests Salesforce data downstream Calls the standard API against the old object names — must be re-pointed on its own side in step with cutover (no seam inside the org)

The last row is the important one, and it generalises: any external system that reads Salesforce's standard API against the old object and field names — data loaders, middleware, reporting and analytics tools, the customer data platform, partner integrations — treats the object name as the contract. There's no interface on Marlex's side to swap; the other party has to re-point, in step with cutover, and these callers have to be discovered from usage logs first. The mechanics of that are on the Activation & cutover page; the point here is simply that they are in scope and easy to miss.

For the developersIntegration edges present in the org: MA_Net4 (Named Credential + N4_Integration_Site), Humact_API/Humact_Lectio, MA_Iberinform, MA_InfoJobs, MA_Signaturit. The NET4 client (MA_Net4Client + DTO + mock + ~8 classes) is already a decoupled anti-corruption-layer family — preserve scope, not rebuild scope. Only NET4 carries outbound callouts among the peripheral groups; invoicing, compliance, commercial and account-management have none. Watch MA_SendOfferToN4's hardcoded placeholder endpoint fallback — flag for cleanup.

The governing decision

The owners define "minimum" — and set what gets dropped

The four rules above produce a candidate list — everything that could be rebuilt. Turning that into the actual rebuild scope is a business decision, and it belongs to the product owners: what is the minimum set of capabilities that has to work natively for TargetRecruit to be switched off?

Framing it as a minimum, rather than a faithful lift-and-shift, is deliberate — but where the line falls is still open for the owners to set:

  • Not everything TargetRecruit does is still used. The audit already found capabilities built on package objects that hold zero data in production (candidate references, the deal-sheet), and reports whose value the business hasn't confirmed. These are candidates to drop, not rebuild.
  • A smaller cutover is a safer cutover. Every capability in the rebuild scope is something to build, test, rehearse and activate in the window. Dropping the low-value ones shrinks the freeze, the uninstall-blocker list, and the go-live risk surface — the descope is a risk-reduction lever, not just a saving.
  • Dropped doesn't mean gone forever. What the owners leave off the minimum list is reconstructed after cutover as evolutive work, on the native platform, at a normal pace — not crammed into the one high-stakes weekend.
What we need from the product owners

A keep / drop / defer decision over the candidate list — especially the reports, the peripheral and "nice-to-have" capabilities, and the never-used features. Everything kept is rebuilt for cutover; everything dropped becomes a post-cutover evolutive item on a backlog. This is the single decision that most shapes the size, risk and duration of the whole programme, and it is theirs to make.

The candidate list itself — every capability, its layer, and its provisional keep/drop/defer disposition — is maintained as the living Functional Inventory workbook: open the Functional Inventory folder in SharePoint (Marlex/Nuvolar access required).

The moving boundary

Delivery continues — so scope is governed at intake, not just at the start

The candidate list is not photographed once and left alone. Marlex keeps delivering to the business throughout the rebuild — new features arrive on the normal cadence, and some of them touch the very objects being disconnected (contract handling, for instance, reaches into the ERP link and the placement and candidate objects). Left ungoverned, that would quietly grow the disconnection scope every sprint: each new feature built on the package is one more thing to rebuild before the package can leave, and the rebuild would be chasing a target moving away from it.

The rule that keeps the boundary shrinking is decided on the Rebuild approach page and matters enough to restate here: new work is built on the new native model from day one, reading any old data it needs through the controlled seam — never extending the package. New delivery then advances the rebuild instead of enlarging it. Scope is therefore governed at two moments, not one: the owners' keep/drop/defer cut sets the starting minimum, and the native-first intake rule holds the line on everything added after.

The one in-flight case to settle first

A feature is being built right now that reaches the ERP link and the placement and candidate objects. It is the first live test of the intake rule: built native-first behind the existing interfaces, it becomes part of the rebuild; built onto the package, it adds disconnection scope at the cheapest moment it will ever be to redirect. Which way it goes is a decision for the programme's technical governance, and the earlier it is taken the less rework it costs.

How the options compare

Minimum-to-disconnect versus a faithful lift-and-shift

The choice of where to draw the scope line has two honest candidates: rebuild the minimum that lets the package be switched off, or faithfully reproduce everything the package does today. Set them side by side by their reasonable outcomes — a plain longer/shorter feature-list comparison hides that the two options carry very differently-sized risks into the one high-stakes weekend.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Minimum to disconnect Chosen

Rebuild only what's needed to switch the package off; defer the rest.

  • A smaller cutover — shorter freeze, shorter uninstall-blocker list, smaller go-live risk surface. almost certain
  • A known backlog of deferred capabilities, rebuilt later at a normal pace. almost certain
  • Something dropped turns out to have been quietly load-bearing. unlikely

Upside, and how big Directly shrinks the riskiest weekend of the programme — the descope is a risk-reduction lever, not just a saving.

Worst case A deferred item is deferred, not destroyed: re-added from the backlog, its data archived not lost.

Faithful lift-and-shift Rejected

Reproduce everything the package does today, one-for-one.

  • Every low-value and unused feature is carried into the single cutover to build, test, rehearse and activate. almost certain
  • A larger freeze, a longer uninstall-blocker list, a wider go-live risk surface. almost certain
  • Effort spent rebuilding fields and features that hold no data — on the candidate alone, 74 of 141 package fields are empty or defaults (measured). almost certain

Upside, and how big Nothing is missing on day one — a real gain, but one that mostly protects features the business may not use.

Worst case The whole accumulated feature set rides one weekend; sizing from the field count overstates the build by about twice.

The asymmetry favours the minimum. Its one real downside — dropping something load-bearing — is unlikely and, crucially, recoverable (deferred, not destroyed), and it is guarded by making the keep/drop/defer call an explicit signed-off decision rather than an omission by default. Lift-and-shift's downsides are near-certain and land where it hurts most: inside the cutover. The preference this serves is the smallest, safest switch — and where "minimum" falls is still the owners' call to set.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Scope isn't a live cutover step, so it doesn't "roll back" in the runbook sense — but it carries its own reversibility question: what if something dropped turns out to have been needed? The mitigation is that a dropped capability is deferred, not destroyed — its data is either migrated or deliberately archived (a business call, see Data migration), and the capability itself goes on the post-cutover backlog to be rebuilt natively. The genuine risk is dropping something quietly load-bearing — guarded, as the comparison above sets out, by making the keep/drop/defer call an explicit signed-off decision.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The minimum line isn't drawn yet. Until the owners make the keep/drop/defer call, the rebuild scope — and therefore the programme's size and duration — is an estimate, not a commitment.
  • Transitive scope can surprise. The dependency closure of the package-coupled set is larger than the set itself; the definitive list comes from Salesforce's own dependency checker at the removal gate, and the audit map is an approximation until then.
  • A packaged, unnamed component could hide. One package in the family carries no namespace prefix, so some components that look Marlex-built may actually be vendor code — a blind spot to close with the vendor before scope is finalised.
  • The direct-API callers must be discovered from usage logs, not metadata, well before the window — an item that spans teams outside Marlex.
  • The intake rule has to be enforced, not just stated. With the business still delivering every sprint, native-first is only real if new work is classified as it enters the backlog — does it touch the coupled surface, and if so is it being built native? An unenforced rule lets the disconnection scope creep back up one ticket at a time.

Continue to Rebuild approach for how each in-scope component is rebuilt, or Change management for the people side of the switch. Return to the overview.

Defining the work · Change management

The people side of the switch

The big-bang means every recruiter changes system on the same morning. Training, documentation, communication and floor support are what keep that from being a shock — a named workstream, not an afterthought.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

Why this is a decision at all. The technical strategy deliberately concentrates the core switch into one rehearsed weekend. That's the right call for reversibility — but it has a direct human consequence: on go-live morning, every recruiter opens a new interface for the first time and works the whole day in it. A flawless data migration still fails the business if the people using it aren't ready. So change management isn't a courtesy line at the end of the plan — it's a workstream the cutover depends on.

What we propose. Treat readiness as a first-class deliverable with four levers — training, documentation, communication, and go-live support — front-loaded so that the new system is familiar before it's live. The dormant build makes this possible: the real new system exists for months before cutover, so people can be trained on the actual thing — in a dedicated training sandbox — not a mock-up. The detail of who runs each lever and to what depth is for Marlex to shape.

The risk this addresses

One morning, everyone switches

The pre-mortem of the big-bang named "change shock" explicitly: because the core crosses in a single window, there is no gradual break-in period for the people who use it. The alternatives that would spread the UI change over time — running both systems live, or moving capability by capability — were ruled out for stronger reasons (reversibility and data-volume; see Activation & cutover and Rollback). So the change-shock risk is one the technical strategy accepts on purpose, and then buys down through preparation rather than by changing the cutover shape.

The one real mitigation the shape itself offers: the edges that slice off early — interviews first — give a subset of users an earlier, smaller taste of the new system, so the big-bang morning isn't the entire organisation's very first contact with it.

The four levers

What readiness is made of

Training

Recruiters trained on the actual dormant system ahead of cutover — not slides, not a mock-up that behaves differently, but the real build they'll use on the day, stood up in a dedicated training sandbox (a lightweight sandbox with representative sample data — deliberately not a full copy of production). Role-based: what a recruiter, a back-office user and a manager each need differs. The dormant-build approach is what makes this possible, because the system genuinely exists before it goes live.

Documentation

Native equivalents of the day-to-day guides — how to create a vacancy, log an interview outcome, convert to a placement — written against the new screens. The old package's documentation stops being true at cutover, so this is a rebuild in its own right, not a light edit.

Communication

A plan that tells everyone what changes, when, and what to do — the freeze dates (when the old system goes read-only), the go-live date, who to call, and what's intentionally different or deferred. Managed expectations are half of a smooth switch; the descope decisions (see What's in scope) especially need clear messaging so a deferred feature reads as "coming later," not "lost."

Go-live support

Concentrated floor support in the first days — people on hand to answer "where did this go?" in real time, and a fast route for issues. The first week after a big-bang is when confidence is won or lost; staffing it heavily is cheap insurance on a large investment.

The sequencing

Familiar before live

The guiding principle is that the new system should be familiar before it is live. Because the build is deployed dormant into production months ahead (see Deployment), the training and documentation can be built and delivered against the real system in the run-up — not deferred to a scramble in the final week. The communication plan runs alongside, peaking around the freeze and go-live dates. Floor support is the one lever that lands at cutover and just after.

How the options compare

One-morning switch plus levers versus spreading the change out

There's a real decision buried here: accept the one-morning change shock and buy it down with preparation, or reshape the cutover to spread the interface change over time. The second sounds kinder to users — but its outcomes show why it isn't available, and why the levers are the only lane left.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Accept the shock, buy it down with levers Chosen

One-morning switch; training on the real dormant system, native docs, a comms plan, concentrated floor support.

  • Adoption friction is reduced to manageable — people meet a system they were trained on, not a surprise. more likely than not
  • Real first-week confusion remains, absorbed by concentrated floor support. probably
  • The early interview slice softens the morning for one group of users first. more likely than not

Upside, and how big The shock is bought down without touching the cutover shape — the reversibility and data-volume gains of the big-bang are all kept.

Worst case A hard first week for some roles — costly in support hours, not in the integrity of the switch.

Spread the interface change over time Rejected

Run both systems live, or move capability by capability, to give users a gradual break-in.

  • Delivers the gentler, staged UI transition users would prefer. probably
  • But reintroduces keeping two live copies in sync — fatal on the core's measured production volumes, and with no way back. almost certain
  • The programme absorbs the syncing and volume costs the strategy already ruled out for stronger reasons. almost certain

Upside, and how big A genuinely smoother human transition — the one thing this option does better, and the reason it's tempting.

Worst case The sync and volume failures from the cutover and rollback pages, traded for a UI convenience. Not a trade worth making.

The gentler option isn't on the table — so the levers aren't a consolation prize, they're the whole answer. Spreading the change out would help the people side, but only by reviving a technical approach that fails on measured volumes and has no rollback. Once that's off the table, the change shock is a cost the strategy accepts on purpose and then buys down. The preference this serves is a workforce ready for a switch it can't avoid — and the dormant build is what makes training on the real system possible before it goes live.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Change management has no technical rollback — but it is itself part of what makes the technical rollback usable. If go-live is aborted at the go/no-go gate or reversed shortly after (see Rollback), the people who were trained and kept informed need to be told, quickly and clearly, that the old system is back and why — otherwise a clean technical rollback still lands as chaos on the floor. So the communication plan has to cover the unhappy path as well as the happy one: a pre-drafted "we've reverted, here's what that means for you" message is part of readiness, not an improvisation for a bad morning.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • This is a programme-plan workstream, not an engineering one. Its shape — who trains, in what format, how much floor support, how the comms plan is run — is for Marlex to define; the strategy's contribution is to name it as load-bearing and to make the dormant system available to train on.
  • Change shock is accepted, not eliminated. The big-bang concentrates the human change into one morning by design; preparation reduces it but can't remove it.
  • Documentation and training scale with scope. The more the product owners keep in the rebuild (see What's in scope), the more there is to document and train on — another reason a lean minimum-to-disconnect scope pays off on the people side too.

Return to the overview, or see What's in scope — the size of this workstream follows directly from how much is kept in the rebuild.

Decision 1 · Target data model

The shape of the new model

The new recruitment objects are designed from how Marlex actually uses the system — measured from ten years of production data — not copied from the shape of the package they replace.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

What this decides. The new native model's structure: which objects exist, how they relate to each other, and the handful of design rules — relationship types, record identifiers, history tracking, shared value lists — that are cheap to set now and expensive to change once a million records are loaded. It also decides how the model handles its volume: the largest new object is born with 1.4 million rows, and the live org's measured hot spots tell us exactly where a naïve design would hurt.

What we chose. Design the model from measured use, not by copying the old shape one-for-one. Concretely: relationships are ordinary lookups by default, with the rigid parent-child form reserved for the few places it genuinely fits; only fields that hold real data are rebuilt; and every migrated record carries its old-system identity so loads can be safely re-run and archived records exactly recalled.

The objects

What the new model is

One deliberate exception first: the candidate is not a new object. Candidates already live on Salesforce's standard person record (the Contact), and they stay there — with a new Marlex record type and new Marlex fields on the same rows. That preserves every file, note, activity and access grant already attached to a million candidates. It is a big enough call to warrant its own page — Keep the standard objects standard — but it is settled here as the model's starting point. Everything else the old package owned becomes a new Marlex-built object. Copying the kept fields onto the candidate record fits the platform's per-object limit with wide room to spare (169 of 800), because the old package's fields don't count against it — the field-headroom check confirms no limit forces a field to be dropped to make room.

For the developersNative targets, carrying Marlex's own vocabulary: MA_Application__c (application), MA_Job__c (job process), MA_Interview__c, native placement, MA_Employment_History__c / MA_Education_History__c, talent pool + junction, MA_Reference__c, native deal-sheet, and a candidate skills/categories child (today Candidate_Category__c) whose object-vs-field shape stays open pending the matching engine. English placeholders (placement, talent pool, deal-sheet) follow the programme's canonical model, which names these entities but assigns no MA_ target yet. Candidate = Contact with the Marlex candidate record type and MA_ fields back-filled onto existing rows.

Alternatives considered

Copy the old shape, or design from measured use

Copy the package's shape one-for-oneRejected

Recreate each old object and field as a like-named native one. It makes the data mapping trivial — and inherits every mistake the package made for Marlex. The old candidate carries 141 package fields, of which only 52 held real data when every field was re-measured against live production (measured); the other 89 were empty, system defaults, or formulas that simply recompute a link or a rating and carry nothing worth moving. Five child objects were built as rigid parent-child attachments that can never be re-pointed — the exact property that now forces restructuring work before cutover. Copying the shape would rebuild the bloat and re-plant the traps.

Redesign the domain from scratchRejected

Merge and re-model concepts freely — fold interviews into applications, rethink the process stages. Tempting on a whiteboard, but every departure from a one-to-one record mapping makes the data migration a transformation problem instead of a copy problem, makes old-vs-new validation ambiguous, and retrains recruiters on concepts, not just screens. The rebuild's safety rests on being able to prove the new system holds the same truth as the old one.

Same concepts, measured slimming, deliberate structureChosen

Keep the old model's concepts one-for-one — a record there is a record here, so migration and validation stay simple — but decide every structural property deliberately: rebuild only the fields that carry data (on the candidate, only 51 of 141 do), choose each relationship type on its merits, and name everything in Marlex's own vocabulary. The mapping stays auditable; the mistakes don't come along.

The design rules

Five properties set now, because they're expensive later

Relationships

Ordinary lookups by default; the rigid parent-child form only where it genuinely fits. The strict form deletes children with the parent, blocks re-pointing forever, and — the operational kicker — serialises writes on a busy parent. The old package chose it five times in ways that now block the cutover. The new model reserves it for true containment with no re-parent case and no write-hot parent (the candidate's own work and education history lines are the clear fit — though whether they stay separate objects at all is settled only when the matching engine choice and its feed contract land); everything else — application to job process, application to candidate, placement, interview — is a lookup, required where the business requires it.

Identity

Every migrated record carries its old-system identity in a dedicated, indexed external key. This is what makes the data load safely re-runnable (a failed load resumes as an update, never a duplicate), lets archived records be recalled exactly, and gives old-vs-new validation an exact join. A second class of external keys carries partner identities — the job-board application already has one — so inbound feeds de-duplicate on arrival.

Record types

Only where the process genuinely branches — never as decoration. The candidate gets the new Marlex record type (why the candidate stays the standard record at all is its own decision). Placements need a type split because seasonal contracts live a different lifecycle from standard ones. A record type added "for flexibility" is a permanent tax on every layout, permission and automation decision after it.

History

Field-history tracking on from day one for the status and decision fields on applications, processes and placements. The old system's audit trail cannot come across in the data move, so the new one starts recording the moment records land — the earlier it's on, the shorter the blind spot.

Vocabularies

Shared value lists defined once, restricted by default. Statuses, countries, sub-regions and other vocabularies that appear on several objects are defined as global value sets so "the same list" can't quietly diverge per object — a divergence the old model exhibits today.

Together, these rules are also what make the model portable: nothing in it hardcodes a single country or brand — variation lives in the country key, record types and shared value lists rather than in the structure — so the model can serve the wider group later without redesign (the build-wide rule behind this is stated on the Rebuild approach page).

Volume

The model is born big — and the hot spots are measured

Most new Salesforce objects grow into their volume over years. These are born with it: on activation morning the application object holds 1.4 million rows and the candidate's history objects nearly 900 thousand more. We queried the live system for exactly where that volume concentrates, and three findings shape the design.

13,151applications on the single busiest job — one mass-recruitment campaign concentrates thousands of writes on one parent record
3,589applications held by the single most active candidate — heavy, but comfortably inside platform tolerances
707,667candidates owned by a single "unassigned" placeholder user today — two thirds of the pool on one owner
  • Busy parents rule out the rigid relationship for applications. The busiest job carries 13,151 applications (measured) and inbound job-board applications arrive at roughly 27,000 a week. Under the rigid parent-child form, every child insert briefly locks the parent — a mass campaign would queue thousands of inbound applications behind one job record. The lookup form doesn't lock the parent. This is the single strongest structural argument on the page, and it's measured, not hypothetical.
  • Live counters are computed off the write path. The old model keeps a whole set of "number of…" counter fields per job, maintained by automation on every application write. The new model computes counters asynchronously (and only the ones the business actually reads live) so a burst of inbound applications never fans out into a burst of parent updates.
  • The load path stays automation-free. Nothing on the new objects' insert path may be required for a record to be valid — validation and enrichment run behind switches that are off during bulk loads. This is what lets 1.4 million rows load in hours rather than days, and it's a standing property, not a migration-weekend hack: the same switches serve every future mass load.
  • Ownership is assigned meaningfully, not parked. Two thirds of candidates today sit on one placeholder owner (measured — 707,667 records). Real ownership is rebuild-time hygiene regardless of the sharing model (see Security & sharing) — and a single owner holding hundreds of thousands of records becomes the classic performance trap the moment the objects are later tightened to private.
