  • The dead weight doesn't board. Whether the 461 thousand never-active candidates migrate at all is a staged business call (Data migration); if they don't, the archive page decides where they live instead. Either way the model is not sized around carrying them forever.
For the developersFan-out measured in production: top candidate 3,589 applications (next: 1,792 / 1,640 / 1,124); top jobs 13,151 / 6,855 / 3,883 applications. Contact ownership: 707,667 on "Sin Asignar", 150,483 on the package's system user, 49,754 on the top human owner. External keys: MA_TR_Migration_Id__c (unique, external ID) on every migrated object; the job-board key pattern already exists as MA_External_Id_InfoJobs__c on the application. Bulk loads run with trigger-bypass switches on (the shouldSkipTrigger guard pattern), parents before children, children upserted by external key.

How the options compare

Measured slimming versus copying the old shape

The genuine fork is the first one: copy the package's shape and get a trivial mapping, or design from measured use and get a better model for slightly more design work. The from-scratch redesign fails on migration provability and isn't compared here.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Same concepts, measured slimming Chosen

One-to-one concepts for a provable migration; every structural property decided deliberately.

  • The model carries only fields that hold data — on the candidate, 52 of the 141 analysed (measured) — and stays legible for years. almost certain
  • A slimmed-away field turns out to matter and is added back after go-live as an ordinary change. possibly
  • A relationship-type call proves wrong after load and needs a painful restructure. unlikely

Upside, and how big Half the field surface never gets built, the write-hot paths are engineered for the measured load, and the five re-pointing traps are not replanted.

Worst case A missed keep-field — recoverable, because the checksummed migration extract keeps the source data recallable by key regardless of what was loaded.

Copy the old shape one-for-one Rejected

Like-for-like objects and fields; mapping is trivial.

  • 74 empty or default fields (measured) are rebuilt, documented, secured and maintained forever. almost certain
  • The rigid parent-child choices are recreated, and with them the next generation of re-pointing traps. probably
  • A mass campaign queues inbound applications behind a locked hot job record. possibly

Upside, and how big The field mapping writes itself — a one-time convenience during build, paid for over the model's whole life.

Worst case The new system inherits the old one's operational scars on day one, and the programme rebuilt them at full price.

A copied shape is a one-time saving; a designed shape is a permanent one. The mapping convenience of copy-the-shape is spent the week the mapping is written. Everything the measured design buys — half the field surface gone, no lock-prone hot parents, no unre-pointable children — is collected every year the system runs. The migration stays provable either way, because the concepts still map one-to-one; only the structure is decided fresh.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

While the build is dormant, the model is the cheapest thing in the programme to change — objects are empty, so a field, relationship or record type can be reworked freely on an ordinary day. That reversibility ends abruptly at the data load: once 1.4 million rows are in, a relationship-type change means restructuring live data. The discipline is therefore a model freeze at the first migration rehearsal — the rehearsal must run against the final shape, and anything the rehearsal disproves gets one bounded correction window before the second rehearsal. After go-live, additive changes (new fields, new types) stay ordinary; structural changes are treated as small projects.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The keep-list confirmations. The 51-field migrate set includes fields no code reads but people may rely on — the product owner's confirmation of those, and of the fields presumed to be system defaults, is due before the first rehearsal.
  • Which counters must be live. The async-counter design needs the business list of numbers that must be visible on the record versus fine in a report — shorter list, cheaper model.
  • The seasonal census. Every record class that reactivates seasonally (the seasonal placement type is the known one) needs enumerating before relationship and record-type choices freeze, because seasonal records are the ones that can't simply finish their lifecycle in the old system.
  • Platform index tuning. External keys are indexed by the platform automatically; whether the reporting filters need additional custom indexes is a post-rehearsal measurement, not a guess to make now.
  • The candidate's child entities wait on the engine. Skills and the work-and-education history are separate objects today partly because the current engine's re-indexation wants them that way — their object-versus-field form freezes only when the matching engine selection and its feed contract land.

Continue to Keep the standard objects standard for the decision this model rests on, then Security & sharing for who can see and edit what. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and live volume queries.

Data model · reference

What we're replacing

The complete inventory behind the data-model decision: every recruitment object, field, record type and relationship in the package we're leaving — and exactly what replaces it in the native build. This is the mapping, not the argument.

Measured live · production org · 14 July 2026 · living document

Read this if you need to see, object by object, what carries over and what is left behind. The decision page explains why the model is shaped this way; this page is the line-by-line what. Every number here is counted against live production on 14 July 2026, not estimated — counts drift daily as job-board applications arrive, so they are re-measured before the migration rehearsal.

The rule that decides everything below: for data, the test is simply is it populated? A field or object holding real data is rebuilt and migrated; an empty one, a formula that only recomputes a link or a total, or a piece of package plumbing is left behind. There is no "recently used" judgement — presence of data is the whole signal.

The shape of the replacement

Twelve objects carry data; two dozen are left behind

12objects rebuilt and migrated (or back-filled onto a standard record) — everything the business actually uses
320fields carry data and are rebuilt, of 1,139 non-system fields examined — the other 819 are empty, formulas or plumbing
23package configuration and process-log objects left behind entirely — they die with the package

Two further objects — candidate references and the deal sheet — are rebuilt as fresh capabilities but hold no data to move (both are empty in production today). Naming note: every native object and field name is English — including the Marlex custom fields that carried Spanish names in the old system, which are anglicised now that their object is rebuilt. Names are shown prefix-free; the final prefix (if any) is a separate naming decision that changes only the label, never the structure.

This page is the readable summary; the complete row-by-row mapping — every field, record type and relationship with its measured fill rate and disposition — is in the workbook Marlex - TargetRecruit Rebuild Data Model Map.xlsx.

What this inventory covers — and what it deliberately leaves out

This is the inventory of the recruitment package's own objects — the ones that vanish when the package is removed and therefore need a native replacement built and their data moved into it. It is not the full list of every object in the rebuild. Objects Marlex built itself over the years — its case-and-task record, training enrolments, invoicing records and the like — are not in the tables below because they are not being replaced: they are Marlex's own, they survive the package's removal untouched, and the rebuild simply keeps them and re-connects their links to the new native objects. So an object being absent here means "kept and re-connected," not "out of scope." Which objects get rebuilt versus kept is set on the What's in scope page.

Every recruitment object — kept or dropped

The objects that carry data or a rebuilt capability, largest first, then those left behind. "Back-fill" means the records already live on a standard Salesforce object and stay there; only a record type and fields are added.

Row counts are live as of 14 July 2026. The biggest object left behind — an email-parsing transaction log with 145,293 rows — holds process history, not business data, and is not migrated. Full disposition detail for every object is in the accompanying workbook.

Reading the "how it moves" column

  • Migrate — the records are copied into a new native object.
  • Back-fill in place — the records already live on a standard Salesforce object (the candidate on the Contact record) and are not copied anywhere; a Marlex record type and the kept fields are simply added onto the rows that are already there.
  • Run-off — records for a still-open process (an active placement) are not migrated; they are left to finish their natural lifecycle in the old system, which keeps running read-only until the last one closes. Only the classes that can't finish on their own — the seasonal placements — are force-migrated in a wave.
  • Archive — records for closed, finished work are moved to low-cost storage rather than into the live model, so the new system isn't sized around history it never edits. Where they live is decided on the archive page.
  • Rebuild capability — the object is built fresh because the business needs the feature, but there is no data to move (it is empty in production today).

Why the rebuilt Job object is called ATS_Job__c, not Job__c

The obvious internal name for the rebuilt job object would be Job__c. That name is already in use in the live system: the recruitment package installed a small hidden configuration table that happens to occupy exactly that name. It holds one row of settings, nothing a user ever sees — but because it is a configuration table, its name is permanent and cannot be changed or reused. Building the new job object under the same name is therefore impossible.

The rebuilt object takes the name ATS_Job__c instead. This was found by building a working prototype of the job object against a copy of the real system before committing the design — which is exactly the point of prototyping: it surfaces this kind of hidden collision now, at design time, rather than during a live go-live. The other rebuilt objects were checked the same way and their names are clear. The name change is invisible to users; it affects only the technical identifier, not any label or screen.

Fields

Field by field — what carries, what's dropped

For each rebuilt object, the fields that hold data and move across. Every native field name is English — the new model uses one language throughout. Fields fall into three origins: renamed package fields (anglicised and de-prefixed from the vendor's names), Anglicised Marlex fields (custom fields that carried a Spanish name in the old system, given an English name now that the object is rebuilt), and carried fields (Marlex custom fields already named in English, kept exactly — a straight copy). Empty, formula and plumbing fields are counted but not listed individually — there is nothing to move.

How each field was decided

  • Kept — the field holds real data. Its measured fill rate is shown in the table beside it; anything above zero that isn't a formula or a piece of plumbing is kept, however low, because low-but-real data is still someone's record.
  • Dropped — one of three things is true: the field is empty in production (nothing to move); it is a formula that recomputes a link, a rating or a total from other fields (the new model recomputes it, so there is no stored value to migrate); or it is package plumbing — a geocoding status, a rendered HTML snippet, an internal identifier — that has no meaning outside the package we're leaving.
  • The signal is presence of data, not recency of use. We deliberately do not judge a field by when it was last touched: the package's "last modified" stamp was overwritten across every record by a past bulk load and is unreliable. A field that is populated is treated as worth keeping; proving a populated field is genuinely still used is a confirmation with the product owner, noted in the open items, not a reason to drop it now.

Record types

Record types — carry the ones holding data, drop the empties

Record types were never population-measured before; they are here. A record type with zero live rows is a piece of unused configuration and is not recreated.

One record type dominates each object: the interim job process (49,516 of 57,110 jobs), the active-proposal and interim-request applications (711K + 526K), the interim-request placement (37,567). Interviews use a single record type. Six job record types and several application/placement types hold no data and are dropped.

Relationships

How the objects connect — 130 lookups, 3 rigid links, 12 dropped

The package connects almost everything with ordinary lookups, and the new model keeps it that way — a lookup lets an object be loaded, re-pointed and even restructured without locking the record it points to. The rigid parent-child form (which deletes children with their parent and serialises writes) is reserved for the 3 places it genuinely fits.

The three rigid parent-child links — and why each earns it

  • Talent_Pool_Member__c -> Talent_Pool__c — pool-owned membership junction; delete-with-pool; member roll-up
  • Education_History__c -> Contact — candidate-owned sub-detail; delete-with-candidate; TR had MD on Education, lookup on Employment — unify to MD
  • Employment_History__c -> Contact — candidate-owned sub-detail; delete-with-candidate; TR had MD on Education, lookup on Employment — unify to MD

The one link that must NOT be rigid

The application-to-job link is a lookup, never a rigid parent-child link. One job in production carries 13,151 applications — above the platform's comfortable limit for a rigid child collection — and a rigid link would lock that job record on every inbound application, queueing a mass-recruitment campaign behind a single lock. The lookup form carries no such lock.

The 12 dropped relationships all pointed at objects that are themselves left behind (deal sheets, saved searches) — there is nothing to connect to. The full list of every recreated lookup, and every field decision behind this page, is in the accompanying workbook Marlex - TargetRecruit Rebuild Data Model Map.xlsx.

Open items

Still to confirm with the team

Most of the mapping is settled on measured evidence. A handful of calls need a business confirmation before the model freezes — they are the places where "the data is present" isn't the whole answer.

Four confirmations before the model freezes

  • Saved searches — keep the data, or just the feature? The old system holds roughly 59,000 personal saved searches. The plan is to rebuild the search capability but not carry the old saved-search records, which are mostly stale. Needs a yes from the product owner before we drop them.
  • Two rare record types. One application type ("Genérica", 2 records) and one talent-pool type ("Contact", 1 record) hold almost no data — confirm whether to carry them, fold them into another type, or drop them.
  • Duplicate summary fields — which is the source of truth? The job-board integration created its own copies of several candidate summaries (education, experience, skills, languages, cover letter) alongside the system's original fields. Both are populated; the business should confirm which set is authoritative so we don't carry two versions of the same thing forever.
  • The placement approval fields. A cluster of placement fields (who requested a change, when, the termination reason, reactivation dates) is populated on only 3–9% of records. They look like a manager-approval workflow that may be dead or superseded — confirm they're still used before carrying them.

Beyond these four, a small number of candidate fields are populated on well under one percent of records; they migrate by the "presence of data" rule, but the product owner may choose to drop the near-empty ones. That is a low-stakes tidy-up, not a blocker. The naming of the rebuilt fields is English throughout; whether any of the carried Marlex fields also take a shared prefix is the separate naming-standardisation decision noted above.

Boundaries

What this page deliberately does not cover

  • Marlex's own back-office objects — invoicing, offers, offices, the InfoJobs question bank, the 3-million-row feedback log. These are already native Marlex objects, not part of the package; they stay exactly as they are. The rebuild only re-points their links from the old placement and job objects to the new ones.
  • Other installed packages — job broadcasting, credentials, the portal content system, document merge. Handled by the integration and portal workstreams, not the recruitment data model.
  • The candidate's own rows — a million candidates and their client contacts are not migrated at all; they already live on the standard record and stay there. See Keep the standard objects standard.

This inventory sits under the data-model decision. For the reasoning behind keeping the candidate on the standard record, see Keep the standard objects standard; for how the data actually moves, Data migration. Every figure measured live against production on 14 July 2026; re-measured before the migration rehearsal.

Decision 2 · Keep the standard objects standard

Keeping package-extended objects on the standard record

The whole model hangs off one choice the other pages take as given: when the package extended a standard Salesforce object, does the rebuild keep that object — or reincarnate it as a brand-new custom one? This page shows the working, with the candidate as the case that makes the stakes plainest.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

Why this gets its own page. The data-model, security and data-migration pages all begin from "a candidate is not a new object — it stays on the standard person record." But that premise is not special to the candidate. Everywhere the package extended a standard Salesforce object, the same call recurs, and it is load-bearing for the rest of the set. So it deserves to be argued once, as a rule, not assumed six times.

The rule. A standard object the package extended stays that standard object. Where the package gave it a record type, the rebuild swaps in a new Marlex record type; the Marlex fields go on the rows that already exist. It is never reborn as a custom object — because everything the platform attaches to a standard record (files, activities, sharing, change history, the standard features) survives only if the record keeps its identity. The package's own record type disappears when the package is removed, so a record-type swap is unavoidable regardless — but the record itself, and everything hanging off it, stays put.

The flagship case. The candidate — the standard person record, the Contact — is the hardest instance of this rule: a million-plus records, each carrying a decade of files, activity, sharing and history. It is where the asymmetry is starkest, so it is the worked example throughout this page. The other five extended objects follow the same rule for the same reason, at lower stakes.

The objects in play

Which standard objects the package extended

The package did not invent a private universe of objects. It reached into six standard Salesforce objects and added fields (and, on some, its own record types). Each of those objects is subject to this rule, because each carries platform-attached context that only survives if the object keeps its identity. The candidate is the one that makes the case; the rest ride the same logic.

  • Contact — the candidate. The critical one: over a million records, each with uploaded CVs, logged calls and emails, access grants and a full change history. Reborn as a custom object, all of that is orphaned. This is the case the whole page turns on.
  • Account. The client and company records. Same principle — the accumulated activity, files and relationships live on the standard record and only survive if it keeps its identity — at a fraction of the candidate's volume and stakes.
  • ContentVersion. The document store itself. It is the mechanism by which files attach to standard records; moving off it would be moving off the very thing that makes "files survive" true. Keeping it standard is not a preference, it is the point.
  • Activity. The logged calls, emails and tasks. These attach to standard records by standard machinery; a custom re-model would have to re-create the timeline by hand.
  • User. The internal users. A handful of added fields on a record type of object that cannot be re-modelled as a custom object at all — it stays standard by definition.
  • Opportunity. A single added field. The extension is trivial, but the rule is the same: the standard object stays, the one Marlex field rides along.
For the developersPackage (AVTRRT__) field counts on the extended standard objects (prod, 15 July 2026): Contact 141, Account 24, Activity (Task/Event) 18 each, User 1, Opportunity 1. These are managed-package fields, so they do not count against the per-object custom-field limit (see Field headroom below). The candidate lives on standard Contact; today's AVTRRT__ record types are package-owned and die at uninstall. Files live in standard ContentVersion/ContentDocumentLink against the record — survive uninstall untouched.

The stakes

What is attached to a candidate today

A candidate is not a row in a table. Over ten years, each of more than a million candidate records has accumulated things the platform attaches to the standard record, not to the package: uploaded CVs and documents, logged calls and emails, who can see the record, and its change history. None of that is package data — it lives on the standard person record and its standard related objects. The choice on this page is really a choice about that accumulated context, on the candidate and on every other extended object: does it survive the rebuild untouched, or does it have to be dragged across?

1.06Mcandidate records already on the standard person object — the case that makes the rule bite hardest
Files · activities · sharing · historyall attached to the standard record, none of it package-owned
6standard objects the package extended, all subject to the same keep-the-record rule

Alternatives considered

Same record, new object, or keep the package's type

Keep the standard object + new Marlex record type + Marlex fields on the same rowsChosen

Each extended object already exists as a standard record under the package's record type. The rebuild changes the record type to a Marlex one and copies data into new Marlex fields on the same rows — an update in place, not a move. Files, activities, access grants and history are all preserved for free because the record they hang off never changes identity. It is also the simpler thing to build and maintain: one object, one identity, no dual-model to keep in sync. What can go wrong (worst on the candidate, where the volume is): re-stamping a million records is itself a mass update inside the freeze (the person record carries active automation that must be held during it); the package's hidden logic might react to the record-type change; and a missed screen or permission assignment could leave some users looking at the old layout on go-live morning. All three are handled in the runbook — but they are the residual risk this choice carries.

A brand-new custom object per extended objectRejected

Re-model each extended object as a fresh custom object, clean and purpose-built. The cost is severe and permanent, and it scales with what the object carries — which is why the candidate makes it unmistakable: it orphans everything attached to the existing standard records — the CVs, the activity history, the sharing, the change log would all have to be re-created against new records, or lost. It forfeits the standard capabilities each object brings (for the candidate: marketing, duplicate management, standard relationships). And it creates a permanent double identity — the same human, or the same client, existing as both a standard record and a custom one — that every future integration and report has to reconcile forever. It is also more to build and more to get wrong: a dual model is exactly the kind of reconciliation that a mostly mid-level and junior delivery team ends up maintaining by hand. A clean object bought at the price of a decade of context is not clean.

Keep using the package's own record typesImpossible

The tidiest-sounding option — change nothing about how the records are typed — is not available. The package's record types belong to the package and are deleted when the package is removed. Records left on them would be orphaned. A record-type change is therefore not something the rebuild chooses to do; it is something the uninstall forces on every extended object that has a package record type. Given that, the only question is which new record type — and a Marlex-owned one on the same object is the answer that keeps everything else still.

Why the chosen option wins

The record has to change type anyway — so change only that

The decisive insight is that the third option's impossibility reframes the whole choice, on the candidate and on every other extended object alike. Because the package's record type dies at uninstall, every affected record must be re-typed no matter what. Once that's a given, the new-custom-object route isn't buying itself a clean start — it's paying, on top of the unavoidable re-stamp, to also abandon the files, history and sharing and take on a permanent double identity. The chosen route does the one thing that must happen (re-type in place) and nothing that doesn't. It is the minimum true change; it is also the option a mid-level team can build and run without a dual-identity model to keep reconciled. That is why the rest of the set can safely treat "the extended standard objects stay standard — the candidate stays a Contact" as settled.

For the developersCandidate = standard Contact; today's AVTRRT__Candidate record type is package-owned (dies at uninstall), records re-stamped to a Marlex MA_ candidate record type. Same pattern on the other extended objects that carry a package record type. The candidate re-stamp is a ~1.06M-row Bulk update inside the freeze, run with trigger-bypass guards on (Contact carries 8 active triggers); it joins the runbook as a first-class load step, measured in rehearsal. The behavioural probe includes a record-type-change probe on Contact; the activation checklist carries the full profile × record-type × page-assignment matrix; duplicate/matching rules are reviewed against the new record type before the first rehearsal.
Why the switch has to be a page assignment, not a profile edit

A user holds exactly one Profile at a time — Salesforce does not support assigning more than one. That single fact rules out "the switch is a profile change" as a mechanism for any object that stays on its existing identity (the candidate, and every other kept standard object): a profile can't be flipped per-user at cutover without a mass profile reassignment, which is slow, hard to stage ahead, and hard to reverse cleanly. The record-page switch instead has to ride the axis that Salesforce does let vary independently of Profile: a Lightning Record Page is assigned per combination of App, object, Record Type and Profile (a FlexiPage assignment) — so which page a user sees on the (unchanged) Contact record is controlled by re-pointing that assignment, or by the record-type re-stamp itself changing which page's assignment rule matches, not by touching the Profile object at all. This is the same principle as the app-switch mechanism on Access & app switch — keep the profile untouched, move the axis that's actually stageable and reversible — applied here to which screen renders rather than to which rights apply.

How the options compare

Same record versus new object

The keep-the-package-type option is off the table (it doesn't survive uninstall), so the real fork — for every extended object — is between re-typing the existing standard record and standing up a new custom one. The comparison below is anchored on the candidate, because it is the instance where the two routes diverge most sharply; the verdict then applies to the whole class.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Re-type the existing standard record Chosen

New Marlex record type + fields on the same rows (1.06M on the candidate); an update in place.

  • Files, activities, sharing and history are preserved with no migration, because the record never changes identity. almost certain
  • The one-time candidate re-stamp of 1.06M rows (measured) runs long or trips the person record's automation inside the freeze. possibly
  • The package's hidden logic reacts unexpectedly to the record-type change. unlikely

Upside, and how big A decade of accumulated context survives the rebuild for free across all six extended objects, each record stays one identity, and the build stays simple enough for a mid-level team to own.

Worst case A slow or automation-heavy candidate re-stamp — a window-sizing problem the rehearsal measures and the bypass guards contain, not a data-loss one.

A brand-new custom object Rejected

Purpose-built object per extended object; records loaded fresh into it.

  • A clean, purpose-shaped object unencumbered by the standard record's baggage. almost certain
  • Files, activities, sharing and history are orphaned — re-created against new records, or lost. almost certain
  • A permanent standard/custom double identity every future report and integration must reconcile. almost certain

Upside, and how big Modelling freedom — real, but small against what a decade of standard-object context is worth, and the freedom is mostly available on the standard record anyway.

Worst case The rebuild ships having thrown away the candidate's history and split every candidate into two identities forever — the same, at lower volume, on every other object re-modelled this way.

The re-type is mandatory; the orphaning is optional — so don't opt in. Both routes re-type the records (the package type can't survive), so the new-object route's "clean start" is not a saving over the chosen one — it is the same mandatory work plus the loss of accumulated context and a permanent double identity. The chosen route's worst case is a window-timing problem the rehearsal sizes; the rejected route's worst case is irreversible. The candidate makes that asymmetry starkest, but it holds for every extended object — which is why every other page can build on "the extended standard objects stay standard" without re-arguing it.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

The record-type re-stamp is part of the cutover, so it rolls back the way the cutover does (see Rollback): before the freeze, the records are untouched on the package's type; the re-stamp happens inside the cutover window alongside the data load, and until the separate later uninstall — the true point of no return — a full rollback returns the records to the package's record type as part of reversing the switch. Because each record is the same physical record throughout — only its type and its Marlex fields change — there is no separate object to unwind and nothing attached to it is ever detached. That is the quiet dividend of the chosen option: the rollback of the biggest object in the org, the candidate, is a record-type reversal, not a data restore — and the smaller extended objects reverse the same cheap way.

Does the plan fit the platform

Field headroom — the copies fit, with room to spare

Keeping the candidate on the standard record means the package fields we keep are re-created as new Marlex fields on that same standard object — value copied in place. A fair question is whether that risks running out of room: Salesforce caps how many custom fields an object can hold. It does not, for one decisive reason.

The package's own fields don't count against that cap. Salesforce excludes managed-package fields from the per-object custom-field limit. So the 141 package fields on the candidate record were never using up the budget the new Marlex fields draw from — and the feared "old fields plus new copies, all at once" peak never bites. The only fields that count toward the limit are the ones Marlex itself created.

On the candidate — the only standard object that receives copied fields in place — that means 117 Marlex-created fields today, plus the 52 kept fields copied across, is 169 against a ceiling of 800. Every other rebuilt entity (jobs, applications, placements, interviews and the rest) is a brand-new object with its own fresh field budget, so it carries no such risk at all.

Custom-field headroom on the standard objects the rebuild keeps (Enterprise Edition, prod, 15 July 2026). Only Marlex-created fields count; package fields are exempt.
Object Marlex-created now Copied in place Projected Limit Status
Contact (candidate) 117 52 169 800 Green — 79% free
Account 185 0 185 800 Green
Opportunity 91 0 91 800 Green
Activity (Task / Event) 5 0 5 100 Green

Why the other objects copy nothing across. Only the candidate carries fields into the rebuild, because only the candidate's data is part of the recruitment model being rebuilt. The package fields on the other standard objects belong to TargetRecruit features the rebuild is not carrying — on Account, the package's own geocoding and deal-economics fields; on the calls-and-tasks records (Task and Event), a broadcast/WhatsApp messaging feature; on Opportunity, a single lookup back into the old job object. None of it is core recruitment data, and almost all of it is empty in production: of these fields, the great majority hold no data at all, and the few that do are geocoding output or recomputed formulas — values a native build regenerates rather than moves. So they are not re-created as Marlex fields; they simply retire with the package at uninstall. This is the same test applied everywhere — populated business data is carried, empty or derived or out-of-scope-feature data is not — not a limits decision.

The same picture holds at the org level: 130 Marlex-created custom objects today plus 11 new rebuilt ones is 141 against a 2,000 ceiling; custom code sits near a quarter of its budget and shrinks on rebuild; automations and custom-metadata are all comfortably clear. No limit forces a field to be dropped or the package removed early to make room — the empty-field cleanup and the one-time record-storage peak are managed for their own reasons, not this one.

For the developersPer-object counts split by NamespacePrefix via FieldDefinition: a null prefix is org-created (counts vs the 800/object EE limit); any prefix is managed-package (exempt). Contact shows 299 total custom fields in Setup — 117 org-created + 182 managed (AVTRRT 141, FCMS 15, CRDNTLS 9, TRGTFCMS 6, TRPRTL/plaunch 5 each, BCST 1). Activities share one 100-field pool across Task and Event. Org custom-objects limit (EE) is 2,000, managed-package objects exempt (211 today). Full working and the org-level table are in the field & object limits note alongside the custom-metadata note.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The candidate re-stamp is a mass update in the freeze. Sizing it is a rehearsal measurement, not an estimate — the same window discipline as the data load, on the same objects. The smaller extended objects re-stamp cheaply by comparison.
  • The package's reaction to the type change is unprobed. Whether hidden package logic fires on a record-type change is a black box until the sandbox behavioural probe clears it — a gating item before the design freezes.
  • The assignment matrix must be complete. Every profile's record-type and page assignment, across every extended object, has to be enumerated, not remembered, or some role sees the old layout on go-live morning — carried by the activation checklist.
  • Duplicate and matching rules. The rules tuned for the package's record type must be reviewed against the new one before the first rehearsal, so they don't misfire on the re-typed records.
  • Candidate child entities. How the candidate's related records are re-modelled still waits on the matching-engine decision (see Matching engine); that choice may add child objects under the re-typed candidate, but does not disturb the keep-the-standard-record rule itself.
  • The dropped features on the other objects are assumed unused — to be confirmed. The judgement that the package's Account geocoding and deal-economics fields, and the broadcast/WhatsApp messaging fields on the calls-and-tasks records, are out of scope rests on the rebuild plan plus their near-empty state in production. That they carry no live business use is a working assumption, not a business sign-off — it needs confirmation with the product owner and Marlex IT before the drop is treated as settled. Low-risk (the messaging fields are empty), but explicitly a validation point, not a closed decision.

This is the premise the data model, security and data migration pages build on. For the question behind the whole programme — why rebuild at all — see Why rebuild. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit.

Decision 3 · The matching engine

The matching engine — buy or build, open

The one capability the rebuild does not reconstruct from the package: reading CVs into structured profiles and matching candidates to vacancies. It runs as an external service behind a Marlex-owned interface — but whether that service is bought from a specialist or built in-house is under active re-evaluation.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 13 July 2026 · buy-vs-build re-opened 16 July 2026

Why this gets its own page. Candidate–vacancy matching and CV parsing are the hardest capability in the whole system, and for a while they were the single biggest feasibility question hanging over the rebuild direction itself: if this couldn't be solved without the package, the premise of the programme was at risk. The Why rebuild page carries the answer in one caveat — "buy the hard component" — but a call this consequential deserves its working shown, not a footnote.

What is settled, and what is open. Settled since 3 July 2026: the engine is not reconstructed from the package, it runs as an external service behind a Marlex-owned interface, and search is two-tier — everyday lookup built natively in the rebuild, heavy semantic matching and CV parsing answered by the engine. What is open again is whether that external service is bought from a specialist (Textkernel — the engine already inside today's package — or Taleva) or built in-house. The 3 July call leaned buy; a cost-driven re-evaluation on 16 July has re-opened it. This page still lays out the buy case in full because it is the reference argument — read it alongside the re-evaluation note directly below.

Open decision — buy vs. build in-house (re-opened 16 July 2026). A specialist-engine quote came in at roughly €44 per user per month across ~450 users — on the order of €1M over five years — which Marlex judged disproportionate for its search mix: an estimated ~60% of searches are simple (location, role, licence) needing only native/keyword search, and only ~40% want semantic ranking. That reframes the engine as potentially over-specified, and puts an in-house build — vector embeddings plus a cheaper AI model, fed directly from the new objects — back on the table as a serious option.

The two risks that decide it. An in-house build (a) is a capability no one internal has built before — no working product to copy, quality-at-scale unproven — and (b) introduces a hard cross-team dependency: an external backend team owns the matching service, Salesforce owns only the interface and the callouts, so if that team does not deliver, the rebuild cannot complete this capability. Buy avoids both — proven quality on day one, an integration task rather than an invention — at a recurring cost. The build case is real precisely where a bought package would force a two-way sync into its own objects (see below); the buy case is strongest on time and certainty before November 2027. The decision needs a named owner, the minimum-to-switch-off-TR requirement defined first, and parity tested on real Spanish CVs before either path is committed. Until then, the engine is carried outside the rebuild effort estimate.

The stakes

What the engine does, and why it can't simply be rebuilt

Inside today's package, when a recruiter uploads a CV it comes back as a structured profile — skills, experience, education — and when they open a vacancy, candidates arrive ranked by fit, not filtered by keyword. Both tricks are powered by a specialist engine (Textkernel) embedded in the package. It is the part of the system recruiters' daily throughput leans on hardest, and it is the part least like ordinary business logic.

Two facts rule out treating it as just another component to rebuild. First, it is a black box twice over: the package's logic is unreadable from outside, and the engine inside it is a specialist vendor's proprietary product — there is no specification to rebuild from. Second, the platform's native search tools are not a matching engine: they find records that contain a term; they do not rank a million candidate profiles against a vacancy by meaning. An in-platform rebuild of semantic matching would strain the platform regardless of how well it was coded.

1.06Mcandidate profiles the engine must parse and index — the scale any answer has to work at
0lines of the engine's logic readable from outside — there is no specification to rebuild from
2tiers of search in the new system: native for everyday lookup, the engine for matching and parsing

Alternatives considered

Buy it, license it, build it, or live without it

Buy a specialist engine as an external service, behind a Marlex-owned interfaceLeaning — under review

Contract one of the engines whose entire business is parsing and matching — Textkernel directly, or Taleva — and integrate it as an external service through the same Marlex-owned interface rule every rebuilt edge follows (see Integrations & middleware). Quality arrives proven on day one, at the level a dedicated vendor sustains. The costs are real: a new external dependency, a recurring fee, and a genuine build task — the pipeline that feeds the engine from the new model and keeps its index current. The dependency is deliberately bounded: the engine answers questions through a door Marlex owns, so a disappointing engine is swapped behind the interface, not migrated off.

License only the engine module commercially, without the platform around itOpen — a buy shape

A variant of buying, still on the table: license the same engine the org effectively uses today as a stand-alone module, rather than contracting a new engine relationship. Smallest quality risk — it is the behaviour recruiters already know — but the commercial terms would tie one component to the recruitment-software vendors whose decisions forced this programme. Acceptable only because the interface keeps it swappable; whether it beats the direct contract is a commercial evaluation, not an architectural one. The choice between the two buy shapes is the first open item below.

Build matching and parsing as Marlex-owned codeRejected — with one exception

Re-create the engine natively, the way the rest of the system is being rebuilt. As the default it fails three ways at once: there is no specification to build from (the engine is proprietary and unreadable); semantic ranking at this volume is the wrong job for the platform's native tools; and even a heroic success would have spent the programme's scarcest resource — time before November 2027 — building an unproven engine for a capability that is not what differentiates Marlex. This was the option whose infeasibility once put the whole rebuild direction in question; taking it off the table as the default is what settled that question. The one exception, worked below: an engine that can only index its own objects would force a permanent two-way sync onto the highest-volume objects in the org — at which point a bounded self-built matcher may be the lesser evil.

Ship without an engine — manual search and filters onlyRejected

Launch the rebuild with native search alone and let recruiters filter by hand. It reads like a scope saving and is actually a capability regression: ranked matching is load-bearing for recruiters' daily throughput, and a replacement system that visibly works worse than the one it replaces fails on adoption — the acceptance bar for the rebuild is capability parity, not feature-count parity. Degraded matching is exactly the kind of parity miss that bar exists to catch.

The deciding axis

Own the platform; rent the engine

The rebuild is argued on ownership — so buying a component needs squaring with that, and the square is this: the two dependencies are not the same shape. The package Marlex is leaving owned the things that must never be rented again — the data model the operation runs on, and the exit path away from it. The engine owns neither. It holds no Marlex data model hostage: profiles and vacancies live in Marlex's own objects, and the engine answers questions about them through an interface Marlex controls. If the engine's vendor is acquired or sunsets the product — the exact event that forced this programme — the response is an adapter swap and a re-index, not a two-year replacement programme. Ownership of the door is what makes renting what's behind it safe.

The same conclusion falls out of the programme's own design doctrine — platform capabilities first, buy what's specialised, build only as the last resort. Parsing and matching are the textbook specialised buy: a commodity to Marlex's business but the entire business of the vendors who sell it. Building it would invert the doctrine at the hardest possible spot.

For the developersTwo-tier search: everyday lookup = native list views, filters and SOSL against Marlex fields; matching/parsing = REST callouts to the external engine behind a Marlex-owned port with a direct adapter first (the same port rule as every edge — see the integrations page). The engine needs a feed: an initial index of ~1.06M profiles plus incremental updates on profile change, built against the new model. Parse results land in Marlex fields, never package fields. Note the model dependency: the candidate's child entities (skills, education, employment history) are separate objects today partly because the current engine's re-indexation wants them that way — their object-vs-field shape in the target data model stays open until the engine choice lands, because the feed contract depends on it.

The shape that decides "buy" is safe

An engine that forces its own objects changes the answer

"Buy" is the right call — but how the bought engine plugs in is not a detail, it is the thing that decides whether buying stays consistent with a rebuild whose whole point is decoupling. There are two shapes a bought engine can take, and they are not equally good:

A service we call

The preferred shape. The engine runs as an external service — the profiles and vacancies stay in Marlex's own objects, we send it what it needs to score and it sends back the results, all through the Marlex-owned interface. Nothing new has to be kept in step; the engine holds no copy of the data. This is the shape the rest of this page assumes, and the one to insist on in the evaluation.

A package that forces its own objects

The shape to watch for. Some engines ship as a managed package that will only index its own objects — it can't be pointed at Marlex's. That quietly re-introduces the exact problem the rebuild exists to remove: to feed it, Marlex must synchronise candidate and vacancy data from its own objects into the engine's package objects, and then synchronise the match results back. A permanent two-way copy, on the highest-volume objects in the system, for the one capability we chose not to own — the coupling the whole programme is dismantling, rebuilt around a new vendor.

This flips the calculation in one specific case. If the only viable engine is a package that forces its own objects, then "buy" carries a permanent synchronisation burden on a million-plus candidate records — and at that point building a simpler matcher ourselves (an external service on our own infrastructure, or a bounded custom build, fed directly from the new model) may genuinely be the better call, precisely because it avoids the sync. That is a real possibility, not something to rule out: the decision to buy is firm, but it is conditional on finding an engine that plugs in as a service, not one that drags its own object model in behind it.

The rule for the evaluation

Prefer, in order: (1) an external service that reads Marlex's own objects; (2) a package that can be configured to use Marlex objects; (3) only if neither exists — weigh a package-that-forces-sync against building a bounded matcher ourselves, deciding on the volume and complexity of the sync it would impose. If the results must live in the vendor's objects and can't be written back cleanly, that is an accepted cost only when no service-shaped option and no feasible build exists. The feasible-team test applies: a permanent two-way sync on the biggest objects in the org is exactly the kind of machinery a mid-level team maintains at its peril — its fragility is part of the cost, not a footnote.

How the options compare

Buying versus building

The two buy shapes share a risk profile and differ commercially, and shipping without an engine fails the parity bar — so the real comparison is buy against build.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Buy the engine Leaning — under review

A specialist engine — contracted directly or licensed as a module — behind a Marlex-owned interface, with native simple search beside it.

  • Parsing and matching arrive at proven specialist quality — the vendors' whole business — rather than at first-attempt quality. almost certain
  • The feed and re-index pipeline lands on schedule as ordinary, well-understood integration build. probably
  • Engine selection or commercial negotiation drags, and the design decisions waiting on it — the candidate child entities, the feed contract — stall with it. possibly

Upside, and how big The single hardest capability in the system comes off the build's critical path and arrives proven — and the feasibility question that once hung over the whole rebuild direction is closed.

Worst case A slow selection squeezes dependent design work — bounded by deciding the engine early, which is why it leads the open items below.

Build it in-house Re-opened 16 Jul

Two different builds hide under this label. Re-creating parsing and semantic matching on the platform stays rejected — it strains native tooling at a million profiles. What re-opened on 16 July is a narrower build: an external service off-platform (vector embeddings + a cheaper AI model on Marlex's own cloud), scoped to the real search mix (~60% simple, ~40% semantic) rather than to a full specialist engine. The outcomes below judge the on-platform build; the off-platform build is judged in the re-evaluation note near the top of this page.

  • Marlex owns matching outright, with no engine vendor at all. almost certain
  • The in-house engine reaches the specialist vendors' parsing and matching quality within the programme's window. rarely
  • Semantic ranking over a million profiles strains the platform's native tools no matter how well it's coded. probably

Upside, and how big No engine vendor — real, but small: the interface already contains the vendor, and matching is not where Marlex differentiates.

Worst case The programme's hardest deadline held hostage by an R&D project — the outcome that put the rebuild direction itself at risk while this was an open question.

The buy case (the leaning, now under review). The ownership argument that decides the rebuild does not extend here, because the engine holds nothing hostage: it answers through a door Marlex owns and can be swapped behind it, where the old package owned the data model and the exit path both. On this reading, buying spends none of the scarcest resource — time before November 2027 — on a component the market already does better, and if the bought engine later disappoints, the answer is a better engine behind the same interface, not an in-house rewrite.

Why it re-opened. That verdict assumed the choice was buy-a-service versus build-the-full-engine-on-platform. The 16 July re-evaluation introduced a third framing — an off-platform service scoped to Marlex's actual search mix at a fraction of the quoted cost — that the original verdict did not weigh. The buy case above still holds on time and certainty; the in-house case now competes on cost and fit. The tie-breakers are the two risks in the re-evaluation note (unproven-at-scale, cross-team dependency) against the cost gap — decided once the minimum requirement is defined and parity is tested on real Spanish CVs.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

This decision's fail-back is unusually cheap, and that is by design. Before cutover: the two-tier split means everyday native search ships with the rebuild regardless of the engine's state, and matching-and-parsing parity is a written criterion at the go/no-go gate — the cutover does not proceed on a hope that the engine will come good. After cutover: a disappointing engine is replaced behind the Marlex-owned interface — an adapter swap and a re-index, while the platform, the model and the data stay put. The one commitment with no cheap reverse is the commercial one: an engine contract binds for its term, which is why the term itself — short enough to preserve the swap freedom the architecture pays for — is part of the evaluation, not an afterthought.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Buy or build in-house (re-opened 16 July 2026). The prior lean to buy is under cost-driven review against an off-platform in-house build. Needs a named owner, the minimum-to-switch-off-TR requirement defined first, and parity tested on real Spanish CVs before either path commits. If it goes in-house, the external-backend-team dependency becomes a tracked delivery risk. Until resolved, the engine sits outside the rebuild effort estimate.
  • Which buy shape — and does it force its own objects. Direct contract (Textkernel or Taleva) versus licensing the module commercially is undecided — and the sharper question underneath it is whether the chosen engine plugs in as a service reading Marlex's objects, or a package that forces a two-way sync into its own. The first is clean; the second may tip the call toward building a bounded matcher ourselves. This gates real design: the candidate child-entity shape and the feed contract both wait on it. Decide early.
  • Commercial terms. Not yet negotiated. The tension to manage: a longer term buys price, a shorter term preserves the swap freedom that justifies buying at all.
  • Parity is proven, not assumed. The engine is evaluated against real Marlex CVs and vacancies before the design freeze — parse quality on Spanish-language CVs is the thing to test, not the brochure.
  • The feed is build scope. An initial index of a million profiles plus incremental updates against the new model is a real integration deliverable, on the same footing as the ERP and job-board edges.
  • A second capability leaves with the package. The package's LinkedIn connection is also vendor-provided; whether it is in live use is still being verified, and if it is, replacing it is a separate decision of this same buy-versus-build kind.

This decision rides the interface rule set on Integrations & middleware and refines the premise argued on Why rebuild; the model shape it gates is on Target data model. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit.

Decision 4 · Security & sharing

Who sees what in the new model

At cutover the new objects open exactly the way the old ones do today, so go-live changes nothing about who sees what — and the private, country-keyed model is tightened afterwards, as a deliberate stage on its own timing. One business decision can pull that stage forward: if the internal HR team is onboarded at cutover rather than after it, the model must be born private on day one.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 13 July 2026

What this decides. The access model of the new native objects: their default visibility, how access is granted back, who owns the records, what permissions users hold, and — a genuinely separate design problem — the door through which anonymous job-board applicants write into the system.

What we chose. At cutover, the new objects carry today's sharing model forward unchanged — they open the same way the old ones open now, so go-live moves no boundary and adds no sharing metadata to the worst window. The private, country-keyed model is still the target, but it lands as a deliberate post-cutover stage: once the system is live and stable, we tighten to private defaults with a country key and one rule per object, on empty-of-risk timing rather than crammed into go-live. Two pieces of new-object hygiene still happen at rebuild time, because neither depends on the open-versus-private choice: records get real owners instead of the unassigned pile they sit in today, and the inbound job-board door is rebuilt as a controlled entry with an explicit field list.

The one condition that changes this. Carrying the open model forward is the right default only while nothing confidential lives in the system. The moment the internal HR team is onboarded — running internal recruitment for backfills and internal moves, where a process can be "replace this director" and must not be visible to the person being replaced — an open model is a live confidentiality breach, not a deferrable nicety. So HR onboarding and privatisation are inseparable: wherever HR lands, private lands with it. That makes this a conditional fork keyed to a single business decision — does HR onboard at cutover, or after it? If after (recommended), day one stays open and privatisation runs as its own later stage. If at cutover, the model is born private on day one — the "born private" option below flips from rejected to chosen. In both branches the HR model is built on the new custom objects and components, never on the legacy package.

Today's reality

The starting point is an open org

The current org is remarkably open. The candidate, application, job and interview objects are all set so that any internal user can edit any record — isolation between countries, offices or teams simply doesn't exist at the data layer, because until now there was only one country and openness was convenient.

308 / 390objects in the census are set open (read/write) to every internal user today
707,667candidates sit on one "unassigned" placeholder owner — ownership carries no meaning today
8anonymous guest-site users are active, including the door the job board writes through

That openness cuts two ways for this decision. It means there are very few real boundaries to preserve — carrying the current model forward is genuinely near-zero-risk, because almost nothing is private to break. But it also means the eventual tightening is real work: to guarantee current behaviour under a private default you would have to stamp a region or country field on every record and rebuild group-based sharing for everyone — a large batch of new metadata. The question this decision answers is when that work lands, not whether.

Alternatives considered

Carry it forward, born private, or never tighten

Carry current sharing at cutover, privatise as a post-cutover stageChosen

At go-live the new objects open the same way the old ones do today — so the access model on cutover morning is identical to the day before, and no new sharing metadata rides into the window. The private, country-keyed target is unchanged; it simply lands later, as its own scoped project on quiet timing: flip defaults to private, add the country key, add one rule per object. The risk it carries is that the tightening must actually happen — an explicit stage with an owner and a date, not a hope — and the country data it needs (a correct country on every record) becomes a post-cutover data task rather than a load-time one.

Born private and country-keyed at cutoverConditional — triggered by HR at cutover

Make every new object private from birth, stamp a country key at the data load, and grant back within-country access with one rule per object — using the rebuild as the one free moment to set the access model. It is the theoretically cheapest place to privatise, because the objects start empty. Against it: to guarantee today's visibility you must stamp region and country fields and rebuild group-based sharing for everyone, and all of that new metadata lands in the cutover — the single worst window to add complexity to. So while privacy alone can wait a few weeks, this option is not permanently rejected — it is conditional. It becomes the chosen path the instant the business decides to onboard the internal HR team at cutover: HR's confidential internal processes cannot sit in an open model even briefly, so if they go live with the cutover, the model must be born private then. Absent that decision, it stays deferred and the carry-forward option wins.

Never privatise — stay open foreverRejected

Carry the open model forward and leave it there. Cheapest of all and exactly what users know — and it hard-codes the single-country assumption into a platform the group intends to share across countries. A second country cannot join without seeing Spain's data, and candidate personal data editable by every user in the company is hard to defend to a regulator. This is the real long-term risk the whole decision exists to close — deferring the tightening is fine; abandoning it is not.

Why this shape

Two things at cutover, one thing after

The reversal from the earlier plan is about sequencing, not the destination: split the work so the cutover stays clean and the private target is still reached.

At cutover — carry sharing forward

The new objects open the way the old ones open today. Same read/write-to-everyone posture, no country key required to go live, no group-based sharing to rebuild. Because the model on cutover morning is identical to the day before, there is nothing new for the access layer to get wrong and nothing new to recompute. This is the feasible choice for the team that will run the cutover: fewer moving parts in the riskiest hours.

At cutover — ownership hygiene

Records get owners that mean something — and this does not wait for privatisation. Two thirds of candidates today sit on one placeholder owner (measured — 707,667 records), which is a performance trap and a nonsense answer to "whose record is this?" regardless of whether the object is open or private. The load assigns candidates to their office's owner structure; inbound records are assigned on arrival, never parked on a system user. This is new-object hygiene, independent of the open-versus-private choice.

At cutover — the guest door

The inbound job-board entry is rebuilt as a controlled service — also independent of the sharing choice. Whether the objects are open or private, an anonymous applicant should write through a narrow, explicit door rather than inherit the old package's portal machinery. The rebuilt entry ships with the new objects; its detail is below.

After cutover — privatise as a stage

Once the system is live and stable, tighten to the target model on its own timing. Flip the new objects' defaults to private, add a country key to every record, add one sharing rule per object that re-grants within-country access. The private model carries two independent axes: the country key (a record's country gates cross-country visibility) and — the axis the HR requirement adds — an internal-versus-external distinction. External candidates and jobs stay visible to the whole recruitment population exactly as today, via a criteria-based rule keyed off a data-layer flag (a record type plus an Is_External__c boolean); internal HR processes are private and roll up a hierarchical internal role tree, so HR sees all internal work, a regional director sees only their own and those beneath them, and nobody sees upward. It is a scoped project with its own rehearsal and its own rollback — not a step bolted to go-live — and it runs when the calendar is empty of other risk, not on cutover morning.

Conditional — HR at cutover

If the business chooses to onboard the internal HR team at cutover, this stage collapses into cutover and the model is born private. Because HR's internal processes cannot be visible to everyone even briefly, privatisation cannot follow later — it moves into go-live, and the "born private" option becomes the chosen one. That is the single trigger that overrides the carry-forward default. The recommendation is to onboard HR after cutover so day one stays clean; HR has no existing users or data, so nothing is lost by standing them up a few weeks later on the custom model. The internal role tree and the internal/external flag are built the same way in either branch — only the timing moves.

Throughout — code-granted access

Any new code that grants record access is written country-aware from the first line. The org already holds a cautionary example: four hand-built sharing routines that cascade access along team and manager relationships with no boundary at all — the moment a relationship spans two countries, they would grant access across it. New programmatic sharing carries the country check even while the objects are still open, so the privatisation stage has nothing to retro-fit; the existing routines are corrected as their objects are touched.

The guest door

The anonymous write path is a design decision, not a leftover

Today, when a candidate applies on the job board, the application enters Salesforce through an anonymous guest user — no login, roughly 27,000 applications a week. We verified how that door actually works: the guest user holds no direct rights on the candidate or application objects at all (measured — zero object permissions); the writes happen inside code that runs with system-level privilege, exposed on public site endpoints. The old package's portal machinery works the same way.

The rebuild keeps that shape — a guest with no object rights, writing only through a controlled service — but rebuilds the door deliberately rather than inheriting it. This happens at rebuild time regardless of the sharing decision, because it is about new-object hygiene, not the open-versus-private default:

  • One entry service per inbound channel, writing only an explicit, short list of fields on the new objects. Nothing about the guest's privilege lets it touch anything else.
  • Arriving records are assigned to a real owner immediately — routed to the owning office, not accumulated on the guest or a placeholder. The 707-thousand-record unassigned pile is not rebuilt.
  • De-duplication happens at the door, on the partner's external key, so a re-sent application updates rather than duplicates.
  • The country is set by the entry service — an anonymous guest has no country of its own, so the channel (which job board, which portal) determines it. That mapping is captured now even though the country field only starts gating access at the later privatisation stage, so nothing has to be reworked when it does.
For the developersGuest surface verified in production: 8 active guest site users (candidate portal, staffing, recruiting, two job-board sites, marketing landing pages, two survey sites). The job-board guest users carry an "edit management" permission set plus site-generated sets; none hold object CRUD on Contact or the application object — inbound DML rides system-mode Apex (the webhook handler and the package's portal pages). The rebuilt entry = dedicated REST endpoint behind the integration port (see Integrations), allow-listed fields, external-key upsert, immediate owner routing. Persona-based permission sets replace profile-driven rights on the new objects at cutover; the OWD flip, country field and per-object sharing rules are the post-cutover privatisation package.

How the options compare

Carry current sharing versus born private at cutover

The stay-open-forever option is the real long-term risk this decision closes, not a live contender, so it isn't compared here. The live fork is between the two sequencings that both leave the target reachable: privacy built in at cutover, or the current model carried forward and tightened afterwards. Which one wins is conditional on the HR-onboarding timing — carry-forward is the default, but a decision to onboard HR at cutover flips the answer to born-private. The comparison below is the default case (privacy has no forcing deadline); read the "chosen" column as chosen unless HR is pulled into cutover.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Carry current sharing, privatise later Chosen

New objects open exactly as today at go-live; tighten to private and country-keyed as a separate post-cutover stage on quiet timing.

  • Go-live changes nothing about who sees what, so the cutover carries zero access-model risk and no new sharing metadata. almost certain
  • The privatisation stage lands cleanly after go-live, on empty-of-risk timing, and the target model is reached. more likely than not
  • The post-cutover stage is deprioritised once the system is live, and the open window stretches longer than intended. possibly

Upside, and how big The riskiest window stays as simple as it can be — identical sharing, no region or group-sharing metadata — and the tightening runs as a well-scoped project with room to rehearse.

Worst case The tightening slips; the org sits open for longer than planned. Visible and recoverable — the stage is scoped, owned and dated, and the open state is exactly today's, not a new exposure.

Born private and country-keyed at cutover Conditional

Private defaults, a stamped country key and per-object sharing rules all set at the data load, using the empty objects as the free moment. Deferred by default — but the chosen path if the internal HR team is onboarded at cutover.

  • Privatising empty objects is the theoretically cheapest place to do it — no live volume to recompute. probably
  • Guaranteeing today's visibility forces region and group-based sharing metadata to be built and land inside the cutover window. almost certain
  • A country-stamping or group-sharing defect surfaces at go-live, in the worst window to diagnose and fix one. possibly

Upside, and how big The privatisation is done in one pass at the cheapest possible moment — real, but it buys a benefit that can safely wait, at the price of loading the worst window.

Worst case A large batch of new sharing metadata plus a stamping defect meet on cutover morning, and an access problem competes with everything else going live at once.

Carrying current sharing is the feasible default; born-private is the right call the moment HR onboards at cutover. Privatising an empty model at birth is cheapest in theory, but the current sharing is so open that there is almost nothing to preserve, so — while nothing confidential lives in the system — carrying it forward is near-zero-risk and keeps the cutover clean. The tightening is then a well-scoped later project that still reaches the same target, and deferring it loses nothing but a few weeks. That default holds until one business decision changes the premise: onboarding the internal HR team introduces confidential processes that cannot sit in an open model even briefly, so if HR goes live with the cutover, born-private stops being the purist option and becomes the necessary one. The recommendation is therefore to onboard HR after cutover, keeping the clean carry-forward path — but the born-private branch is real and pre-designed, not rejected. The residual is discipline, not design: the privatisation stage must be owned and dated so "later" does not become "never" — which is what the residual-items section pins down.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

At cutover there is nothing to roll back on the access layer: the new objects open exactly the way the old ones do today, so the sharing model at go-live is identical to the day before. A full cutover rollback (see Rollback) returns users to a system whose permissions never changed, and the new-object side had no boundary to unwind. That is the whole point of carrying sharing forward — the riskiest window inherits no access-model risk to reverse.

The post-cutover privatisation is its own gated project with its own rollback, and that rollback is deliberately cheap: because tightening only adds restriction, undoing it means opening back up, which is the easy direction — a broader grant or a reverted default, minutes rather than a recalculation. Access is structure, so the privatisation is cutover-style, never a runtime switch — a lesson the plan already carries from the talent-pool analysis on the cutover page — but it runs on quiet timing where a fail-back costs the stage, not the programme.

In the born-private branch — HR onboarded at cutover — this cheapness moves into the go-live window with the privatisation. The access layer then does carry rollback risk: the private defaults, country key, sharing rules and internal role tree all land at cutover, and a full cutover rollback must unwind them. The fail-back direction is still the easy one (open back up, un-assign the internal grants), but it is no longer "nothing to reverse" — which is precisely why the recommendation is to keep HR onboarding after cutover unless the business requires otherwise.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The privatisation stage must be owned and dated. Carrying current sharing forward is only safe if the tightening actually happens — the private, country-keyed model is the unchanged target, just deferred. It needs an owner and a date after go-live so "later" does not quietly become "never".
  • Country data becomes a post-cutover data task. The private model needs a correct country on every record; because it no longer lands at the data load, stamping every record's country (everything existing is Spain) moves into the privatisation stage as its own data-quality work item, alongside deciding how the country is set for records that arrive through the guest door.
  • The ownership assignment map. Moving 707 thousand candidates off the placeholder owner needs a business rule (which office owns which candidate) — a data-quality decision with a deadline before the first migration rehearsal, and one that does not wait for privatisation.
  • Within-country visibility of commercial records. Whether offers and invoices are visible country-wide or only to the deal team is an open business question that shapes the privatisation stage and decides how the existing hand-built sharing routines are corrected — country-wide retires them; team-private keeps them with the country check added.
  • The HR-onboarding timing decision gates the whole sequencing. Whether the internal HR team onboards at cutover or after it is a business decision that decides whether the model is born private on day one or carried forward and tightened later — the two are inseparable because HR's confidential internal processes cannot live in an open model. It needs an explicit owner (the People lead) and a date before the cutover plan is locked. The recommendation is after-cutover, so the go-live window stays clean; the born-private branch is designed and ready if the business chooses otherwise. Independent of timing, the internal model needs its own design confirmation: the internal-versus-external flag (record type plus Is_External__c), the net-new internal HR role hierarchy, and a pass over the periphery objects (interviews, screenings, placements, activities, attachments) so a private internal process does not leak through a still-open child record.
  • The guest door review. The rebuilt entry services deserve a dedicated security review (field lists, abuse limits, what an attacker can do with the endpoint) before activation — anonymous write paths earn that scrutiny, independent of the sharing timing.
  • Old-system openness persists until retirement. The old objects stay open-to-all until they're retired — accepted deliberately, because retro-tightening a system on its way out buys risk and no future.
  • Sharing-dependent features need multi-persona proof, not single-user. Anything gated by the OWD, role hierarchy, a sharing rule or a permission/profile grant designed on this page can pass a single-user test while still being wrong for the role that actually needs it — see the testing-context rule on Testing behaviour parity.

Sharing decides who sees which records; the companion question — which objects, fields and app each user can reach, and how that moves to the new build — is on Access & app switch. Or return to the overview. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and live access-model queries.

Decision 5 · Access & app switch

Moving users off the old app and objects, at the same access level

At go-live every user should stop seeing the TargetRecruit app and its objects, and start seeing the new app and the new objects — with exactly the access they hold today. This page audits who can reach what now, groups users by that access, and sets out the permission sets that reproduce it on the new model.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 15 July 2026

What this decides. How each user's permissions — which objects and fields they can open, which app and tabs they see — move from the old package to the new build without changing what any of them is allowed to do.
