This is a distinct problem from who can see which records: that is the sharing question, carried forward unchanged at cutover and covered on the Security page. Here the question is object, field and app access, and it is genuinely close to a swap.

What we chose. Reproduce today's access on the new objects with a small set of permission sets mirrored from what each profile and set grants today — folded down to a handful of role sets plus the few narrow add-on sets that cut across them — built and assigned ahead of time while switched off, then flipped at cutover by assigning and un-assigning permission sets only, with no change to any profile in the go-live window. So that the flip is symmetric and reversible, the legacy app's visibility is moved out of the profile into its own permission set before cutover (a change users never feel), and the switch becomes: un-assign the legacy-app set, assign the new-app and new-object sets. Rolling back is the same toggle in reverse. Users keep the exact access level they have now. The reason it is nearly a swap and not a redesign is a fact the audit surfaced: the access model is simple and shallow — a handful of user groups, about fifteen objects, no permission-set groups, and broadly full access within the recruitment population — with a few small, exact differences the reproduction copies faithfully.

Today's reality

Access is shallow, broadly uniform, and split across two places

We read the live access model of the production org. It is far simpler than a decade-old package usually leaves behind — which is what makes the switch tractable.

509active users in total; the recruitment population is about 440 of them across four business groups
15package objects carry the access that has to be reproduced — not hundreds
699 / 717users work in one app — the stock Sales app, not a bespoke recruitment app

Three facts shape everything that follows:

  • Almost everyone has close to the same access — but not exactly. Within the recruitment population the object rights are broadly full create-read-edit-delete with read on candidate contacts, but the audit found real, small differences that have to be reproduced faithfully rather than flattened: recruiters can create-read-edit but not delete two objects (jobs and the talent pool); team leaders carry a narrow see-all on exactly two objects, not a broad one; and back-office carries a wide see-all across almost everything. There is no deep lattice to preserve, but "everyone gets full rights" is not literally true — the reproduction has to copy the actual grants, object by object.
  • The access is split across two layers — and not where you'd expect. The object rights themselves sit in the profiles, not in a permission set. The permission set that every recruiter carries grants the tabs and the app surface — what they see — plus a partial top-up of object rights. Reproducing today's access means reproducing both layers, and knowing which layer holds which grant is half the work.
  • There are no permission-set groups in play. The org has only the fifteen standard groups Salesforce ships, none assigned to a user. Access is profiles plus individual permission sets — a flat structure with nothing bundled to unpick.
A correction the audit forced

It was previously assumed the recruitment profile was near-minimal and that the recruitment permission set carried the object access. The live read shows the reverse: the recruitment profile grants the object rights directly (create-read-edit-delete on most objects, create-read-edit on jobs and the talent pool), and the permission set adds the tabs and only a partial object top-up. This matters because it decides what has to be removed at cutover — the object rights come off the profile, not the permission set.

The objects in scope

Fifteen objects, not a package's worth

The access that has to move is the access to the objects the rebuild replaces. The package ships hundreds of objects, but almost all of them are internal configuration no user ever opens; the ones that carry real user access — and therefore real access to reproduce — are the fifteen the data model rebuilds:

Old object (what users reach today) Becomes
Candidate contact Standard contact, re-typed
Application Application
Job Job
Placement Placement
Interview Interview
Job search Job search
Employment history Employment history
Education history Education history
Candidate source · source Candidate source · source
Talent pool · pool membership Talent pool · pool membership
References · deal sheet · saved search Rebuilt natively (capability, not data)

Everything else the package installs is configuration objects — search settings, home-page components, connectors, quick-review setup — that no business user opens and no permission has to be rebuilt for. They are dropped with the package, not reproduced.

The clusters

Four business groups, plus administrators and machines

Grouping every active user by the access they effectively hold — profile and permission set combined — collapses the whole population into a short list. There are not dozens of personas to reproduce; there are four business groups doing recruitment work, the administrators, and the non-human accounts that don't cross over as people.

Cluster Users What they can do on the recruitment objects today
Recruiter — the core population ~373 Create-read-edit-delete on twelve of the objects, but create-read-edit only — no delete — on jobs and the talent pool; read on candidate contacts; the recruitment tabs and both package apps visible. No see-all anywhere.
Team leader ~39 The recruiter's full create-read-edit-delete on all objects, plus a narrow see-all / edit-all on exactly two objects — jobs and one talent-pool object — not a broad widening across the model.
Director ~22 Full create-read-edit-delete across all objects, no see-all. This is the only group with literally uniform full rights — including delete on jobs and the talent pool, which the recruiter lacks.
Back-office / support ~23 Full rights with see-all and edit-all across almost every recruitment object (jobs are the one exception — create-read-edit and see-all, but no delete or edit-all) — a cross-desk operational role.
Administrator ~29 Modify-all-data across everything — reproduced by the standard administrator profile, not by a rebuilt permission set.
Integration & portal accounts ~30 Machine and site accounts (data feeds, the marketing and analytics connectors, the anonymous job-board door). These don't switch as people — they are re-pointed with the integrations, covered on the Integrations and Security pages.

The shape of the answer follows directly, with one discipline that keeps it honest: build the replacement sets by mirroring what each source actually grants, not by hand-designing a persona. For every profile and permission set that grants anything on an object, tab, page or field being replaced, we mint one mirror permission set that reproduces the same grants on the new objects — then collapse the mirrors that turn out identical. Because most of the low-count system and integration profiles carry the same full-rights signature, the mirrors fold down to a small handful — effectively the four business groups above — but the folding is discovered from the live grants rather than asserted, so a difference like the recruiter's missing delete, or a leader's see-all being two objects and not the whole model, is copied faithfully instead of being rounded off to "everyone gets full rights". Administrators keep the standard administrator profile; the machine accounts are re-pointed with the integrations.

The proposal

Replacement permission sets that reproduce each cluster

Every grant is rebuilt as a permission set on the new objects — never back into a profile. This keeps the profile minimal and makes the switch a matter of assigning and un-assigning sets, which is fast, reversible, and easy to prove. The base cluster grants become the role sets below. But the base clusters are not the whole story: on top of them sit a set of narrow, cross-cutting add-on permission sets — one gives almost every user a field filter on candidatures, others let sub-groups create talent pools or edit specific placement fields. These are already permission sets today, they cut across the clusters rather than belonging to one, and each has to be mirrored one-for-one onto the new objects and fields. They are part of the scope, not a footnote.

New permission set Reproduces Grants on the new objects
ATS Recruiter The recruiter grant (today split: object rights in the profile, tabs in the package set) Full create-read-edit-delete on the fifteen new objects, read on contacts, the new tabs. This is the base every recruiter gets.
ATS Team Leader The leader's wider reach The recruiter base plus see-all / edit-all on jobs and the talent pool.
ATS Back-office The support grant Full rights with see-all / edit-all across the recruitment objects.
ATS App access The visibility of the new recruitment app Visibility of the new recruitment app and its tabs — held separately so app access and object access can be flipped independently at cutover.
TR Legacy app access The visibility of the two old package apps, moved off the profile Visibility of the two package apps and their tabs. Assigned to everyone before cutover (a change no user feels), un-assigned at go-live, and re-assignable in seconds if go-live is reversed — this is what makes rollback a toggle instead of a profile edit.
Add-on sets (one-for-one mirrors) The cross-cutting permission sets that already exist today and grant something on an in-scope object or field A mirror of each existing add-on set, reproducing its grant on the new objects/fields: the near-universal candidature field filter (carried by almost every recruiter today), the talent-pool creation right, the placement-field edit sets, and the like. Same assignees, same grant, new objects. Not new roles — narrow top-ups that sit on top of the role sets.

The add-on row is a category, not one set: it stands for each existing cross-cutting permission set that touches an in-scope object or field. The mirror method finds them mechanically — any set granting something we're replacing gets a mirror — so none is missed, however small its assignment count. The largest, a candidature field filter, is assigned to nearly the whole recruitment population, so it is not optional.

What lives where today, and where it should live after. The grants that matter sit in three places today, and each moves on its own timing — the one rule is that nothing that has to reverse quickly is left in a profile:

App visibility
— move before cutover

Today: in the profile. The recruitment profile makes both package apps visible. Before cutover that visibility is lifted out of the profile into the TR Legacy app access set and re-granted to the same users — a net-zero change no one feels. This is the one preparatory move the rollback depends on: once app visibility is a set, turning the legacy app off (and back on) is an assignment, not a profile edit in the go-live window.

Tabs

Today: in the package permission set. The recruitment permission set makes the package tabs visible. After: the new tabs live in the ATS App access set; the legacy tabs travel with the TR Legacy app access set, so they turn off with the legacy app.

Object rights
— refactor after cutover

Today: in the profile. The recruitment profiles grant create-read-edit-delete on the fifteen package objects directly. At cutover this is left untouched — a user still holding rights on the old objects is harmless while those objects are being retired, and the new-object access comes entirely from the ATS role sets. Moving the old object rights out of the profile into a set is real hygiene, but it is post-cutover cleanup, kept out of the go-live window because it changes nothing about go-live safety.

Field access

Today: mostly profile, some in dedicated field sets. A few narrow permission sets already gate specific fields (placement fields, filters). After: field visibility on the new objects is set on the role sets, and the existing narrow field sets are reproduced one-for-one on the new fields where they still apply.

Why permission sets and not profiles

Putting the access that has to move in sets, not profiles, is what makes the cutover a switch rather than a migration. A set can be assigned to the whole cluster ahead of time while it points at empty, dormant objects, and un-assigned in seconds if go-live is reversed — and, crucially, the legacy app can be turned back on by re-assigning a set rather than re-editing a live profile under time pressure. It also moves the org toward minimal profiles, so a second country or brand later inherits a clean base instead of a profile stuffed with a decade of grants.

App & screen access

The right pages on the right objects, on day one

Access to an object is not the same as a usable screen. A user switched onto the new objects with no assigned record pages sees a raw default layout — technically full access, practically unusable. So the switch has to carry the UI, not just the rights:

  • A new recruitment app replaces the two package apps. Its visibility is granted by the ATS App access set; the legacy apps' visibility — already lifted into the TR Legacy app access set before cutover — is un-assigned at the same moment, so no user can navigate back into the old surface, and re-granting it (on rollback) is a single re-assignment.
  • New record pages for every new object, assigned to the new app and, where the object varies by type (jobs, applications), to the right record type — so a recruiter opening a job on the new object lands on the page built for that kind of job, exactly as the package does today across its several job layouts.
  • Page layouts and their assignments are rebuilt per object and per user group, matching what each cluster sees now. The audit read the existing layout and record-page assignments so the new ones can be a faithful reproduction, not a guess.
For the developersObject rights verified live on the 14 in-scope AVTRRT objects (2026-07-16 prod read). The four recruitment profiles are not uniformly full-CRUD: Marlex (373 users) = CRED on 12 objects but CRE- (no delete) on AVTRRT__Job__c + AVTRRT__Hotbook__c, no ViewAll/ModifyAll anywhere; Marlex Director (22) = CRED on all 14, no ViewAll; Marlex Leader (39) = CRED on all 14 plus ViewAll+ModifyAll on Job + Candidate_to_Hotbook only; Sales Support (23) = CRED+ViewAll+ModifyAll on 13/14, Job = CRE-+ViewAll only. Object CRUD lives in the profiles; TR_Permission_set (467 assignees) carries tab settings + a partial 10/14 top-up and zero applicationVisibilities. Cross-cutting add-on sets that also grant on in-scope objects/fields and must be mirrored: Candidaturas_Filtros_FLS (499), MX_Crear_TalentPool (40), MX_Editar_Placement_Status/_Prorroga (31/31), MA_Editar_Campos_Placement (27), MX_Facturacion, MA_HumactIntegracion_PS, and the Marlex Leader PS (Job+pool VA/MA). Apps come from the Marlex profile (both AVTRRT apps visible; default standard__LightningSales). No permission-set groups assigned. Build method: mirror-per-grantor deduped by permission signature — for each profile/PS granting anything on a replaced object/tab/page/field, emit one PS reproducing the legal PS-level parts (CRUD/FLS/tab/app/FlexiPage) on the new objects, then fold identical signatures. Yields ATS Recruiter / Team Leader / Back-office role sets + the add-on mirrors. Separate ATS App access set = new app visibility + new tabs; TR Legacy app access set = the two package apps' visibility + their tabs. The one profile change is done before cutover — lift the two AVTRRT app visibilities off the Marlex profile into the TR Legacy app access set and assign to the same users (net-zero); the go-live window then contains no profile edit: cutover un-assigns the legacy set and assigns the new sets. Profile object-CRUD is left in place at cutover and refactored profile→PS post-cutover. FlexiPage assignments per app and record type mirror the existing TR default pages. The stock Sales app can remain the home for users who work there.

The switch

What flips at cutover, and how it reverses

Because access is structure, not data, the switch is cutover-controlled, never a runtime toggle — the same lesson the rest of the plan carries. The design goal is that the go-live window contains no profile edit at all: everything that flips is a permission-set assignment, which is fast, pre-stageable and reversible. That is only possible because the one profile change — lifting the legacy apps' visibility into a set — is done days earlier. In order:

Before cutover — the one profile move

Lift the two legacy apps' visibility off the profile into the TR Legacy app access set and assign it to everyone who has it today. Net-zero: users see and reach exactly what they did before. This is the move that turns the whole switch into set assignments.

Before cutover — build & pre-assign

Build the new permission sets and record pages. Pre-assign the ATS role sets to every user in each cluster while the new objects are still empty and switched off — an assignment to a dormant object grants nothing visible, so this is safe days ahead.

At cutover — assign the new

Assign the ATS App access set: the new app, its tabs and the new record pages light up on the now-live objects. The role sets, already assigned, start granting real access the moment the objects carry data.

At cutover — un-assign the old

Un-assign the TR Legacy app access set. From this moment the two package apps and their tabs disappear for everyone — the point of the whole exercise — with no profile touched. The profile's object rights on the old objects are left as they are and cleaned up later.

Rollback

Re-assign TR Legacy app access, un-assign the new sets. Because every moving part is a set assignment rather than a profile edit or migrated data, the reversal is minutes and complete — the access model returns to exactly today's, including the legacy app coming straight back. This rides the full cutover rollback on the Rollback page.

How the options compare

Turn the legacy app off with a set, or edit the profile in the window

The role sets and their pre-assignment aren't in question — that part is settled. The real fork is how the legacy app gets turned off, because that is the one grant that must change at go-live and the one whose reversal decides whether rollback is clean. Move its visibility into a set beforehand and un-assign at cutover, or leave it on the profile and edit the profile in the window. Both hide the old app; they behave completely differently when go-live has to reverse.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Pre-move app visibility to a set, un-assign at cutover Chosen

Lift the legacy apps' visibility off the profile into a set before go-live (net-zero); the switch and its reversal are both set assignments.

  • The go-live window contains no profile edit at all — every flip is an assign / un-assign, reversible in minutes. almost certain
  • Rollback brings the legacy app straight back by re-assigning one set, with nothing to unpick. almost certain
  • The pre-cutover move is mis-scoped and a user briefly loses or keeps the wrong app before go-live. unlikely

Upside, and how big The riskiest window carries only set assignments; the single profile change is done earlier, on quiet timing, where a mistake costs nothing and is caught before it matters.

Worst case The preparatory move grants the wrong app to someone — visible immediately, days before go-live, and fixed by editing one set.

Edit the profile at cutover to remove the app Rejected

Leave the app visibility on the profile and strip it in the go-live window, re-adding it if rollback is needed.

  • It needs no preparatory move, so it feels like one less step. probably
  • A profile edit lands inside the go-live window — slower to make, slower to verify, and it applies to everyone on the profile at once. almost certain
  • Rollback means re-editing a live profile under time pressure rather than re-assigning a set. more likely than not

Upside, and how big One fewer preparatory task — a small convenience, spent in the most expensive place.

Worst case A profile edit misfires at cutover and the reversal is another profile edit, in the window where speed matters most.

The whole point is to keep profile edits out of the go-live window. Both options hide the legacy app; only pre-moving its visibility to a set makes both the switch and its reversal pure assignments, done and proven on quiet timing before cutover. The preparatory move is small and net-zero — users feel nothing — so there is no real cost to it, and it buys the one thing that matters most in the window: a rollback that is a toggle, not a live profile edit under pressure. Leaving object rights on the profile is fine because they never have to reverse at cutover; leaving the app there is not, because that is the grant the switch turns on.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

This is one of the cheapest parts of the cutover to reverse, because none of it is data and none of it is a profile edit in the window. A full rollback re-assigns the TR Legacy app access set and un-assigns the new ATS sets — the legacy app and tabs come straight back, the new app disappears, and every user is returned to the exact access they had the day before, in minutes. The object rights on the old objects were never touched at cutover, so there is nothing to re-instate there. Nothing to recompute, nothing to migrate back, no profile to re-edit under pressure. The one thing to verify in a fail-back is that the legacy app and its tabs are visible again — the rehearsal drills that path in both directions, on real users.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The pre-cutover app-visibility move must be proven net-zero. Lifting the legacy apps' visibility off the profile into a set is the move the whole rollback story rests on. It has to be verified — every user who could open the legacy app before can still open it after the move, and no one gains it — before go-live relies on it. Small and reversible on its own, but it is a real step with its own check, not a free assumption.
  • The object-CRUD profile refactor is a committed post-cutover item. Leaving the old object rights on the profile at cutover is safe only if the cleanup actually happens afterwards — moving them profile-to-set so the profile ends up minimal. It needs an owner and a date after go-live, the same discipline the privatisation stage carries, so "later" does not become "never".
  • A per-cluster access check belongs in the rehearsal. The proposal reproduces access by cluster; the rehearsal must confirm, for a real user in each group, that they can do exactly what they can do today on the new objects — no more, no less — before go-live trusts it.
  • The add-on permission sets need a one-for-one field mapping. The mirror method identifies every cross-cutting set that touches an in-scope object or field — the candidature field filter, talent-pool creation, the placement-field edit sets — so none is missed. The remaining work is exact rather than uncertain: each mirror has to be checked against the new field names and reproduce the grant only where the field still exists. A small but precise mapping task, and now inside the scope rather than a loose end.
  • The long tail of low-count grants. Beyond the four clusters, a scattering of single-user profiles and specialist sets grant rights on the in-scope objects (most are integration, analytics or portal accounts). The mirror method catches all of them by construction — anything granting a replaced object gets a mirror — but each single-user profile still needs a decision: fold it into a cluster mirror if its signature matches, or give it its own small mirror if it is genuinely distinct. The check is confirming every active user maps to at least one mirror before cutover, so none is left without a switch path.
  • Administrator access is assumed standard. The plan relies on the standard administrator profile carrying modify-all on the new objects, as it does today — worth a confirmation rather than an assumption.
  • This is object and app access only. Who sees which records — the open-versus-private question — is the sharing decision on the Security page, carried forward unchanged at cutover and tightened later. Nothing here changes record visibility.
  • The internal HR team is a net-new cluster, not in today's population. The clusters above reproduce the access of users who exist now; the internal HR / talent-acquisition team that would run internal recruitment has no users or data in the org today, so it is additive, not a reproduction. Onboarding it means a new persona permission set (its object and app grants on the new model), the record types and internal/external flag that separate internal from external work, and a net-new internal role hierarchy — all built on the new custom objects, never on the legacy package. Its timing is the same fork the Security page turns on: onboard after cutover (recommended, keeps go-live clean) or at cutover (which forces the born-private model on day one). Access-wise it is a straightforward mirror of the recruiter grant scoped to internal work; the confidentiality that makes it distinct is a sharing concern, handled on the Security page.

That completes the access side of what the new system is. Continue to Rebuild approach for how the old system is taken apart and replaced, or return to the overview. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and live access-model queries.

Decision 6 · Rebuild approach

New component, or a branch in the logic?

For each thing being replaced: build a fresh native component and switch it on later, or refactor the live one in place? And what actually turns the new behaviour on?

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

The decision. Every one of the hundreds of Marlex-built components fused to the package has to be rebuilt. For each, there are two ways to do it: build a new native component beside the old one and switch over later, or refactor the existing one in place. And once built, something has to turn the new behaviour on — a setting the team can flip, or the cutover event itself.

What we chose. Build a new component beside the old one as the default; refactor in place only at the few designed seams where old and new must run together past cutover. Turn logic on with a setting; let structure — the data model, screens, permissions, sharing — change only at the cutover. This keeps the running business untouched until the day it switches, and keeps the transition cheaper to build than the thing it replaces.

Why there's a decision at all

The package is a black box on shared objects

Two facts about the package shape every choice on this page. First, the package is a black box: Salesforce exposes its data-model shape — objects, fields, layouts — but never its logic. No code inside TargetRecruit can be read (confirmed: zero package code files are retrievable). What it does when a record changes can only be established by testing, not by inspection — so anything we assume about how it reacts to a write stays provisional until proven in a sandbox. Second, the package lives on Marlex's standard objects, not only its own — the candidate is the standard Contact, carrying 177 package fields and roughly 35 separate package elements. There is no clean seam to cut a shared object into pieces.

Together these push the default hard toward new component beside the old: Marlex's own components are readable, but how they interact with the unreadable package can only be proven by testing, never by inspection — so editing them in place, live, is a bet you can't check. And you cannot slice a shared object apart. The exceptions are few and deliberate.

For the developersThe rebuild scope is measured, not guessed. A July 2026 namespace-dependency scan found 632 Marlex-built components that reference a package object or field — the coupling surface to rebuild or re-point (managed-package internals are separate and are not rebuilt). By type: 144 Apex classes, 100 Flows, 53 reports, 40 custom-metadata mapping records, 30 apps, 30 LWC, 29 record pages, 29 path assistants, 28 formula/lookup fields, 24 field display-format specs, 15 layouts, 13 Visualforce pages, 10 permission sets, 10 report types, 9 email templates, 7 triggers, plus object sub-components (buttons, list views, validation rules, record types) and a handful of dashboards/sites. This is the effort surface, not a sliceability measure — what can cut over independently is decided by the objects a capability touches (see Activation & cutover), not by this count.

Alternatives considered

New component vs refactor-in-place

Four ways to produce each rebuilt component. The pre-mortem is why three were rejected as the default — and why one is kept as a narrow exception.

A new dormant component beside the old oneChosen · default

Build a native twin, deploy it switched off, flip to it at cutover; the package component simply dies when the package leaves. What can still go wrong: the live component keeps evolving while its dormant twin sits waiting — a fix made to the live one that isn't mirrored into the twin means go-live quietly regresses it. Across hundreds of coupled components that isn't an edge case, it's a certainty unless managed. It also carries a double-maintenance cost, and a twin that only ever passed its own tests may meet real usage for the first time on go-live morning.

Refactor the live component in placeRejected · default

Edit the running component to point at the new model. Every edit is a production change to the live business before cutover — nothing can be built ahead safely, and the risk lands early and continuously instead of at one rehearsed gate. For logic we can't even read, this is editing blind on a live system.

Refactor in place at the designed seams onlyAdopted · exception

A small number of Marlex-owned services were built to be model-agnostic and switchable. At those seams, refactoring the one service in place is right — it's the point of the design. The risk: the switch flips early or accidentally, or the new target it points at doesn't exist yet, and a flip with nothing behind it fails at runtime. Kept narrow, and guarded (below).

Build it in a separate org, then deployRejected

The org is the target. A separate-org build drifts from the real org's configuration, data shapes and package surface, and deploy-day becomes its own big-bang. Building in the live org, dormant, is what lets every rehearsal run against the system that actually counts.

Selected — and how the residual risk is controlled

The dormant-twin default's one real cost — twin drift — is managed as discipline, not hope: a register pairing every live component with its dormant twin, a rule that both change in the same code change, and a change freeze on the affected components that tightens as cutover approaches. It's the single biggest residual risk of the whole build-ahead approach, so it's engineered against rather than assumed away.

Why this cost is bigger than it first looks — the business keeps shipping

Marlex does not stop delivering to the business during the rebuild — the recruitment operation continues to receive new features on its normal cadence. That is the reality the twin-drift control has to survive: every change made to a live component that has a dormant twin has to be applied to the twin too, or the twin silently falls behind. So twin drift is not a rare event to guard against — it is a standing weekly workload for as long as coexistence lasts. Two things keep it bounded: the intake rule below (most new work is built native from the start, so it is the rebuild rather than a twin to maintain), and keeping each twin's life short (see Deployment — the components that change most are duplicated latest). The drift tax is the change-rate of the coupled surface multiplied by how long its twins live; the plan attacks both terms rather than relying on discipline alone.

The activation decision

What turns the new behaviour on — a setting or the cutover?

A built-dormant component still needs an on-switch. There were two clean extremes and a split; we took the split.

Gate everything behind settingsRejected

Impossible on platform fact: structure — record types, pages, roll-ups, sharing, permissions — physically cannot be turned on by a setting. The option dies on what the platform allows.

Change everything at the cutover, no settingsRejected

Loses the ability to test new logic in production ahead of time — every logic change becomes a deploy-under-pressure item crammed into the cutover window, the exact thing the approach exists to avoid.

Settings for logic, cutover for structureChosen

A rule the programme has agreed: a setting can turn logic on or off (automation, validation, calculations); it cannot change structure (the data model, which screen a user sees, roll-up totals, who can see what). Structure changes only at cutover, by an admin action. What to watch: mislabelling something structural as logic — formula fields are the classic trap, they read like logic but are compiled structure — and settings left switched on months early, or transition settings that outlive the cutover forever. Each is guarded by a pre-classified component matrix (below), default-off settings with restricted flip permission, and a named post-cutover clean-up sweep.

For the developersA flag can gate logic — Apex, invocable-from-Flow, validation rules, and what a formula evaluates at runtime (a formula can read $CustomMetadata/$Setup) — via Custom Metadata or hierarchy custom settings. It can never gate structure: data model, record-page/record-type/layout/app assignment, roll-ups, sharing. A formula field reads like logic but is compiled structure — not flag-gateable, and a formula referencing an AVTRRT__ field blocks uninstall. The trigger-bypass config currently lives in the managed AVTRRT__Config_Settings__c (dies at uninstall) and must be re-homed to MA_ before the first slice.

The other side of the switch

The old components need an off-switch too

Turning the new behaviour on is only half of the flip. The old behaviour it replaces has to stop at the same moment — and not everything old can be stopped the same way. The rule is symmetrical with the on-switch, and it decides where a small, deliberate refactor of the to-be-retired components is needed:

Stops by config or setup

No flag needed. Anything that can be switched off from configuration or setup — deactivating a record-triggered flow, unscheduling a job, un-assigning a permission set, retiring a layout — is simply turned off at cutover by an admin action. These need no code change to the old component; they are stopped from the outside.

Only stops by code

Needs a feature flag added to the old component. A triggered automation or a batch job whose logic must not run after the switch, but which has no config-level off-switch, needs a flag put into the deprecated component so it can be made inert at the flip. That is a small, deliberate refactor of some old triggers and batches — worth calling out because it is real build work on the components being retired, not just on the new ones.

User interface — leave it

No flag, no touch. The old screens and apps don't need stopping in code: the cutover moves users from the old application to the new one, and the old application is simply made invisible to them. An unused screen behind a hidden app does no harm until the package is removed, so the UI layer is left alone rather than flagged.

Why this is worth naming

It is tempting to assume "the new thing turns on, so the old thing turns off" for free. It isn't free for the code-only cases: a live trigger or batch that keeps firing after the switch will double-process records or write into objects that are moving. Enumerating which old components need a flag — and adding it during the build, not discovering it at cutover — is a named work item on the deprecated side of every coexistence pair.

A case inside the on/off rule

When the entry point doesn't move, but the logic behind it does

Everything above assumes the new behaviour arrives on a new object, so the new and old entry points are naturally two different components. There is a narrower case the same rule has to cover: the entry point itself — the trigger — stays on the object it already fires on, because that object isn't moving, but the logic it delegates to is what's coupled to the package and has to be rebuilt on the canonical model.

The shape of the case

The trigger stays valid — it still fires on the right object. The existing handler holds the legacy, package-coupled logic. A new handler is built for the canonical logic. Both handlers now compete for the same trigger event, and they must never run at the same time: whichever one is active has to be the only one that runs, chosen by configuration, not by which one happens to be deployed.

Two designs could make that choice. Only one is sound:

Two independent triggers on the same object, one deactivatedRejected

Simple to write, but it puts the safety property — exactly one runs — in the hands of trigger activation state and execution order, which Salesforce does not guarantee across two separate triggers on one object. A slip during deployment or a forgotten deactivation step means both fire on the same record.

One trigger per object, routing to legacy or canonical by configurationChosen

A single trigger reads a switch — the same Custom Metadata/hierarchy-setting mechanism used everywhere else in this decision — and calls exactly one handler. There is one entry point, so there is structurally no way for both implementations to process the same event; the routing lives in one place, not in the coincidence of two components' activation state.

The exception this creates to "don't touch production components"

The trigger is a live production component, and the routing switch has to be added to it. This is a deliberate, narrow exception to the rule that rebuilt logic lands on new components only (see the alternatives above): the entry point itself gets a minimal, controlled edit — adding the routing read and the call-out to the new handler — while the legacy handler it currently calls is left untouched until the switch flips away from it. The edit is additive (a branch, not a rewrite) and goes through the same review and freeze discipline as everything else this page treats as risk-bearing production change.

The same shape — one entry point, two candidate implementations, exactly one active — is not unique to Apex triggers. It has to be checked wherever an existing, non-migrating entry point could end up delegating to either a legacy or a canonical implementation:

Where a same-entry-point routing switch may be needed, beyond the trigger/handler case
Entry point What to check
Platform Event subscribers / event handlers Whether a canonical subscriber must take over from a legacy one on the same event, without both processing the same message.
Queueables, batch jobs, async processes invoked from shared logic Whether the enqueuing logic can call either implementation, and needs the same one-active-at-a-time guarantee as the trigger case.
Outbound integrations or listeners Cases where activation can't be managed purely by deployment (e.g. an always-on listener) and needs an explicit routed switch instead.

For automations with a native activate/deactivate mechanism — Scheduled Apex, Flows — the default stays what the rest of this page already establishes: deploy the new version inactive, and document the deactivate/activate sequence as part of the cutover runbook (see Activation & cutover). An additional feature flag is only worth adding there if the cutover needs immediate rollback or a period of deliberate coexistence beyond what native activation gives — not as a default for every native-togglable component.

The UI has the same shape of problem with a different mechanism. When an object stays on its existing identity — the candidate staying on Contact is the case that makes this bite — the screen a user sees can't be routed by a profile switch, because a user holds exactly one Profile: see Keep the standard objects standard for why the record-page assignment (App × Record Type × Profile), not the Profile itself, is the axis that carries this switch.

For the developersPer component that uses this pattern, define explicitly: which Custom Metadata/setting carries the routing value; which component types require it (trigger/handler routing) versus which are covered by native activation alone (Scheduled Apex, Flow); the cutover sequence (flip the switch, then retire the legacy handler once cutover is confirmed, never the reverse); the rollback sequence (flip the switch back — the legacy handler must still be present and untouched to make this free); and what logging or debug signal confirms which handler actually ran for a given transaction, so "which path is live" is verifiable in production, not just assumed from the metadata value.

Where the real choice lives

For most components, it isn't a choice

Putting the two decisions together: "new component vs branch" is only a genuine decision for business logic. For everything declarative or structural, a new component is simply inevitable.

How each kind of component is rebuilt
Kind of component How it's rebuilt Why
Reports, fields, layouts, record types, roll-ups, permissions, sharing, dashboards, paths A new component — built on the native model, activated at cutover Not a choice — a report's base object is fixed, a field can't be re-pointed to another object, a layout is tied to its object. There's no logic to branch; there's only the artefact, and it's rebuilt.
Flows that touch a package object directly A new flow on the native object, activated at cutover Wired to the package object; can't be branched or switched — rebuilt.
Business logic (Apex, delegating flows) — most capabilities A new component + a setting It flips once, one-way, at cutover. A branch that only ever takes the new path afterwards is just a translation layer you didn't need.
Business logic at the integration edges (and the placement run-off, only if that fallback is taken) A branch (the translation layer) The only place both backends are genuinely live at once, and the only place the decoupling outlives the cutover — so the branch earns its cost.

The translation layer (the "anti-corruption layer" pattern) — a Marlex-owned service that maps between the old and new models and routes each piece of work to either backend — is more expensive to build than a setting, so it is used only where the cost is justified: the integration edges (the connectors to the ERP and the job board, where the decoupling from vendors is permanent) and the placement run-off if that fallback is taken. The test is simple: does this capability need to run on both systems at once, or does the decoupling outlive the cutover? If yes, the layer earns its cost. If it flips once, one-way, and never looks back — which is most capabilities — a setting on a dormant new component does the same job for far less.

For the developersThe built example is MA_PlacementService + its DTO/mapper, target chosen by a Target_Backend value in a Custom Metadata Type — the only genuinely object-agnostic, switch-ready service in the org today. The interview equivalent (MA_InterviewService) is a 5-field stub bound to AVTRRT__Interview__c, so it needs an object-agnostic refactor before any switch works — and the interview slice flips one-way anyway, so it wants a dormant new object + a setting, not a full ACL. Reserve the ACL for the integration edges (MA_Net4Client, the InfoJobs publication service — already ACL'd, decoupling permanent) and the placement bridge.

This is worth stating as a flat rule for the developers who build day to day, because "use the translation layer" is easy to over-apply. New work falls into three buckets, and only one of them wants a full layer:

Native only

A plain domain service — no layer. A component that reads and writes only the new model has no old backend to abstract from. Wrapping it in a port and adapter to "match the pattern" builds machinery between internal modules that does nothing and, worse, is one more thing for a mid-level team to maintain. Most new work is here.

Needs old data during coexistence

Read it through the one shared seam — not a per-component layer. A native component that needs data still living on the old objects goes through a single Marlex-owned read seam — the only thing allowed to touch the old objects while both systems run. At cutover that one seam re-points from the old model to the new, and everything above it is unchanged. Giving each such component its own port/adapter/DTO stack would multiply the machinery and still all have to be rebuilt at cutover — the sync-in-miniature the whole rebuild avoids.

A true integration edge

A full translation layer — this is where it earns its cost. Two live backends at once, or a decoupling that outlives the cutover: the ERP and job-board connectors, and the placement run-off if that fallback is taken. Here the port and adapter are the point, not overhead.

Why one shared seam, and why it has to exist early

The decisive test is not "does it touch old data" — it is "does the decoupling survive the cutover?" For a new feature reading old data it does not: after go-live there is one model and any per-feature abstraction is dead weight to rip out. So a single shared read seam plus a feature switch does the job for a fraction of a full layer — and it is the choice a mid-level and junior team can hold. The one hard dependency: that shared seam (and the native foundation behind it) must be stood up early, before the bulk of new delivery. If it isn't there when the business's continuing work needs old data, developers reach for a direct package reference by default — re-creating exactly the coupling the rebuild exists to remove. Standing the seam up first is a milestone-zero prerequisite, not a mid-build nicety (this is the same seam the delivering-while-rebuilding rule depends on).

The defaults layer

Per-component build rules

Underneath the per-capability routing sits a settled set of defaults per kind of component — how each is rebuilt, whether a setting can turn it on, and how it's cleaned up when the package leaves. It's a reference layer, not a routing one: a capability's crossing route is chosen first by its data (see Activation & cutover), then this decides the mechanics for each of its parts. Its real value is the cleanup rules — the handful of component types that silently sink a cutover if missed.

The three component types that sink a cutover if missed — all structural, none switchable
Component type The trap
Permission set Left unassigned at cutover → users locked out on go-live morning.
Scheduled job / batch Still pointed at a package object → throws the instant the package is gone. Must be unscheduled and re-pointed before uninstall.
Formula / roll-up referencing a package field A hard dependency that silently blocks the package from being uninstalled at all. Must be removed or re-pointed before the gate.

The full defaults matrix — every kind of component, whether a setting can activate it (logic can, structure can't), and how it is cleaned up when the package leaves — is below. It is a reference to check a component against, not a way to route a capability.

Defaults by component type — switch-activatable? and how it's cleaned up at uninstall
Component type Switch-activatable? Cleanup at uninstall
Apex class Yes (logic) Native class re-pointed; package classes auto-die
Apex trigger Yes (via bypass switch) Native trigger re-pointed; package-object triggers auto-die
Record-triggered flow Activation no / branch yes Deactivate + delete package-object flow; native already active
Screen flow Partial (via its launcher) Package screen flow replaced at cutover
Scheduled flow / batch No — danger Unschedule before uninstall — throws otherwise
Autolaunched / invocable flow Yes (behind a switch) Native invocable re-pointed; package one auto-dies
LWC Partial (page assignment) Placed at cutover; packaged component dies
Aura / Visualforce No Packaged UI auto-dies; native replaces (VF often a net-new build)
Formula field No (compiled structure) Remove/re-point before uninstall — a package-field formula blocks it
Roll-up summary field No (structural) Package roll-up dies; native roll-up already in place
Lookup / master-detail field No (structural) The five master-detail children must be restructured pre-cutover
Validation rule Yes (self-bypass via a setting) Remove/re-point any that reference a package field before uninstall
Record type No (assignment = structure) Re-stamp records to the native record type first, or they orphan
Page layout No (structural) Package layouts die; native assigned at cutover
Lightning record page No (structural) Packaged pages die; native activated at cutover
App No (structural) Packaged apps die; native assigned at cutover
Permission set No (structural) Assign at cutover — a miss = users locked out
Sharing rule / Apex sharing No (structural) Package sharing dies; native activated at the cutover weekend
Report / report type No Rebuilt native (base object is fixed); activated at cutover
Dashboard No Rebuilt on native reports; activated at cutover
Email template Partial (the send logic) Package-merge templates break; native replaces
Custom metadata / custom setting Yes — this is the switch Re-home config off the managed setting before uninstall (it dies with the package)
Quick action No (placement = structure) Package actions die; native placed at cutover
Path assistant No (tied to record type) Package paths die; native pre-built

A build rule

New work is built native from day one

Because the business keeps delivering through the rebuild, there is a rule for where that new work lands, and it is the single most important lever the plan has against a moving target: every new feature is built on the new native model from the start — never as a fresh extension of the package. Where a new capability needs data that still lives in the old objects, it reads it through the same controlled seam the rebuild uses during coexistence; it does not add new automation, fields or logic onto the package's objects.

The consequence is decisive for scope. If new work were built on the package, every sprint would enlarge the surface that has to be disconnected — the rebuild would be chasing a target that grows faster than it can close. Built native-first, the same new work does the opposite: it is the rebuild advancing. A feature born on the new model needs no twin, because there is no old counterpart to keep in step — so the disconnection scope only ever shrinks, never grows (the scope boundary this protects is on What's in scope). The one thing this requires is that the native foundation — the model, the seam to read old data, the shared services — is stood up early, before the bulk of new delivery, so there is always somewhere native to build.

A build rule

Built portable by construction

One rule applies to every component the rebuild produces, whatever route it takes: business variation is configuration, not code. Country, brand, business vertical, contract modality, language — the dimensions along which Marlex's operation varies — are never hardcoded as branches in the new logic. New components read them from configuration records, so a behavioural difference between two countries is a data change an administrator makes, not a code change a developer deploys. The old org shows exactly what the alternative produces: division and geography baked into dozens of near-duplicate components that must each be changed by hand.

This rule is also a deliberate bet on the system's future beyond Spain. The rebuild does not pre-design a group-wide standard — what other group companies will share is decided later, on evidence of real reuse. What it does instead is cheaper and stronger: it builds every component so that adopting it elsewhere is parametrisation, not refactoring. Nothing in the new build assumes a single country, and the model's own carriers for variation — the country key, record types, shared value lists — are set from birth (see the design rules), even though the sharing model that eventually keys on the country field is a deliberate post-cutover stage, not a go-live change. When the group's standard is defined, the components that join it will already be shaped for it.

How the options compare

Dormant twin versus refactoring in place

The default rebuild choice — a new dormant component beside the old one, versus editing the live component in place — comes down to where the risk lands and how big it is. Both have a real downside; they are not the same size.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

New dormant component beside the old Chosen

Build a native twin switched off; flip to it at cutover; the package component dies with the package.

  • The live component gets a fix the dormant twin doesn't, and go-live silently regresses it — if twin drift is left unmanaged. almost certain
  • A regression still slips through to go-live despite the twin register, the same-change rule and the tightening change freeze. unlikely
  • A twin that only passed its own tests meets real usage for the first time at go-live. possibly

Upside, and how big The running business is untouched until the switch, and all the risk lands at one rehearsed gate instead of trickling through production for months.

Worst case A regressed fix at go-live — real, but engineerable down to unlikely, and reversible by re-applying the fix.

Refactor the live component in place Rejected

Edit the running component to point at the new model, before cutover.

  • Every edit is a production change to the live business before cutover — risk lands early and continuously, not at one gate. almost certain
  • Editing logic that can't even be read (the package is a black box) on a system the whole operation runs on. almost certain
  • Nothing can be built ahead safely — the dormant-then-switch shape is forfeited entirely. almost certain

Upside, and how big No second copy to keep in step — the twin-drift problem never arises. A genuine saving, but bought at the cost of live-production exposure.

Worst case A pre-cutover production incident on the running recruitment platform, with no rehearsed gate to contain it.

The twin's downside is bigger to manage but smaller to bear. Twin drift is the single largest residual risk of building ahead — but it is a known, engineerable one, reduced to unlikely by discipline and reversible when it happens. Refactoring in place trades that for continuous, near-certain exposure on the live business, editing logic no one can read. The preference this serves is keeping the running business untouched until one rehearsed moment — which is why refactor-in-place survives only at the few designed, guarded seams, never as the default.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

This decision is the one that makes rollback possible at all. Because the default is a dormant new component and the on-switch for logic is a setting, nothing here touches the live business until the cutover — so reversing a logic activation is flipping a setting back, and the old component is still there and untouched. The structural pieces don't reverse by setting; they reverse by the cutover rollback (turning assignments back), covered under Rollback. The refactor-in-place exception is the one place a change lands on a live service — which is exactly why it's held to the few designed, guarded seams and never used as the default.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Twin drift is the standing residual risk of building ahead — controlled by the twin register, the same-change rule, and the tightening change freeze, but never zero.
  • The switchable-service groundwork is less done than it looks. Only the placement service is genuinely switch-ready; the interview one and others need real work, and each setting-flip assumes a native target object and configuration that don't exist yet — a build task hiding behind every flip.
  • Hidden package reactions. Whether the package quietly reacts when a record changes is unknowable from the outside — it gates the refactor-in-place seams and the first slice, and is settled by the sandbox behavioural probe (see Rehearsal).
  • Same-entry-point routing isn't inventoried yet. The trigger/handler case is named and the mechanism chosen, but the full census — which triggers, Platform Event subscribers, queueables/batches and outbound listeners need a routing switch versus which are covered by native activation alone — hasn't been done. Until it is, assume any entry point on a non-migrating object that currently calls package-coupled logic is a candidate and check it explicitly.

Continue to Deployment for how the switched-off build reaches production, or jump to Activation & cutover for how each capability crosses. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit; counts are point-in-time.

Decision 7 · Deployment

How the switched-off build reaches production

Deploy the new system piece by piece as it's written — or hold it all back for one big deployment at cutover? A build-time decision, distinct from switching it on.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

Deployment is not activation. Deployment is how the new, dormant metadata physically gets into production. Activation — flipping it live, moving the data, re-pointing callers — is a separate decision on its own page (Activation & cutover). The plan does both differently: it deploys progressively over months, and activates the core big-bang in one weekend. Keeping the two apart is what avoids the trap of thinking "gradual" has to mean one thing.

What we chose. Deploy each piece of the new build into production, switched off, on the ordinary day it's finished — not held back for one large deployment at cutover — with timing keyed to how fast each component changes: stable pieces early, volatile ones late, so no twin waits longer than it must. This depends on one hard precondition: every deployed-but-not-live component must have a provable "off" state.

The distinction to hold onto

Two different "gradually"s

The word "progressive" gets used for two unrelated things in this programme, and conflating them causes real confusion. This page is about only the first.

Progressive deployment This page

The dormant metadata arrives in production a bit at a time, as it's built. Nothing is live; the business sees nothing. A build-time logistics choice about how new, switched-off code lands in the org.

Progressive activation Cutover page

Capabilities go live one at a time (slice) rather than all at once (big-bang). A go-live choice about the order the business actually crosses over. Covered under Activation & cutover.

The actual plan combines them

The build is deployed progressively (this page) and, for the core, activated big-bang (the cutover page). You can deploy every piece gradually over months and still switch the core on in a single weekend — the two decisions are independent, and reading them as one is exactly the confusion to avoid.

Alternatives considered

Deploy as you build, or hold for one deployment

Deploy each piece dormant, as it's finishedChosen

Every component lands in production on an ordinary day, switched off. By the cutover, the entire build is already present in production and proven to deploy — only the switch-on and the data load remain. What can go wrong: a component that isn't genuinely dormant — one active automation, one assigned permission — touches the live business before anyone intended. That risk is real, and it's why the precondition below is a hard gate, not a nicety.

Hold the whole build for one deployment at cutoverRejected

Deploying a large body of new metadata can fail in ways a sandbox never shows — production-only configuration, install order, platform limits reached only with the real schema. Holding it all for cutover puts the single riskiest step — pushing new code — back inside the very window the whole approach exists to keep clear. If it fails there, the cutover fails with it.

Why the chosen option wins: deploying dormant on an ordinary day means a failed deployment costs nothing — no cutover is at stake, it's fixed and redeployed. Sandbox rehearsal still runs throughout, but it proves the procedure; production-dormant deployment proves the deployment itself against the one org that counts. By cutover, "will it deploy?" is already answered.

When, not just whether

Deploy each twin as late as it safely can be — because the business keeps changing

"Progressive" answers how the dormant build lands; it leaves open when each piece should. That timing is not a detail — it is the main lever on the plan's biggest running cost. A dormant twin only has to be kept in step with its live original for as long as both exist; the longer a twin sits waiting, the more live changes it must absorb to avoid drift. And Marlex is still delivering to the business every sprint, so the live originals genuinely keep moving.

The rule that follows is to key each component's deployment timing to how fast it changes, not to deploy everything as early as possible:

Stable components — deploy early

A component the business rarely touches can go dormant months ahead at almost no drift cost. Deploying it early banks the "will it deploy?" proof cheaply, because there is little live change to mirror while it waits.

Hot components — deploy late

A component under active weekly delivery should be duplicated last, so its twin lives for weeks, not months. Every week a hot twin exists is a week of changes that must be applied twice — so the shorter its life, the smaller the drift tax it levies.

This keeps every twin's dual-maintenance window as short as its change-rate allows, without pulling any deployment risk back into the cutover weekend — the stable majority is still proven early, and only the genuinely volatile pieces wait. It is the deployment-side half of controlling twin drift; the intake rule (new work built native from the start, on Rebuild approach) is the other half, keeping the count of things needing a twin from growing in the first place.

The window is a target, confirmed by rehearsal — not a fixed date

Because the dual-maintenance cost scales with how long coexistence lasts, there is real pressure to make the overall rebuild window short. But how short it can safely be is set by two measured facts, not by will: the dress-rehearsal wall-clock, and how much the delivery team can build twice while still shipping to the business. The plan states a target window and lets those facts confirm or adjust it, rather than committing to a date the drift tax then overruns.

The precondition

Dormancy has to be provable, not assumed

Progressive dormant deployment is safe only on one condition, and it's a strict one: every deployed-but-not-yet-live component must have a demonstrable "off" state — objects empty, automation inactive, screens and permissions unassigned, settings defaulted off. This is why it depends directly on the activation discipline from Rebuild approach: logic sits behind default-off settings, structure sits unassigned until cutover. Dormancy is safe precisely because each component's inert state can be shown — a settings check, an assignment check — not taken on trust.

A dependency, not an assumption

Progressive dormant deployment assumes Marlex has a repeatable way to deploy to production regularly (a deployment pipeline rather than occasional manual change sets), and that there are no package or change-freeze constraints that would block a steady cadence. These are dependencies to confirm with Marlex IT, not things the strategy can assert on its own.

How the options compare

Deploy-as-you-build versus hold for one deployment

Both options eventually get the same dormant build into production. The difference is when a deployment failure can happen — and, decisively, where it lands if it does. That "where" is what a flat pros-and-cons list would miss.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Deploy each piece dormant, as it's finished Chosen

Every component lands in production switched off, on the ordinary day it's built.

  • A deployment fails on an ordinary day — fixed and redeployed with no cutover at stake. possibly
  • A component isn't genuinely "off" and touches the live business early. unlikely
  • By cutover, "will it deploy?" is already answered — only switch-on and data load remain. almost certain

Upside, and how big The single riskiest mechanical step — pushing new code — is proven against the real org, on days when a failure costs nothing.

Worst case An accidental early activation reaching live users — the provable-off gate and the post-deploy write-probe exist to catch it first.

Hold the whole build for one deployment Rejected

Push all the new metadata at cutover, in one large deployment.

  • A large deployment fails on production-only config, install order, or a platform limit reached only at real scale. possibly
  • If a failure does happen, it lands inside the cutover window — and the cutover fails with it, under time pressure. almost certain

Upside, and how big One deployment event to manage instead of many — a coordination convenience, and little else.

Worst case A failed deploy takes the cutover down with it, under time pressure, with the business frozen and waiting.

Same failure, wildly different cost — and that's the whole decision. A deploy failure is only possibly going to happen either way; what differs is that deploy-as-you-build absorbs it on a throwaway day, while hold-for-one-deploy detonates it inside the frozen cutover window. The preference this serves is keeping the cutover window clear of anything that can be proven earlier — and a deployment is exactly that. The convenience of one big push isn't worth putting the riskiest step where a failure is most expensive.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Progressive deployment is the easiest thing here to reverse, precisely because nothing it deploys is live. A dormant component that misbehaves is deactivated or removed like any ordinary production change, on an ordinary day, with no cutover at stake — the opposite of a one-shot deployment, where a failure surfaces only under cutover pressure with no time to recover. The one thing to guard is a component that turns out not to be genuinely dormant; the provable-off-state check exists to catch exactly that before it reaches production, and a write-probe after each deploy confirms it.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • A "dormant" component that isn't. The whole safety of the approach rests on the off-state being real — controlled by the provable-off-state gate and a post-deploy write-probe, but it's the risk to keep watching.
  • The deployment cadence depends on Marlex's pipeline. Confirm with Marlex IT: a repeatable production deploy pipeline, sandbox refresh cadence, and any package-interaction or change-freeze windows that would constrain a steady progressive cadence.
  • Change-rate keying needs a live read of the backlog. Deploying hot components late assumes we know which components are hot — which means watching the delivery backlog throughout, not deciding the order once. The volatile set shifts as the business's priorities do.

Continue to Activation & cutover for how the deployed-dormant build is actually switched on. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit.

Decision 8 · Activation & cutover

How each capability crosses from old to new

Slice a capability off early, big-bang the shared core, or keep both systems live and synced? And what has to re-point to the new model on the day.

Grounded in live production write volumes · 12 July 2026 · pre-load approach adopted 15 July 2026

The decision. Activation is the act of switching a capability from old to new in production — distinct from deployment, which only gets the dormant build into the org. There are three ways a capability can cross: slice it off early on its own, big-bang it with the shared core in one weekend, or keep both models live and synced while it moves piece by piece.

What we chose. Big-bang the shared core (candidate, job, application) in one rehearsed weekend, with placements migrating alongside it by default; slice the edges (interviews first) off ahead of it; never keep two copies synced. The bulk of the core data is pre-loaded into the dormant objects weeks ahead and refreshed one-way, so the weekend moves only the delta — a one-way copy, not the two-way mirror ruled out below. Both systems do run side by side for months — but by single ownership, never by syncing duplicate copies.

The routing question

One question assigns each capability its route

One question decides a capability's route: can it be isolated from the shared core and the candidate? If yes, it slices off early on its own. If no, it rides the core's single big-bang — placements included. A third approach, run-off, is held in reserve for one situation only (below).

Slice

A self-contained capability moves early, on its own — a clean handover of ownership, not a sync. Its history migrates once, the new system takes over, and it writes one-way into whatever hasn't moved yet. Small, easy to rehearse, and if it goes wrong the damage is contained.

Big-bang

The shared core — and, by default, placements — switches over in one go, in a single planned weekend rehearsed twice first. The bulk of the data is already pre-loaded into the dormant objects, so the weekend moves only the delta and flips. One cutover, one reporting model, no parallel-operations tail.

Run-off (fallback)

New records native from day one; existing ones finish in TargetRecruit and the old system empties over time. Held in reserve for placements only if migrating them live proves unreliable — it trades a harder weekend migration for an ongoing parallel-operations and split-reporting cost.

The alternative that had to be tested

Two very different things get called "moving gradually"

The obvious alternative to a big cutover of the core is to keep both systems live and continuously synchronised, moving the core one piece at a time. It was dismissed early as "two sources of truth" — by assertion, without analysis. But the word "gradually" hides two designs with opposite feasibility. Both keep the systems live in parallel; only one needs syncing.

Progressive by ownership Adopted

Each record has exactly one owner. The moved capability owns its data outright and only ever reads one-way from what hasn't moved. Both systems run side by side for months — but there is never a second copy of a record to reconcile. This needs no sync, and it's how interviews and the edges already move — and how placements would move too, if the gradual fallback were ever taken.

Progressive by mirroring Ruled out

Both systems hold their own copy of the same records, kept in step by two-way sync. This is the "two sources of truth" case — and the only place the core would need it, because the same job, application or candidate is touched by moved and not-yet-moved capabilities at once. It is exactly where the numbers say it fails.

So the real question is narrow

The whole programme is progressive except the shared core — the candidate, job and application backbone the rest of the system hangs off. The only genuine either/or is how that core crosses: big-bang it, or mirror-sync it. Progressive-by-ownership is feasible and adopted everywhere else; mirror-sync is the one thing that had to be tested — object by object, against real volumes.

Testing the sync alternative

Where sync is viable, and where it's fatal

Each shared object was analysed against live volumes and the risk of writing into a black-box package object. The verdicts:

Sync feasibility per shared object (live prod, 12 July 2026)
Object Records Verdict Why
Candidate 1.09M No sync needed One physical Contact carrying both field sets — never copied, so nothing to sync. New logic reads the fields it needs on the one record.
Job / process 57K Not viable Read by every domain; both models would write it → two-way sync, loops, and it fires package logic on every write.
Application 1.4M Not viable Highest volume; tens of thousands of inbound job-board creates a week; a mirror blows processing limits and can't tolerate the lag.
Placement 46K No sync Migrates with the core by default (only ~9,400 live). If the run-off fallback is used instead, still no two-way sync — a one-way, read-only feed into reporting covers the bridge.
Interview 124K Viable-live Low-volume leaf — a live sync would be safe, but the slice moves fine by ownership, so no sync is built.

Two live findings kill any two-way mirror of the core before principle even enters. The candidate pool is rewritten wholesale by a background job every one to two weeks — a single automated system account modified over a million of the ~1.06M candidate records (of ~1.09M Contacts in total) in seven days, versus a few hundred each for real recruiters; a change-triggered sync would fire on hundreds of thousands of records inside that window, blow processing limits, and trigger the package's hidden logic unpredictably. And applications turn over faster than any sync can keep up cleanly — about 78K created and 188K modified a month, the job board alone creating ~27K a week; a just-applied candidate would be invisible in whichever system didn't yet own them for the lag window, a live operational failure for a staffing desk.

A second, volume-independent blocker

Even setting volume aside, five child objects are locked to a core object by a tight relationship that allows only one parent. Two live copies of a core record couldn't both keep these children valid — a structural impossibility that kills core mirroring on its own. Either ground alone closes it; together they close it firmly.

The one-line finding

No object needs a two-way sync. The two that could sync safely (the candidate as a field-map, interviews) don't need it; the two that would need it (job, application) can't survive it. The progressive route is real — but delivered by ownership, one-way writes, and field-mapping on one record, which is exactly what the strategy already does.

The pre-load is not this — it's a one-way copy, not a two-way mirror

Before the weekend, the bulk of the core data is loaded into the new objects while they sit switched off, and refreshed one-way as the old data changes (see Data migration). That is not the mirroring ruled out above. Mirroring keeps two live copies of every record in step with two-way sync while both are being operated on — the fatal case. The pre-load has only one live copy — the old system, which stays the single source of truth — and a dormant copy nobody touches, refreshed from it. There is no second writer, no write-back into the old objects, and nothing to reconcile between two authorities. It shrinks the cutover weekend to a delta without reintroducing the thing the numbers killed.

For the developersContact 1.09M, ~1.05M modified in 7 days by one TR system user (mass reindex/enrichment batch, ~1–2 wk cycle; every Contact shows last-modified ≥ 2025, the fingerprint of a recurring bulk process). AVTRRT__Job_Applicant__c 1.4M, 78K created + 188K modified/30d, InfoJobs guest user 27K creates/wk. The candidate "sync" is a before-save field-map on one record (allocation-free; Contact already carries 8 active triggers) — the data move treats it as an in-place UPDATE, not a load into a twin. Any one-way projection keys on an MA_TR_Id__c external-id upsert with an origin marker and never writes into an AVTRRT__ object. The five non-reparentable master-detail children: two on Job, two on Job Applicant, one on Placement.

How ownership works in practice

Single ownership is the rule that keeps both systems live safely

For the months between the first slice and the final cutover, both systems are live at once. Single ownership — one system per record, never two — is what keeps that safe: when a capability moves, the new system becomes the sole home of its records and the old system no longer holds a copy. In practice:

Take interviews (the first slice). Once they move, interviews live only in the new system. But an interview outcome still needs to update the candidate — who, until the core moves later, still lives in TargetRecruit. So the new interview writes that update one way, into TargetRecruit. What it never does is the reverse: TargetRecruit doesn't write back into the new interview, and no process keeps a second interview copy in step. One owner per record, updates flowing one direction into whatever hasn't moved yet — not two copies of the same record kept synchronised.

The flip itself, for a kept object's trigger

Where an object keeps its trigger, cutover is a configuration flip — not a redeploy

Most of the new logic lands on new objects, so its cutover is simply the data move above. There is one concrete exception, and it is specifically about Apex triggers: a handful of objects keep their identity — they are Marlex's own, not part of the vendor package, so they stay — and the trigger on them stays valid because it still fires on the right object, but the logic that trigger delegates to is what's rebuilt on the new model. For those triggers, the switch from old logic to new is a distinct cutover action, and it was designed to be as small and reversible as a setting change.

A trigger's name can only exist once on an object, so there can't be a separate "new" trigger deployed next to the old one — both the old and the new logic have to sit behind the same trigger, which reads a configuration switch to decide which one runs, never both (see how the rebuild is built for why one trigger routed by configuration is the only safe design). So on the day, cutting one of these objects over means flipping that switch — a configuration change, not a deployment — with the new logic already deployed and dormant behind it. Three properties matter for the weekend:

  • One switch per object. Each kept object's trigger is cut over — and rolled back — on its own, so this never becomes an all-or-nothing flip across every one at once. It lines up with the per-capability routing the rest of this page uses.
  • The flip is not the whole cutover.
