Flipping to the new logic doesn't by itself silence the old vendor automation on a package object; where that applies, deactivating the old automation is a separate, deliberate step. For the objects in question here — Marlex's own, not the vendor's — there is no package automation to silence, so the flip is clean.
  • You can see which side is live. The switch is worth nothing if no one can confirm which logic actually ran; a log signal records the active side for every transaction, so "which path is live" is verifiable, not assumed.
Scheduled and batch jobs cut over differently

The other kind of TR-coupled logic — the roughly fifteen scheduled and batch jobs that read the old model (placement schedulers, candidate batches, the job-board feeds) — does not need this switch. A scheduled or batch job has a native on/off state, so its cutover is the ordinary one this page already describes: deploy the new version inactive, and swap the schedule from old to new as part of the cutover runbook. A configuration switch is only worth adding to one of these if it needs instant rollback beyond stopping the schedule. The job-board and outbound feeds are a separate matter again — they move behind the integration layer and re-point to the new model, covered under Integrations & middleware, not here.

Worked examples

Five capabilities, five ways the routes land

Together these span every dimension of the system — synchronous and asynchronous logic, code and configuration, data model, security, screens, integrations and analytics.

Interviews Slice

All the interview automation is already Marlex's own code, and a partial translation layer is already built. So: create the new interview object dormant, flip the switch to point automation at it, move the small amount of interview history, done — the first proof the whole approach works, at minimal risk.

Depends on one cheap test first: confirming in a sandbox that no hidden package logic quietly reacts when an interview record changes. And the existing interview translation layer is a stub bound to the package object — it needs an object-agnostic refactor before the switch works.

Placements & back office Big-bang (run-off fallback)

Placements move with the core by default: the roughly nine thousand live placements migrate in the cutover weekend along with everything else, and the new system owns placements from go-live. Invoicing already runs on Marlex's own objects, so finance keeps using one system the whole time. This keeps the plan to a single cutover and one reporting model.

Only if the rehearsal shows those live, mid-lifecycle placements can't be migrated reliably in the window does the run-off fallback apply: new placements start native, the ones already running finish out in TargetRecruit, and the old system empties over time — at the cost of running both in parallel and splitting placement reporting until it completes.

Either way, seasonal placements that pause and restart never truly end, so they are migrated in one planned batch during a quiet period rather than left to wind down.

Candidates, processes & applications Big-bang

The shared core everything depends on. It can't be sliced (it runs through the same candidate record) and can't wind down gradually (the same candidate is active in old and new processes at once). So: build the new model dormant months ahead, pre-load the data into it weeks ahead and keep it refreshed one-way, rehearse the delta-and-flip twice, then switch everything together over one weekend — data model, screens, permissions, portal, job-board connections.

The candidate stays on Contact, under a new Marlex record type built dormant. The candidate's field data is back-filled into the new fields during the pre-load — quietly, on records that keep their old record type — so on the day only the changed fields and the record-type and page assignments flip. Candidates keep being Contacts, with standard relationships intact.

Users get the new access, and keep the old. The switch grants each recruiter their persona-based permission sets on the native model — but deliberately leaves their TargetRecruit license and permissions on the old AVTRRT__ objects in place, because the old system is the dated fallback and a rollback needs them able to work in it. Reclaiming the TargetRecruit licenses and stripping the old object permissions is a separate teardown, gated by the fallback expiry, not done at go-live (see Operating the new system).

Candidate data stored in TargetRecruit's own fields must be moved out before the package is removed — uninstalling deletes it.

Job-board integration (InfoJobs) Rides the core cutover

Publishing a job out to InfoJobs goes through the switchable service, so InfoJobs never knows which system owns the job — that part can move early. Candidates coming back in from InfoJobs land wherever applications live, so that side switches over with the core. InfoJobs sees no change either way.

Reporting & analytics Rides the core cutover

Any report built on a package object has to be rebuilt from scratch — Salesforce won't let you re-point a report at a different object. The new reports are built ahead of time against the empty new model and switched on at cutover, and the business decides which of the 53 affected reports are still worth rebuilding. If the placement run-off fallback is ever taken, it adds a real reporting cost here — while both systems are live, placement figures split across the two, so for the few numbers that matter a defined way to add them together would cover that period. Migrating placements with the core (the default) avoids that split entirely.

The step that's easy to forget

Re-pointing every external caller

At the cutover, every outside system that talks to Salesforce must be switched to the new model — and these split into two very different kinds.

Through Marlex-owned endpoints Easy

The connectors that go through a Marlex-owned service — the ERP client, the job-board publication service — keep their address; we change what they touch underneath. The outside party sees no change. This is the translation layer earning its cost.

Direct against the standard API The dangerous ones

Systems that call Salesforce's standard API directly against the old object and field names — data loaders, middleware, reporting and ETL tools, the customer data platform's ingestion, partner integrations. For these the object name is the contract; there is no seam on our side. When the old object goes away they break or silently write to a dead object.

A cross-team dependency with real lead time

A direct-API caller that must re-point has to be re-pointed on its own side, in step with cutover — a cross-team dependency, not a switch we control. Finding these callers takes the platform's own API and connected-app usage records, because nothing in the build itself reveals them. That census has now been done (see below); the one caller it surfaced is coordinated with its owner ahead of the window.

There are three ways to make a direct caller re-pointable, best first, and the choice per caller shapes both the cutover and its rollback:

Route it through the platform

Best, where the timing allows. If the caller is moved behind the integration platform before cutover, the switch to the new objects happens inside the platform's own layer — a change Marlex controls, in one place, without touching the outside system. Rollback is equally in-hand: point that layer back at the old objects.

Give it a Marlex-owned endpoint

Second choice. Build a small custom entry point (a REST endpoint) that the caller targets instead of the raw standard object, so the object swap happens behind an address Marlex owns — the same seam trick the ERP and job-board connectors already use, extended to a caller that didn't have one.

Map it and re-point on its side

The fallback when neither is ready in time. At minimum, every direct caller is known and its re-point is coordinated with its owner. The rollback path here is explicit and must be written down: to reverse, those systems are pointed back at the legacy objects and their APIs — so the old endpoints have to stay answerable until each caller is confirmed migrated.

Status: the inbound surface has now been measured — and the news is good

The census that used to be the open unknown here has been done properly, from the live platform's own usage records. Those records go back six months, and a sample spanning that window — nearly nine hundred thousand individual calls — was read to find every caller that touches the objects being retired. The result is reassuring and specific: exactly one outside system writes to the old model — the job-board application feed. Everything else that runs today — the ERP link in both directions, the e-signature service, the training-certificate feed, the company-data lookup, reporting — already works against the new model and carries through the cutover untouched. The recurring bulk load of around a million contacts writes to a standard object, not the retiring one, so it too is unaffected.

Two smaller consumers only read the old model rather than write it. One is a sourcing tool whose query must be re-pointed to the new objects or it returns nothing after the cutover — a small, coordinated change with its vendor, not a blocker. The other merely inspects the data model's shape to merge documents and will simply see the new objects once they exist. So the dangerous category above shrinks to a single write-coupled feed; the rest is one read re-point and a short list of dormant or in-development connections whose owner and status Marlex confirms before the window.

How the options compare

Big-bang versus mirror-sync, outcome by outcome

The prose above says mirror-sync fails; this makes the comparison explicit. For the one genuine either/or — how the shared core crosses — here are the reasonable outcomes of each option and how likely each is, with the size of what's at stake. A plain list of pros and cons would treat a minor inconvenience and a fatal flaw as one tick each; what matters is whether the upside is worth risking the downside, at their real sizes.

rarely — under 10%
unlikely — 10–30%
possibly — 30–50%
more likely than not — 50–70%
probably — 70–90%
almost certain — over 90%

These likelihoods are educated guesses, not measured facts — attaching an honest estimate to each branch beats refusing to guess. Where a branch rests on a counted figure it is marked measured.

Big-bang the core Chosen

One rehearsed weekend; the bulk pre-loaded ahead, the old system held intact underneath.

  • A clean weekend — the delta fits the window easily, validation passes, the switch flips. probably
  • The delta is bigger than expected and is re-attempted next window (a lost weekend, not lost data). unlikely
  • A missed structural item (a permission, a scheduled job) surfaces on go-live morning. unlikely
  • A transform bug reaches the new model and is caught late, past the cheap-rollback window. unlikely

Upside, and how big One cutover, one reporting model, no parallel-operations tail — and a real, dated fallback the whole time.

Worst case A wasted rehearsed weekend, or a bounded rollback replaying the records created since the switch. Survivable.

Mirror-sync the core Rejected

Two live copies of every core record, kept in step by two-way sync.

  • The candidate batch that rewrites ~1M records every 1–2 weeks (measured) re-fires the sync on hundreds of thousands of records and blows processing limits. almost certain
  • A just-applied candidate is invisible in whichever copy doesn't yet own them for the sync lag — a live failure for a staffing desk. probably
  • Five child objects locked to one parent (measured) can't be held valid across two copies — a structural impossibility. almost certain
  • The copies drift and there is no single trustworthy copy to return to. almost certain

Upside, and how big A gentler UI transition for users — a real but modest gain, and one the adopted route already delivers at the edges without sync.

Worst case A forensic un-merge of a million-plus live records with no known-good state — effectively unrecoverable.

The comparison isn't close, and it isn't a matter of taste. Big-bang's worst outcomes are unlikely and survivable; mirror-sync's worst outcomes are near-certain and unrecoverable, and two of them rest on counted production facts (the 1M-record batch, the five non-reparentable children), not judgement. The preference this serves is operational continuity with a real way back — and only one option offers both. Progressive-by-ownership captures the transition gain mirror-sync was reaching for, without the copies to reconcile.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Rollback is different for each route, which is why it has its own page — but in short: a slice reverses small and contained (one leaf object, little data); the big-bang reverses against the old system held intact underneath, free before the go/no-go gate and bounded after it to replaying the records created since the switch; and the rejected sync route had no clean way back at all — which is the single strongest reason it was rejected. The route you pick is the rollback you get.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • A missed reader after an ownership flip. A report, flow or integration still pointed at the old object silently consumes stale data — controlled by a pre-flip sweep of every remaining reader, in-org and external, before each flip.
  • A misclassified edge. A capability believed sliceable turns out to be core-coupled (the talent-pool lesson — component count is a decoy). Routed by the objects a capability reads and writes, not by its name or size.
  • Accidental bidirectionality. A future change adds a "helpful" write-back into a package object, silently recreating the mirror that was rejected — blocked by a standing code-review rule: nothing writes into a package object as a sync measure.
  • Open: is migrating the ~9,400 live placements in the weekend realistic, or is the run-off the safer default? The rehearsal decides this. The external-caller list has now been measured — one caller writes the old model and re-points, one read-only tool needs its query re-pointed; what remains is for Marlex to confirm the owner and status of a few dormant connections before the window.

Continue to The freeze for the pause that makes the delta clean, or Rollback for how each route fails back. Counts are live production as of 12 July 2026 and will drift.

Decision 9 · The freeze

The pause that makes the last of the data move clean

A full write-freeze on the package objects — everything that writes them stops — timed to open right after the background job that rewrites the entire candidate pool, so the delta left to move is as small as possible.

Grounded in live production write volumes · 12 July 2026 · pre-load approach adopted 15 July 2026

The decision. Reconciling the last of the data cleanly needs a moment when the old system stops changing — a freeze. The question isn't whether to freeze (a moving source can't be reconciled reliably) but what it has to stop and when it should open. A source is "moving" if anything is still writing it, so the freeze has to stop every writer at once — not just one loud job.

What we chose. A full write-freeze on the package objects — automation, scheduled jobs, inbound integrations, and recruiter edits all stopped — opened immediately after the background job that rewrites the whole candidate pool finishes its cycle, so the delta the freeze has to move is as small as possible. The batch sets when the freeze opens; the freeze itself covers all four write sources. The freeze is what begins the reversible window; nothing irreversible happens until after it.

What the freeze is now for. The bulk of the data has already crossed — the full load ran into the dormant new objects weeks earlier and was refreshed one-way as the old data changed (see Data migration). So the freeze no longer covers a ten-year move; it covers the delta — whatever changed since the last refresh — plus the switch. That makes the window shorter and its timing-after-the-batch argument sharper, because a smaller starting delta is now the whole point.

What the freeze stops: all four write sources at once — scheduled and background jobs, triggered automation, inbound integrations, and recruiter edits. Each is detailed below; the candidate batch is the loudest of them and drives the timing.

Why the batch drives the timing

A background job rewrites the entire candidate pool

Of the four writers, one dictates when the freeze can open cleanly. The live org has a rhythm the freeze has to respect: a single automated system account was measured modifying over a million of the ~1.06M candidate records in seven days — the fingerprint of a background job (a search re-index or enrichment pass) that rewrites essentially the whole candidate pool every one to two weeks. Real recruiters, by comparison, touch a few hundred records each.

The delta the freeze has to move is exactly "what changed since the last pre-load refresh." If the freeze opened during that batch, the batch would have just rewritten essentially the whole candidate pool — so almost every candidate would count as changed, and the delta would explode from a small predictable set into another near-full move. That would stretch the window and make the validation checks fail for the wrong reason. So the freeze is scheduled to open just after a batch cycle completes — and, ideally, just after a final pre-load refresh — when the pool is momentarily stable and the delta is at its smallest. This is a timing argument, not a scope one: whenever the freeze opens, it stops all four sources — the batch simply picks the cleanest moment to start.

For the developersThe batch is a TR system/integration user ("Admin Tgt") that modified ~1.05M of the ~1.06M candidate-record-type Contacts in a 7-day window — including a ~600k spike in a single ~3-day stretch (the Contact object holds ~1.09M rows in total). Every Contact shows last-modified ≥ 2025 — no record is older — confirming a recurring bulk rewrite and making LastModifiedDate useless as a delta key (it flags 100% of the pool as "fresh"). Ask TR support whether the batch is suspendable for the freeze weekend: if yes, suspend it; if no, time the freeze to open right after a completed cycle. Placement carries a similar signature — a nightly state-machine cascade touches ≈ the whole placement base every 30 days. The freeze list is generated from the component map: every scheduled/async job, every trigger and record-triggered flow on a package object, every inbound endpoint (job-board webhook, ERP, other OAuth callers), and the read-only reshuffle for recruiters — one enumerated set, not four people's memories.

What "freeze" really means

Every writer stops, or it isn't a freeze

A freeze that only stops people typing isn't a freeze. Four kinds of writer have to go quiet, and each is a distinct, verified action — miss any one and the source is still moving:

People

Recruiters are put into read-only on the package objects. They can look, but not change — for the length of the window.

Automation

The triggers and record-triggered flows that fire on package objects are deactivated, so a save elsewhere can't cascade a write into the data being migrated.

Scheduled & async jobs

Every scheduled or background job that reads or writes a package object is suspended — and this is the sharp one: a job left running would not just dirty the data, it would throw the instant the package is later removed. These jobs are suspended now and re-pointed after the go/no-go gate passes — not merely paused, but not re-pointed so early that lifting the freeze couldn't restore them exactly as they were.

Inbound integrations

The outside systems that write into the old objects are held or re-pointed: the job-board feed pushing about 27,000 candidate records a week, ERP traffic, and any other tool hitting the standard API against the old object names. A live inbound write during the window is just as corrupting as a batch that never stopped.

The freeze is also the moment external callers get cut over

Stopping inbound writes and cutting them over are two halves of the same act: the same window is when those outside systems — the inbound job-board feed, and any tool that hits the standard API directly against the old object names — are re-pointed to the new model. That re-pointing is covered under Activation & cutover; the freeze is what makes it safe to do, because nothing is writing the old objects while the switch happens.

Where the freeze sits

The freeze opens the reversible window

The freeze isn't the risky part — it's the opposite. Everything from the freeze up to the go/no-go gate is fully reversible: the old system is frozen but completely intact, so if anything looks wrong the freeze simply lifts and the old system carries on exactly as before. The irreversible step is the activation after the gate, not the freeze before it. This is why the freeze is described as opening the reversible window rather than closing anything down for good.

The freeze also has to be short, because it stops the business — all of it, since all four writers are held. Keeping it short is done by moving everything movable in advance: not just the decidable prep — the data cleanup, the field map, the archive calls — but the bulk of the data itself, pre-loaded into the dormant objects weeks earlier and refreshed one-way. So inside the window only the unavoidable work runs: move the small delta, validate, flip. That preparation discipline — the pre-load, the delta and why it isn't the ruled-out two-way sync — lives on the Data migration page.

How the options compare

Timing the freeze after the batch versus not

Freezing is not in question, and neither is its scope — all four writers stop regardless. The real decision is when the freeze opens relative to the background job that rewrites the whole candidate pool. It looks like a scheduling detail; the outcomes show it isn't.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Open the freeze right after a batch cycle Chosen

Wait for the ~1M-record rewrite to finish, then freeze all four writers into the quiet gap — with the bulk already pre-loaded.

  • The delta is at its smallest — the pre-loaded copy is current and only a handful of records changed since the last refresh. almost certain
  • The validation checks pass on their merits, so the go/no-go decision is a real one. probably

Upside, and how big A trustworthy go/no-go gate — the difference between proving the move worked and guessing. Costs only a scheduling choice.

Worst case The batch cycle is mis-timed and the freeze waits a little for the next quiet gap — a delay, not a failure.

Freeze on a fixed date, unaligned Rejected

Pick a calendar slot without regard to the batch rhythm.

  • The freeze opens mid-batch, with ~1M candidates just rewritten (measured, every 1–2 weeks). probably
  • Almost every candidate now counts as "changed since the last refresh", so the delta balloons into another near-full move. probably
  • The window stretches and parity checks fail for the wrong reason — a false no-go that wastes it when nothing was broken. probably

Upside, and how big A date the business can fix without waiting on batch timing — a convenience with no safety benefit.

Worst case A wasted cutover weekend and a validation failure no one can distinguish from a real one.

The chosen option's downside is a short wait; the alternative's is a wasted weekend. Both options stop the same four writers — the difference is purely when the window opens. The batch rhythm is a measured production fact — a single system account rewriting essentially the whole candidate pool every one to two weeks — so freezing without regard to it is not a neutral choice, it is a probable delta-explosion. The preference this serves is a freeze that starts clean, so the go/no-go gate means what it says. Better still if the batch proves suspendable for the weekend — an open question to the package vendor.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

The freeze is the most reversible step in the whole cutover: lifting it is the rollback. Reactivate the automation, un-suspend the jobs, restore the inbound feeds, take recruiters out of read-only, and the old system is running again exactly as it was — because nothing irreversible has happened yet. The only way the freeze itself causes lasting harm is if one of the four writers was missed and kept writing during the window (dirtying the data) or a job was left pointed at a package object (so it throws at uninstall) — which is why the suspend-and-re-point step is enumerated from the component map and verified, not assumed.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • A missed writer. One un-suspended job, un-deactivated automation, or un-held inbound feed writing during the freeze corrupts the delta — controlled by generating the full suspend/re-point list (all four sources) from the component map rather than remembering it, and verifying with a write-probe after the freeze.
  • Batch timing. The freeze date depends on knowing the background batch's cycle — and, better, on whether TR support will confirm the batch can be suspended for the weekend. Both are open questions to close before the window is set.
  • Freeze length. How long the window actually needs to be comes only from the full-copy rehearsal's measured wall-clock (see Rehearsal) — until then it can't be committed to the business.

Continue to Data migration for the pre-load that empties the window down to a delta, and what actually moves inside it. Counts are live production as of 12 July 2026 and will drift.

Decision 10 · Data migration

Ten years of data into a new model

How the data actually crosses — the bulk pre-loaded ahead of time, only the delta moving at the freeze. What migrates, what's dropped, and what's a business call.

Grounded in live production counts · 12 July 2026 · pre-load approach adopted 15 July 2026

The decision. This page decides how the data crosses: a controlled extract-transform-load, a pure in-org batch, or migrate-on-access — and when the bulk of it runs relative to the freeze. It takes as given where the candidate lives — that the candidate stays the standard Contact, which is its own decision — and works out what that means for the mechanics of moving the data.

What we chose. Extract → transform → load into the new objects — but run the full load weeks before cutover, directly in production, into the dormant (switched-off) new objects, and repeat it as a one-way refresh so the copy stays current. At the freeze, only a small delta moves — what changed on the old objects since the last refresh — then validate, gate, activate. The heavy, slow, risky part is done ahead of time and rehearsed against real data, not crammed into one weekend. Because the candidate stays on the standard Contact, its data is back-filled into new fields on the same records rather than loaded as copies into a new object — a distinction that shapes the whole move.

What this is not. This is a one-way incremental load (old → new), repeated — not the bidirectional mirroring that was tested against live volumes and ruled out. Nobody works on the dormant new objects, so there is a single source of truth (the old system) right up to cutover; the new objects are a repeatedly-refreshed copy, never a second live copy to reconcile.

Alternatives considered — how the data crosses

Pre-load then delta, and why not the others

Pre-load into the dormant objects, then move only the delta at the freezeChosen

Run the full extract → transform → load into the new (switched-off) objects weeks before cutover, in production, and refresh it one-way as the old data changes. At the freeze, only the small set of records created or changed since the last refresh — the delta — has to move, then validate and flip. The heavy millions-of-rows part is done, timed and proven ahead of the window; the window itself shrinks to a delta plus the switch. What can still go wrong: the delta watermark is chosen wrong and misses changed records (validated against business activity, not the poisoned audit date — below); a field is silently mapped to the wrong target (caught by a record-level comparison, not just counts); or the pre-loaded copy drifts from the source (it can't operate on, and is fully refreshed by, the one-way load — the source stays authoritative).

All the data movement inside one cutover windowRejected — superseded

Freeze, then extract → transform → load → validate the whole ten years of data in the one weekend. Correct in shape — it is the same pipeline — but it crams the slowest, riskiest millions-of-rows work into the single irreversible window, leaves no chance to validate against real production data ahead of time, and gives key users nothing to check until go-live day. The pre-load keeps every one of its safeguards and moves the bulk out of the window.

A pure in-org batch migrationRejected

Tempting because it reads the old and writes the new in one place — but at 1.4M applications it fights the platform's processing limits and can't be rehearsed deterministically. The transform logic belongs in a re-runnable staging layer, not inside the load.

Trickle with a live two-way syncRejected

Keep two live copies aligned with a running two-way sync as the core moves piece by piece — the mirroring option tested object-by-object against real volumes and ruled out (see Activation & cutover). The pre-load is not this: it is a one-way copy nobody operates on, refreshed from a single authoritative source, so there is never a second live copy to reconcile — the very thing that made two-way sync fatal.

Lazy migrate-on-accessRejected

Move each record only when it's next touched. It never fully converges, which means the package can never be removed — blowing straight through the November-2027 support deadline and the uninstall gate.

Scope & order

What moves, and in what order

Per-object volumes and disposition (production, 12 July 2026)
Object Records Disposition
Candidate (Contact) 1,061,549 Back-fill in place
Client contacts (Contact) 33,384 Back-fill in place
Applications 1,399,329 Migrate (open) + archive (closed)
Interviews 124,153 Migrate (slice-first)
Jobs / processes 56,821 Migrate 21,380 open + archive 35,441 closed
Saved searches 59,380 Review with the business
Placements 46,345 Big-bang with core + forced seasonal wave + archive (run-off = fallback)
Employment history 466,674 Migrate
Education history 420,678 Migrate
Talent-pool membership 5,859 Migrate
Talent pools 597 Migrate
Candidate references 0 Drop — rebuild capability, migrate nothing
Deal sheets 0 Drop — rebuild capability, migrate nothing
A real scope reduction the component maps couldn't show

Two capabilities — candidate references and the deal-sheet behind placement pricing — are built on package objects that hold zero data in production. The capability is rebuilt; nothing migrates. This is only visible from the live data, not the metadata. (To confirm with the business these aren't seasonally populated.)

For the developersDependency order (parents before children): Contact back-fill (in place) → Job (open) → Job Applicant → Placement, with Employment/Educational History parallel to Job, Interview ahead of the spine (leaf), Hotbook + junction with/after Contact. Every insert carries the source record Id in an MA_* external-ID field so children re-parent by external ID and a failed multi-hour load re-runs as an idempotent upsert. The move-grain is the union of objects a shared component reads — the spine is atomic (one quick-view reads nine package objects at once).

Two kinds of move

The candidate is a back-fill; everything else is an insert

That the candidate stays on the standard Contact is decided on its own page (Keep the standard objects standard). What matters here is the consequence for the data move: it splits the work into two fundamentally different operations, and conflating them is the classic way this kind of migration goes wrong.

The candidate — an update in place Back-fill

The candidate records already exist as Contacts. Their data is copied from the package fields into new Marlex fields on the same rows, and the records are re-stamped to a new Marlex record type. Nothing is created; the sharing, ownership, activity history and attached files on each record are preserved for free — a load into a new object would orphan all of them.

Everything else — an insert Load

Jobs, applications, placements and the candidate's history lines are new, empty objects. Their records are extracted from the package, transformed, and inserted, each carrying its old-system identity so children re-parent correctly. These are true loads into empty targets, not updates.

The re-stamp is a first-class step, not a footnote — and it is a delta-time step

Re-stamping roughly 1.06 million candidate records to the new record type is itself a mass update — the person record carries active automation that must be held during it — so it is measured in rehearsal, and the risks it carries (the package possibly reacting to the record-type change; a missed screen or permission assignment) are handled on the candidate page and in the activation checklist. Because the re-stamp is what makes the new record type visible to users, it belongs at the cutover flip, not the pre-load — the pre-load back-fills the fields quietly on records that keep their old record type until the switch.

Pre-load and delta — the shape that moves the risk out of the window

Move the millions ahead of time; move only what changed at the freeze

The single change that makes the cutover window small and safe is when the bulk of the data moves. Instead of extracting, transforming and loading ten years of data inside the one irreversible weekend, the full load runs weeks ahead, in production, into the switched-off new objects, and is refreshed one-way as the old data keeps changing. By the freeze the new objects already hold essentially the whole dataset; only the delta — the records created or changed since the last refresh — has to move in the window.

The pre-load Weeks before, in prod

The full extract → transform → load into the dormant new objects, run and re-run while the old system stays live and authoritative. Because nobody operates on the new objects, each run can simply refresh them toward the current state of the old data — a one-way copy, not a negotiation between two live systems. This is where the millions of rows, the long-running batches and the platform limits are met and timed — with weeks of room, not one weekend.

The delta at the freeze In the window

Once writes stop, only the records that changed on the old objects since the last pre-load refresh are moved — a small, predictable set. Then the same validation runs, the go/no-go gate decides, and the switch flips. The freeze covers a delta plus the flip, not the full ten-year move.

This is a one-way incremental load, not the ruled-out sync

The pre-load repeats the same one-way old → new load; it never writes back into the old objects, and nobody works on the new ones, so the old system is the single source of truth until cutover. That is categorically different from the two-way mirroring tested against live volumes and rejected (see Activation & cutover), where two live copies of every record would have to be kept in step. Here there is only ever one live copy — the old one — and a repeatedly-refreshed dormant copy beside it.

How the delta is captured

  • The watermark can't be the audit date — but field history is the better signal. Every candidate shows a recent last-modified date, and the live data explains why: an ERP-sync status flag is rewritten across the pool intermittently (hundreds of thousands of writes in a burst), so the record-level "last modified" bumps without any real candidate change — flagging almost the whole pool as "changed" and making it useless as a delta key. Field history is switched on for the candidate, and it is per-field, so the delta can key on changes to the fields being migrated and ignore the churn on sync/bookkeeping fields — a genuinely small set rather than the whole pool. The catch: the platform tracks only about twenty fields, so history covers a subset of the migrated fields; the rest fall back to a keyed field-by-field comparison against the last refresh, and business-activity dates (last application, last placement) back up the whole thing. Choosing and proving the watermark per object — which fields history covers, whether its retention spans the pre-load-to-freeze gap — is a rehearsal task, not a live guess.
  • Deletes are handled explicitly. A record removed on the old side between refreshes won't appear in a "changed since" query, so the pre-load can't discover it by watermark alone. Deletes are reconciled by a keyed comparison of what exists on each side — the new objects carry each source record's identity, so a record present on the new side but gone on the old is retired in the same pass. The archival sweep that removes the never-active tail and the consent-declined candidates runs before the pre-load, so it isn't chased as a delta.
  • Candidate is a field-delta; the children are a row-delta. The candidate is a back-fill on the same Contact rows, so its pre-load is a mass field UPDATE and its delta is the fields that changed since the last refresh — no new rows. The child objects (jobs, applications, interviews, placements, history) are inserts into empty targets, so their delta is new-or-changed rows, re-parented by the same external-ID keys the full load uses.
The pre-load doubles data storage for the whole dormant window — so archive first

Holding the pre-loaded copy alongside the still-live old data means the org carries both at once, not just for the cutover weekend but for the weeks the copy is kept refreshed. The org already sits about three-quarters full; the worst-case sizing shows the do-nothing move peaking near the storage limit. That peak now lasts the length of the dormant window, so the archival sweep — the consent-declined candidates and the never-active tail — must run before the pre-load, not deferred, to keep the peak comfortably clear. Archiving first is what makes pre-loading safe under the limit; buying storage is the fallback, not the plan.

For the developersThe pre-load is the same idempotent upsert pipeline as the full load, scheduled to re-run on a cadence into the dormant objects. Each insert/upsert keys on the source record Id in an MA_* external-ID field, so a refresh is a keyed upsert (no duplicates) and children re-parent by external ID. Delta selection: Contact's SystemModstamp/LastModifiedDate are poisoned (an intermittent bulk rewrite of MA_Estado_sync_N4__c — an ERP-sync status flag — is the top churner, ~329k writes/30d), so prefer ContactHistory filtered to the migrated fields (history is on; ~5.35M rows; but only ~20 fields are tracked, so it covers a subset of the 51 — confirm the migrated fields are in the tracked set and that retention spans the pre-load window). Fields history doesn't cover fall back to a keyed field-by-field diff against the last refresh; business-activity dates (last application/placement) back-stop. Child objects: prefer SystemModstamp where a bulk load hasn't poisoned it (validate per object in rehearsal), else the keyed diff. A full keyed diff on the last refresh catches deletes and any watermark misses. Candidate delta = changed AVTRRT__* fields re-mapped to MA_* on the same rows; child delta = new/changed rows upserted on external ID. The record-type re-stamp and record-page/app assignment are held to the cutover flip (they are user-visible structure, not data). Automation stays bypassed during pre-load runs exactly as during the full load.

The candidate field map

"Fields to rebuild" is a different set from "data worth migrating"

A field can be referenced by code but hold no data (rebuild the field, migrate nothing) or hold data but never be read by Marlex code (data present — but worth keeping?). Only live population queries resolve this. The package defines 177 fields on the Contact object; 141 of them sit on the candidate specifically — not the ~60 first assumed (that was the code-referenced count). Crossing code-reference against actual population — including a 20,000-record sample for the long-text fields aggregate queries can't count — produces the real map:

The 141 package fields on the candidate, by disposition
Disposition Fields Meaning
Migrate 51 Holds real data worth keeping — the field-map set (34 code-referenced-and-populated + 14 populated-but-uncoded + 3 content-bearing long-text)
Drop 74 Empty or a system default — migrate nothing, don't rebuild the field (63 zero-population + 11 empty long-text)
Review — always 100% 15 Populated on every record → almost certainly a package-derived default, not user data — presumptive drop
Review — marginal 1 A candidate-summary field populated on under 1% — a business call

The headline: of 141 package fields on the candidate, 74 (52%) are empty or system defaults and need neither a rebuilt field nor a data move. Sizing the candidate rebuild from the field count — even the ~60 code-referenced — overstates it by about twice.

The long-text fields — corrected by sampling

Fifteen fields are long-text or rich-text that aggregate queries can't count, so a 20,000-record sample was pulled — and it overturned an earlier assumption that all résumé and summary fields should migrate on the strength of the résumé renderer's dependency. In fact the rich and parsed résumé fields, the cover letter and the AI-summary field are effectively empty and are dropped, not migrated. Only three carry meaningful content — the plain résumé text and the two education-summary fields — so the renderer's need is met by migrating one résumé field, not five. The résumé documents themselves are safe regardless: they live as standard files linked to the Contact and survive the uninstall untouched.

Data quality

The ~30% staleness is real — but it isn't duplicates

External precedent warned of ~30% stale-or-duplicate data. The live data both confirms and reframes it:

  • Exact duplicates are a rounding error. Only ~875 redundant candidate records share an identity number (~0.08%); the package's own duplicate flag marks ~0.3%. A deterministic identity-plus-email merge rule resolves them.
  • The real staleness is the never-active tail — and it exceeds 30%. 601K candidates (57%) have applied at least once; 461K (43%) have never applied to anything. That is the decisive data-quality decision, and it is a business call, not an engineering one: migrate all 1.06M, or migrate the active 601K and archive the 461K dead tail into a cold store? The permanent cost of carrying that dead tail forward is weighed below.
  • The audit date is useless as a staleness proxy. Every candidate shows a recent last-modified date — the fingerprint of a past bulk process that touched every record. Staleness must be derived from business activity (last application, last placement), never the audit field. (The created date is intact and confirms the ten-year span: ~76K candidates predate 2016.)

Cleanse and archive before the pre-load for what's decidable in advance (the duplicate merge, tagging and removing the dead tail and the consent-declined candidates, dropping the empty fields), so none of it is carried into the copy or chased as a delta; reconcile at the freeze only the small delta the frozen window itself creates. Doing the archival sweep ahead of the pre-load — not just ahead of the freeze — is what keeps both the storage peak and the irreversible window under control.

The runbook

The data runbook — the bulk before the freeze, the delta inside it

  1. Archive & cleanse — before the pre-load

    Remove the consent-declined candidates and the never-active tail, resolve the decided duplicate merges, and drop the empty and default fields, so none of it is copied or chased later. This keeps the storage peak clear for the whole dormant window (see The archive).

  2. Pre-load — the full move, weeks ahead, in prod

    Extract every object plus the 51 mapped candidate fields (including the three content-bearing long-text fields the résumé renderer reads), transform in the re-runnable staging layer, and load into the dormant new objects. Candidate = field back-fill on the same rows; everything else = insert into empty objects, parents before children, each carrying its package reference. This is where the millions of rows and the platform limits are met, with weeks of room.

  3. Refresh — one-way, repeated

    Re-run the load on a cadence so the dormant copy tracks the old data as it changes — a keyed upsert (no duplicates) plus a diff that catches deletes. This is what lets key users validate against real, current data ahead of the window, and shrinks the freeze-day delta to whatever changed since the last run.

  4. Freeze

    Stop all writes to package objects and open the reversible window — timed around the background batch, detailed on The freeze.

  5. Delta — move only what changed

    Move the records created or changed on the old objects since the last refresh — a small, predictable set — into the pre-loaded objects. The extract-transform-load is the same pipeline; only the volume is small. Package-field data must still be verified-extracted before any later uninstall (the automatic 48-hour export is a fallback you plan never to touch).

  6. Validate — the go/no-go gate, before the point of no return

    Prove success, don't assume it: row-count parity per object, zero orphaned children, a core-records spot-check (known candidates render with résumé, history, applications, placements), and field-population parity plus a sampled record-by-record comparison on the 51 migrated fields — population counts alone can't catch a field mapped to the wrong target. Because the bulk was validated during the pre-load, this pass focuses on the delta and the totals. Pass → flip the record-page, app and record-type assignments and the switches (the irreversible activation). Fail → roll back to the still-frozen, still-intact package org and re-attempt next window.

  7. Uninstall — a separate, later gate

    Not part of go-live morning. Requires zero remaining references to the package (the 632-component map is the checklist) and happens only after the new system has run in production long enough to trust, with package-field data verified-extracted first.

Placements

Placement data — migrate with the core by default

Placements move with the core big-bang by default: only the live placements need migrating on the weekend, and the live data shows that is a small set. The gradual run-off is held in reserve — taken only if the rehearsal shows those live, mid-lifecycle placements can't be migrated reliably in the window.

The 46,345 placements by lifecycle state (one record carries no state and reconciles the total)
Status Records Treatment
Finalised 33,465 Archive — terminal history
Cancelled 3,506 Archive — terminal
Active 6,590 Migrate big-bang with the core (run-off = fallback)
Seasonal-suspended 1,793 Forced wave — the suspended slice of the ~6,959 seasonal set (see below)
Warranty / pending 990 Migrate big-bang with the core (run-off = fallback)

Only ~9,400 placements are actually live; ~37K are terminal history — an archive load either way. So the big-bang only has to migrate the live ~9,400 (plus the archive), not all 46K as in-flight records — a small enough set that migrating them in the window is realistic, which is exactly why big-bang is the default and run-off only the fallback.

Seasonal placements — a forced move either way

Seasonal ("fijo discontinuo") placements cycle active-and-suspended indefinitely and never terminate. Under the big-bang they migrate with the rest; under the run-off fallback they can't wind down (they'd keep the package alive past the November-2027 deadline), so they'd need a forced migration wave at a seasonal trough regardless. Either way the ~6,959 of them are moved in one bounded operation, ideally timed to their inactive period — never left to drift.

The relationship restructure — five children, and it comes first

Records tied to a core package record by a tight relationship that can't be re-pointed break the instant the parent goes native. A full scan found five such record types (not the one first assumed): training-platform enrolments, two job-linked, two application-linked. Each must be restructured before the first native record of its parent type exists — a step required whether placements big-bang or run off. This is the most under-costed item in the placement plan, and it's also the volume-independent reason the core can't be kept in two synced copies (see Activation & cutover).

For the developersThe five non-reparentable master-detail children into spine objects: MA_Humact_Inscripcion__c→Placement, MA_Oficina_Proceso__c→Job, MA_Pregunta_Infojobs__c→Job, DISC_Test_Results__c→Job_Applicant, MA_Respuesta_candidatos__c→Job_Applicant. Each restructures to a lookup or re-parents to the native object. No dual-write, no sync job: each record's owner is fixed by when it was created. Downstream invoicing (MA_Movimiento_Factura__c, Marlex-native) takes a provider-switched generator so both origins feed one invoicing object — finance sees one system throughout.

Tooling & volume

What moves the data

  • Bulk interface for the volume — the 1.4M applications and 1.06M candidate back-fill need chunked, staged batches; the pre-load runs them as hours-long jobs weeks ahead, with room to spare, not squeezed into the freeze window. The transform (dedup, mapping, record-type, external-ID logic) lives in a re-runnable staging layer, not in the load.
  • Native automation bypassed during load — 1.4M inserts (and every refresh) must not fire 1.4M automation runs; the bypass switches already exist. Automation is re-enabled after each run.
  • Idempotent re-runs are the mechanism, not just a safety net — every insert carries the source record reference, so each pre-load refresh is a keyed upsert that never duplicates, and a load that fails partway re-runs cleanly. The same property that saves a failed hour-three load is what makes the repeated one-way refresh possible at all.

How this is proven — the full-copy rehearsal that times the pre-load and the delta and produces the measured freeze-window wall-clock — is its own decision, on the Rehearsal page.

How the options compare

Two decisions, weighed by size and likelihood

Two choices live inside the data move, and each deserves the same treatment: reasonable outcomes, how likely each is, and — the part a feature-list can't show — how big what's at stake is. The first is how the data crosses. The second, the archive-versus-migrate call on the never-active candidates, is the clearest case in the whole programme of a decision that turns entirely on payoff magnitude.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

How the data crosses — pre-load then delta versus the alternatives

Pre-load ahead, move only the delta at the freeze Chosen

Full load into the dormant objects weeks ahead, refreshed one-way; at the freeze, only what changed moves — then prove it and go live.

  • The bulk load is timed and validated against real production data weeks ahead, and key users check it before the window. almost certain
  • The freeze-day window is small and predictable — only the delta plus the switch. probably
  • A wrong delta watermark misses changed records — caught by the keyed diff and the record-level comparison at the gate. unlikely

Upside, and how big The slow, risky millions-of-rows work leaves the irreversible window entirely; the transform stays deterministic, re-runnable and rehearsable — and now proven on live data ahead of time.

All-in-window · in-org batch · two-way trickle · migrate-on-access Rejected

The alternatives, each failing on a different, decisive count.

  • Moving the whole ten years inside one weekend crams the slowest work into the irreversible window with no chance to validate on real data first. probably
  • In-org batch fights the platform's limits at 1.4M applications and can't be rehearsed deterministically. probably
  • A two-way trickle sync keeps two live copies in step — the very mirroring the strategy ruled out on live volumes. almost certain
  • Migrate-on-access never fully converges, so the package can never be removed — blowing the November-2027 deadline. almost certain

Upside, and how big Each is simpler to describe — but each fails on a hard constraint (window risk, volume, two live copies, or convergence). The pre-load keeps the safe shape and moves the bulk out of the window.

The 461K never-active candidates — a decision decided by magnitude

Both options here are feasible — neither fails technically. The choice turns purely on the size of the cost each carries, which is exactly why a pros-and-cons list can't settle it and a payoff comparison can.

Archive the never-active tail Recommended

Migrate the 601K active candidates; move the 461K who never applied to a cold store.

  • A permanently leaner search index, data-retention surface and migration volume. almost certain
  • An archived profile is genuinely needed later and has to be recalled from the cold store. rarely

Payoff, and how big 43% of the candidate pool — a measured 460,847 records — kept out of the new model's index and retention obligations forever. The benefit compounds every year the platform runs.

Migrate all 1.06M candidates The costlier default

Carry every candidate across, active or not.

  • 461K dead profiles (measured) bloat the new model's search index and data-retention surface permanently. almost certain
  • Every dead profile is a standing data-protection obligation with no offsetting activity. almost certain

Payoff, and how big Nothing is ever unavailable in the live system — but the thing preserved is 461K profiles with no downstream activity, at a cost that never stops being paid.

This is the payoff-magnitude decision of the programme. Migrating everything buys a benefit — nothing to recall — whose likelihood of ever mattering is rarely, against a cost that is almost certain and permanent: a search index and retention surface 43% heavier for good. Archiving flips that trade. Because both options work, the right tool isn't a failure-mode comparison — it's weighing a rare, small upside against a certain, large, compounding one. It is a business and data-protection call, and the sizes point one way. (A smooth go-live wouldn't retroactively prove "migrate all" was right, either — a good outcome from a lopsided bet is still a lopsided bet.)

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

The pre-load itself carries almost no rollback risk: it only writes the dormant new objects, which nobody uses, so an abandoned pre-load is discarded and re-run — the old system never noticed. The irreversible part is still the flip, which is exactly why the go/no-go gate sits before the point of no return, not after. Up to the gate, a failed validation rolls straight back to the frozen, intact package org and re-attempts on the next window — costing a weekend, not data. After the gate, rollback means replaying the records created since the switch back into the package (bounded, and covered under Rollback). The reason a rollback exists at all is that the package stays installed with its data intact until a deliberately-later uninstall gate — remove it too early and the fallback is gone.

Open items — the business calls

Decisions that gate the data move

For the business / product owner — due before the pre-load, earlier than the freeze
Question Why it blocks
The 461K never-active candidates: migrate all, or archive the dead tail? The single biggest data-quality decision — sets migration volume, search-index size and data-retention surface. Now also gates the pre-load: the archival sweep must run before it, or the dormant copy doubles storage past a safe margin for weeks.
The 14 populated-but-uncoded fields, and the 15 always-100% "default" fields: keep or drop? Adds/removes ~29 fields from the candidate map; a wrong drop loses real data.
References (0 rows) and deal sheets (0 rows): confirm not seasonally populated? Confirms the scope reduction.
Saved searches (59K): migrate, or rebuild-empty and let users recreate? Adds/removes a 59K-row object from scope.
Seasonal-placement trough dates? Times the forced seasonal move; and if the run-off fallback is taken, sets its hard end date against the November-2027 deadline.
Full-copy sandbox availability and refresh cadence? Rehearsal is the only real-volume test; it blocks window sizing and the pre-load timing. A dependency, not an assumption.
Storage headroom for the dormant window — archive first, or buy storage? The pre-loaded copy holds old and new data at once for weeks, peaking near the limit. Archiving first keeps it clear; buying storage is the fallback.

These are due before the pre-load — earlier than the freeze — because the rehearsals and the pre-load both have to run on decided rules, or they prove the wrong thing.

Continue to Rollback for how go-live fails back, or Rehearsal for how the data move is proven before it runs for real. Counts are live production as of 12 July 2026 and will drift — re-run before the migration is scheduled.

Decision 11 · Rollback

How we get back if go-live fails

The way back is different for each way a capability crosses — and the difference between them is the argument that decides the whole shape.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

The decision. Every cutover needs a way back if it fails. But rollback isn't one plan — it depends on which way a capability crossed. A big-bang rolls back differently from a slice, and both roll back completely differently from keeping two systems synced. This page lays the rollback of each considered strategy side by side, because comparing them is what actually chose the strategy.

What we chose. The old system is kept installed and intact underneath as a real fallback, with a go/no-go gate before anything irreversible, the way back itself rehearsed and time-boxed, and package removal held as a separate, deliberately-later gate. Reversibility, not build effort, is the deciding factor — and it is the single strongest reason the core big-bangs rather than syncing two live copies.

Rollback by route

Each way of crossing has its own way back

Rollback is conditional on the choice: each way a capability crosses has its own way back — or none. Here is the way back for each route the strategy uses or considered.

Big-bang rollback The core

The old system stays installed and untouched underneath, so there is always a known-good copy and a single dated moment when ownership hands over. Before the go/no-go gate, rolling back costs nothing — the migrated data is validated before activation, so a failure just doesn't flip. After it, rollback is bounded: turn the new automation off, hand the screens and permissions back, and replay only the records created since the switch. Cost grows slowly with time since go-live; the true point of no return — removing the package — comes last, deliberately, and gated.

Slice rollback The edges

A sliced capability (interviews first) is the easiest to reverse: one leaf object, a small amount of history, and one-way writes into what hasn't moved. Rolling back is pointing the automation at the old object again and stopping the new one — contained to that single capability, with no effect on the untouched core. This small blast radius is exactly why the slice goes first: it proves the machinery where a mistake is cheap.

Run-off rollback Placement fallback

If the placement run-off fallback is ever taken, its rollback is gentle by construction: records start life in the chosen system and nothing flips destructively, so "rolling back" is just directing new placements to the old system again. The one thing that must stay clean is the one-way reporting feed — read-only on the old side, never a write-back — so it never becomes a second thing to reconcile.

Trigger flip-back Cheapest of all

For a kept object whose trigger routes between old and new logic by a configuration switch (see Activation & cutover), rollback is that same flip in reverse — and it's the cheapest way back there is, because the old logic was never removed: it sits dormant behind the same trigger, untouched, ready to take over the moment the switch flips back. No redeploy, no data replay for the logic itself. This is why the old logic is left in place rather than deleted at cutover — it is deleted only later, once the new path is confirmed, never the reverse. (Scheduled and batch jobs roll back by their own native on/off, not this switch — the same asymmetry the cutover page draws.)

Sync rollback Rejected — no way back

Once two live copies are syncing, there stops being a single copy you can trust. If a loop, conflict or dropped update pushes them out of step, "roll back to the good state" has nothing to point at. Undoing it means forensically reconstructing which of a million-plus records is right in which system, while both are still being written — and the exposure is continuous across the whole months-long transition. Even just stopping the sync freezes the divergence rather than fixing it. This is the decisive reason sync was rejected for the core.

"But isn't going gradually easier to roll back?"

It depends which "gradually," and this is the crux. Going gradually by slicing capabilities off one at a time — is easier to reverse, and that's exactly why the edges move that way and interviews go first: each slice is a small, self-contained, cheaply-reversible step. But going gradually by keeping the shared core in two synced copies is the hardest thing here to roll back, not the easiest — there's no single trustworthy copy once they drift. So for the edges, gradual wins on reversibility and we use it; for the core, the one rehearsed big-bang — with the old system held intact as a dated, clean fallback — is the easier rollback, not the harder one. "Gradual is safer to reverse" is true for the parts that can be isolated and false for the one part that can't.

What "rollback" actually reverses

Two things to undo — the metadata and the data

Rollback isn't only about data. A go-live flips two kinds of thing, and getting back means reversing both — but they reverse very differently, and that asymmetry is what makes the rollback bounded rather than open-ended.

Metadata Reverse, don't un-deploy

Code, automations, the data model, record pages, record types, permissions and sharing. Because the new build was deployed alongside the old package and switched on at cutover — never replacing it in place — rolling the metadata back is reversing the switch, not un-deploying code: turn the new automations off at their settings, reassign the old record pages, record types, permission sets and sharing, and the old package's own metadata is still there, untouched, ready to take over again. Nothing has to be rebuilt or redeployed to reverse it.

Data Replay the delta

The records that changed since the freeze. Before the go/no-go gate there's nothing to replay — the old data is frozen and intact. After go-live, the only data to move back is what was created or changed natively since the switch; that delta is replayed into the old system. The whole pool never has to be reconstructed, because the old copy was never overwritten.

Why the split matters

Metadata reversal is near-instant and low-risk because the new components are additive and the old ones were never touched — it's flipping settings and reassigning, the same admin actions as activation, run backwards. The data replay is the part that grows with time since go-live, which is why the bounded-delta property (and the dated expiry) matter. The one metadata caution: writes replayed into the old package may trigger its hidden logic, so that path is probed in rehearsal (see below), not assumed.

A third thing to undo — the outside connections, and one of them isn't ours to flip

Beyond the metadata and the data, the outside systems that connect in also point at whichever objects are live, and rolling back means pointing them back. How that's done splits three ways by who owns the switch. Where Marlex owns the connection — a Marlex-built service, or one routed through the integration platform — the switch is on our side and reverses in the same window as everything else. But a few tools connect through their own connector, with the mapping to our objects held on their side; there is no switch for Marlex to flip. Rolling those back means the tool's owner re-points their connector at the old objects — a step on someone else's system, on someone else's clock. Two of these are expected live before go-live, so this is engineered the same way as the rest of the fallback: each such owner agrees in advance who re-points, within what window and on what signal, and holds the reference that says which old object to point back at. The way back that runs on another party's action is the one to nail down before it's needed, not during.

The deciding comparison

One dated handover versus no known-good state

Put the two options for the shared core side by side on the one axis that matters — is there ever a copy you can trust to return to? — and they are opposites in kind, not degree.

Rollback, big-bang versus keeping both live and synced
  Big-bang on a dormant build Keep both live and synced
A known-good copy to return to? Yes — old system until go-live, new after; one dated handover No — two live copies, no single authority once they diverge
Rollback blast radius Bounded — the records created since the freeze Unbounded — the whole synced pool, continuously
When is rollback cheapest? Any time before the go/no-go gate (≈ free) Never — coupling exists from the first sync write
What rollback involves Flip settings back + replay a bounded delta Forensic un-merge of a live million-record pool
Point of no return Package removal — deliberate, last, gated Effectively the first two-way write — unmarked, early

This is why the recommendation lands where it does: the approach with a real off-ramp wins, and that is the one rehearsed switch on a system built dormant ahead of time. Reversibility, not build effort, is the deciding factor.

Alternatives considered — the rollback mechanism

Why the intact-fallback approach, and not the others

Keep the old system intact underneath + go/no-go gate + late separate removalChosen

The old system is never modified during the build and is held read-only once the new one is live, so it stays a clean fallback until it's deliberately removed. What can still go wrong is real and is engineered against (below): the way back is never rehearsed and fails when invoked; something keeps writing the old system and quietly spoils it as a fallback; the replay itself fires hidden package logic; a bad first morning triggers a panic rollback that costs more than fixing forward; or the fallback feels so safe that removal keeps slipping toward the support deadline.

Run both live and compare outputsRejected

This is keeping two copies synced by another name — the no-known-good-state problem applies in full.

Backup / snapshot restore as the rollbackRejected

A restore rolls back the business's own legitimate work along with the migration — losing everything done since the snapshot. Slow, partial, and worse than the failure it's meant to fix.

No rollback — fix forward onlyRejected

Unacceptable exposure on the system the entire recruitment operation runs on. There has to be a way back.

What makes the fallback real

Five commitments that turn a claimed rollback into an engineered one

The honest finding from working this through: as first written, the rollback was a capability we asserted, not one we'd engineered. Five commitments close that gap — this is where the pre-mortem bit hardest.

Rehearse it

The final rehearsal ends with a timed flip-back drill — reversing the structure and replaying the delta — so the way back is an exercised procedure, not a hope. (On the Rehearsal page.)

Write the criteria

The conditions that would trigger a rollback are agreed and written down, with a named decision owner, before go-live — decided in calm, not argued during an incident. This is what stops a bad first morning becoming a panic rollback that costs more than fixing forward.

Lock the fallback

The old system is held read-only by permissions, not by policy — and verified with a write-probe after go-live. If anything keeps writing it, it quietly stops being a clean copy to return to.

Probe the replay

Replaying records back into the old system is itself a write into a package object — which may fire hidden package logic. The sandbox behavioural probe covers this path too, so the black-box risk isn't discovered live inside the rollback.

Give it an expiry

The fallback carries a dated expiry: after an agreed number of days the plan becomes fix-forward, the old system is retired as a target, and its removal goes back on a schedule. A fallback without an end date quietly becomes the reason removal never happens — right as the support deadline arrives. What must be true on that removal date is written down too — the uninstall go-criteria on Operating the new system.

One caveat that keeps the recommendation honest

The clean rollback holds only while the build really is deployed dormant and rehearsed twice with a go/no-go gate. A big-bang done without the dormant build — deploying under cutover pressure — would forfeit most of this advantage. The rollback case is an argument for the dormant big-bang specifically, not for any big-bang.

How the options compare

The rollback mechanism, and the door it keeps open

The comparison above weighed big-bang against sync. This one weighs the three ways to build a fallback for the chosen big-bang — and frames every step by the kind of door it is. A two-way door can be walked back through; a one-way door can't. The entire runbook is arranged to keep the last two-way door — the go/no-go gate — as late as possible, and to hold the one-way door — removing the package — separate and last.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Keep the old system intact underneath Chosen

Never modified during the build; held read-only once the new one is live; removed deliberately, later.

  • A rollback invoked before the go/no-go gate succeeds at essentially no cost — the validated data just doesn't flip. almost certain
  • A rollback invoked after the gate succeeds as a bounded replay of only the records created since the switch — given the timed flip-back drill in the final rehearsal. probably
  • The replay into the old system fires the package's hidden logic (probed in rehearsal first). possibly
  • A bad first morning triggers a panic rollback — headed off by written invocation criteria. unlikely

The door The go/no-go gate is a two-way door held to the last safe moment; package removal is the one-way door, kept separate and dated.

Snapshot-restore · fix-forward-only Rejected

The two mechanisms considered instead — each closes a door it shouldn't.

  • A snapshot restore rolls the business's own legitimate post-freeze work back too — losing real data. almost certain
  • Fix-forward-only leaves no way back at all on the system the whole operation runs on. almost certain
  • (And sync, from the comparison above: no known-good copy once two live copies drift.) almost certain

The door Each turns a recoverable situation into a one-way door — restore destroys good work; fix-forward has no return; sync erases the known-good state.

Invoking a rollback is itself a decision with a probability and a cost — so it gets criteria, not instinct. The probability a rollback is needed is low if the two rehearsals and the go/no-go gate do their job; the cost of invoking it wrongly — a panic rollback on a rough first morning that fixing forward would have handled — can exceed the cost of the problem. That is why the way back is rehearsed, its trigger conditions written down in calm beforehand, and its fallback given a dated expiry after which the plan becomes fix-forward. The preference this serves is a real off-ramp that is used deliberately, not reflexively. And the asymmetry cuts both ways: a smooth go-live would not retroactively prove the fallback was unnecessary — it was the cheap insurance that made the smooth go-live safe to attempt.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Rollback fidelity decays with time. The new model is richer than the old one; records born native may not map back faithfully, so the further past go-live, the less faithful a rollback — which is what the dated expiry acknowledges and bounds.
  • Two of the commitments need governance sign-off. The written rollback-invocation criteria (with a named owner) and the dated fallback expiry are decisions for the programme's governance board, not the delivery team — flagged for that decision.
  • The replay path is black-box-dependent until the probe clears it — the same hidden-package unknown that gates the first slice.
  • One class of rollback runs on a third party's action. A few connected tools re-point from their own side, not ours, and two are expected live before go-live. The way back for those depends on each owner's readiness — agreed and dated in advance, but outside Marlex's direct control on the day.

Continue to Rehearsal — where the way back is drilled and every other decision is proven before it runs for real. Return to the overview.

Cutover · reference

Removing TargetRecruit — the uninstall dependency surface

Once the rebuild is live and the data has moved, the old package still has to come out of the org. This is the measured evidence of everything that can block that uninstall or silently break after it — including the parts no dependency tool can see.

Measured live · production org · 17 July 2026 · read-only discovery · living document

Read this if you are planning the day the package is finally removed. Uninstalling a managed package is not a switch — Salesforce refuses it if anything outside the package still points at it, and there is a whole class of dependency it cannot check that simply breaks afterwards. This page is the counted surface for TargetRecruit, straight from the live org.

The findings, in one line. TargetRecruit is not one package but six; the core alone has 1,658 references from the org's own components that must be cleared first; and the real risk sits in what the checker can't see — 99 shared reports and 47 private ones built on the old objects, dynamic code that names the old objects only as text, and outside systems that read them. Those don't block the uninstall — they break the morning after.

The one rule that governs everything below

Salesforce's uninstall check is static only. It looks at metadata that formally references the package and blocks the uninstall until those are cleared — annoying, but self-announcing: it tells you what to fix. It is blind to anything that resolves at run time by a name rather than a formal link: code that builds a query from a string, an outside system calling in, a report in someone's private folder. Those pass the check and fail in production after cutover. The things that block are the easy ones. The things that break silently are the risk.

What we are actually removing

TargetRecruit is a family of six packages, not one

The org has six TargetRecruit-family managed packages installed. Removing "TargetRecruit" means removing all of them — and because one is data-coupled to the core, order matters. The core is the root of the tree, so it comes out last.

Package What it is Objects Uninstall order
Core AVTRRT TargetRecruit Managed — the candidate / job / application / placement model. v6.286. 110 Last — everything else clears first
BCST Job Broadcasting — pushes jobs to job boards. 15 Before / with the core — its records point at the core's Job object
CRDNTLS Credentials — candidate skills / questionnaires. 12 Independent — any order
TRGTFCMS Candidate Portal — external portal configuration. 13 Independent — any order
TRINTG TR Integration — connectors and logging. 5 Independent — any order
TRPRTL Portal shared base (4 modules) — portal sites and sessions. 8 Independent — any order

Coupling checked by inspecting every satellite object's lookups: only BCST__Job_Broadcast__c and BCST__Job_Extra_Field__c reference the core (AVTRRT__Job__c). The other four are settings / logger / portal packages that carry almost no data and don't touch the candidate or job model — they can be removed on their own. The core has no lookups back into any satellite, which is why it uninstalls last.

The core package alone accounts for 480 metadata items — 110 business objects plus 370 system-generated companions (history, sharing, change-event and tag objects). All of them, and every record they hold, are deleted on uninstall.

The blockers — what stops the uninstall

1,658 of the org's own components point at the core

Salesforce won't let the core package out while anything in the org still references it. Measured against the core namespace, that is 1,658 references, dominated by the org's own Apex and page layouts. These are the findable blockers — each one has to be edited away or deleted before the uninstall will start.

What references the old package References Distinct items Effect
Apex code 1,177 132 Blocks — 132 of the org's own classes name the old classes/objects
Page layouts 356 14 Blocks — old fields / related lists sitting on layouts
Custom fields (on other objects) 85 58 Blocks — formulas and cross-object references into the old model
Email templates 33 19 Merge fields go blank; blocks if a shared letterhead is involved
Buttons / links 6 6 Blocks
Custom tab 1 1 Blocks

A good sign in the numbers: the check found no flows, validation rules or workflow rules built on the old objects in the org's own layer. The coupling is code and layout, not declarative automation — a smaller and more predictable surface to unpick than it could have been.

Source: the platform's own dependency index. It is authoritative for formal metadata links like these — but it deliberately excludes reports, and it cannot see run-time references. That gap is the next section.

The blind spots — what breaks after, not before

What the dependency check cannot see

Three categories carry the real cutover risk, because none of them appear in the blocker count above. Two of them are large and measured here; the third is inherent to any package removal.
