Reports — a large surface the checker omits entirely

Reports are excluded from the platform's dependency index by design, so none of the reports below show up in the 1,658. We classified every report in the org by reading its definition through the reporting API:

Report population Count Can we see what it's built on?
Shared / public reports on the old objects 99 Yes — 77 built directly on old objects, 22 pulling old fields into standard reports
Reports in users' private folders 47 No — we can list them, but the source object can't be read for another user's private report

The 99 shared reports are a clean cleanup list — delete or re-point them before the window. The 47 private ones are the trap. An admin can enumerate them (with the right permission and a special query scope), and several are clearly recruitment reports by name — but the platform will not reveal which object a private report is built on to anyone but its owner. A private report on an old object typically blocks the uninstall with an error the dependency viewer can't trace back to a culprit. The only way to pre-empt it is to have each owner move or delete their private reports (and empty the recycle bin) before the day.

Dynamic code — names the old objects only as text

Code that builds a query or looks up an object from a string holds no formal link to the package, so it never appears as a dependency. In the retrieved code we found 28 classes that combine dynamic queries with a reference to the old namespace. These don't block the uninstall — they throw at run time once the object is gone. This is the classic morning-after regression, and it is only findable by reading the code, never by a dependency tool.

Outside systems — invisible to Salesforce

Any external system that queries the old objects — the middleware, the reporting feed, the job board — keeps working right up to the uninstall, then starts erroring. Salesforce cannot see who calls in from outside. This is the same surface already counted on the measured integration surface; the point here is only that removing the package is one more moment those edges have to be re-pointed by.

Also sweep before the window

Private list views (invisible per user), custom report types on the old objects, and any analytics datasets fed from them — smaller, but each is a quiet reader of the retiring model.

How the uninstall itself behaves

What it deletes, and the traps in the mechanics

  • It deletes everything in each package — and cascades. All objects, fields and records go, including related data you might not expect (child records under the package's own parent objects).
  • It fails closed, not halfway. A single unresolved blocker stops the whole thing before anything is removed — so there are no half-deleted leftovers, but expect repeated failed attempts, especially the permission-set loop: the uninstall refuses while any user is still assigned a package permission set, and fresh ones can appear on each retry. Strip them all, retry until clean.
  • The data export lasts only 48 hours. The uninstall offers a one-time copy of the package's records — download it immediately, because it's gone after two days and the relationships aren't re-importable as-is. Take a full export independently as well.
  • Rehearse it in a sandbox first. This org has a known quirk where package-shipped custom metadata misbehaves under the deploy tools; the safe path is to dry-run the whole uninstall in a full or partial copy, resolve every blocker there, then repeat the cleared sequence in production.

The pre-cutover sweep

The order of operations before the window

Every step below is read-only discovery or reversible cleanup, done well ahead of the day:

  • Map all six packages' cross-references to fix the uninstall order (satellites first, BCST before the core, core last).
  • Clear the 1,658 blockers — retire or re-point the org's own Apex, layouts, fields, templates, buttons and the tab that reference the core.
  • Delete or re-point the 99 shared reports on the old objects.
  • Have owners clear their 47 private reports (move to a shared folder or delete, then empty the recycle bin) — the one step no admin can do alone.
  • Read the code for dynamic references — the 28 flagged classes — and retire them with the rest of the old Apex.
  • Re-point every outside caller from the integration surface.
  • Strip all package permission-set assignments, then dry-run the uninstall in a sandbox until it runs clean.
  • Full data export — plus the 48-hour package export at uninstall time, downloaded on the spot.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The private-report blind spot. 47 private reports exist; we can't confirm which are on the old objects, so this is an owner-outreach task, not a query. If missed, it surfaces as an untraceable uninstall failure.
  • Dynamic references are a floor, not a ceiling. The 28 flagged classes come from reading the retrieved code; any run-time reference built from a truly external string can still slip through. The mitigation is the sandbox dry-run — a clean uninstall there is the real proof.
  • Cross-package coupling is measured, but managed-to-managed links are under-reported by the platform's index — the BCST → core coupling was found by inspecting objects directly. The sandbox rehearsal is what confirms the full order holds.
  • Open: the uninstall is the tail end of the cutover, not the cutover itself — it happens only once the new model is proven and the old data has been archived. Its timing follows the rollback window closing, not the go-live.

Continue to Rollback for how the cutover fails back before this uninstall is ever reached, or back to Activation & cutover for the switch that precedes it. Counts are live production as of 17 July 2026 and will drift.

Decision 12 · Testing behaviour parity

Capture what each feature does, then hold the rebuild to it

Before a feature can be rebuilt safely, its current behaviour has to be written down — because most of it lives in a package whose logic can't be read. This page decides how that behaviour is captured, turned into acceptance tests, and reused to prove the native replacement matches.

Rebuild strategy · draft for peer review · 15 July 2026

Why this earns its own page. The rebuild replaces features whose behaviour nobody has fully written down — and can't simply read, because the logic sits inside a managed package that is a black box on shared objects. The functional inventory says which features exist and whether each is kept, rebuilt or dropped; it does not say how a rebuilt feature actually behaves — the edge cases, the side effects, the exact outcome of a given action. Rebuild from that alone and the gaps only surface after go-live, reported by recruiters. This page decides how the behaviour of each rebuilt feature is captured up front and made into a test the new build must pass.

What we chose. A characterisation pass over the features being rebuilt — the classic discipline for replacing a system you don't fully understand: capture the current behaviour as written, executable expectations before rebuilding, then rebuild until the native version reproduces them. Behaviour is captured two ways at once — by reading the metadata that can be read, and by observing the running feature in a sandbox on dummy data — and written up as plain given/when/then acceptance criteria. Those criteria become two things with very different lifespans: a durable behaviour specification that is the acceptance backbone of the rebuild and each capability's go-live criterion, and an executable test suite on the native side that becomes the permanent regression net. AI-driven exploration (including browser automation against the old screens) is the one-time accelerant that produces the capture — not an asset anyone maintains.

Alternatives considered

Rebuild from the inventory, characterise everything, or characterise what's being rebuilt

The real question is where the specification for the new build comes from. There are three honest answers, and only one survives the fact that the thing being replaced is unreadable.

Rebuild from the written requirements aloneRejected

Treat the functional inventory and the requirements as the specification and build to them. This is the default when nothing is decided — and it under-specifies by design. The inventory records that a feature exists and what it is for; it does not record how the package behaves when a record changes, what it quietly writes elsewhere, or which of ten edge cases matters. Those are exactly the behaviours a black box hides, so building blind means discovering the missing ones in production.

Characterise everything, exhaustivelyRejected

Systematically observe and script every feature and every path in the old system. This over-invests in a product that is being retired: features that are kept as-is or dropped need no rebuild specification, and exhaustively scripting the old package's own screens produces throwaway artefacts that die with it. Effort spent proving behaviour nobody will rebuild is effort not spent building.

Characterise what's being rebuilt — behaviour first, the black box firstChosen

Run a targeted characterisation pass over the features on the rebuild list, prioritising the ones whose behaviour is opaque because their logic is locked inside the package. Capture each as written given/when/then criteria, and let those criteria be the acceptance backbone the native build is measured against. It converts an unreadable black box into a written, testable specification — the one thing that lets a mid-level team rebuild a feature they can't see the source of, with confidence they matched it.

How a feature's behaviour gets captured

Read what's readable, observe what isn't, write it down

Characterising one feature is a short loop. Two sources are combined because neither is enough alone: the metadata is precise but partial (the package's own logic isn't in it), and the running behaviour is complete but has to be inferred from the outside. Together they produce a specification more trustworthy than either.

  1. Read what's readable — the metadata is the deterministic half

    Custom fields, validation rules, Marlex's own automation and layouts can be read directly and describe behaviour deterministically — no observation needed. This is the cheaper, more reliable half and it comes first: where the behaviour is in metadata Marlex owns, read it rather than guess it from the screen.

  2. Observe what isn't — the black box, in a sandbox, on dummy data

    What the package does on its own can't be read, only watched. In a sandbox — never production — create, change and delete the records in question and record what happens: what the screen shows, what gets written, what fires downstream. Browser automation driven by AI makes this cheap to do at breadth and to repeat. This is the same experiment as the black-box probe on the Rehearsal page, widened from a few gating questions to the whole rebuild list.

  3. Write it down as given/when/then

    The output of each feature is a set of plain acceptance criteria — given this record in this state, when the user does this, then this is the outcome — plus a note of the underlying logic that produces it. That write-up is the durable artefact; the exploration that produced it is not. Writing the behaviour down is also where the feature's real scope and purpose finally gets understood, not just its surface.

On dummy data, in a sandbox, never production

Characterisation creates and mutates records, so it runs only where writing is safe — a managed sandbox, never a production org, which stays read-only throughout the programme. The records are fabricated: no real names, emails or other personal data copied from production. The behaviour of a feature is independent of whose data flows through it, so dummy data proves it just as well and carries none of the risk.

What the capture is worth over time

One capture, two artefacts — with different lifespans

The same characterisation produces two things, and conflating them is the trap. One outlives the old package by years; the other is disposable. Deciding which effort to sink where is most of this decision.

The behaviour specification — durable

The written given/when/then criteria per rebuilt feature. This is the acceptance backbone of the rebuild: it is what "done" means for each native component, and it is the per-capability go/no-go the cutover flips against. It is authored once, refined as understanding deepens, and it stays true after the package is gone — because it describes a business behaviour, not a screen.

The old-system scripts — throwaway

Any browser-automation script that drove the old package's screens to observe them is a one-time capture tool. The product is being retired, so maintaining an automated suite against it is effort spent on something being switched off. Run it, harvest the behaviour, keep the write-up, discard the script.

The native suite — the permanent regression net

On the new side, the acceptance criteria become real automated tests, and this is where lasting test investment goes. The durable, cheap layer is code-level — Apex and Flow tests and component unit tests — co-located with the code and run automatically. A thin browser-driven layer covers only the few critical user journeys end to end. This is the suite the continuous-proof harness then runs nightly to catch drift.

The reusable asset is the test case, not the click-path

It is tempting to imagine the scripts that drive the old screens simply re-run against the new ones. They can't: the old package's screens and the native build share no layout, no URLs, no structure, so a click-path recorded against one is meaningless against the other. What ports across is the acceptance criterion — the given/when/then business outcome — re-implemented against the new system, mostly as a code-level test rather than a screen script. Plan to reuse the cases, and expect to re-author the mechanics.

Where the effort is spent

What gets characterised, and what doesn't

Characterisation is targeted, not total. The rebuild list decides the scope, and within it the opaque features come first because that is where reading the metadata leaves the biggest gap.

  • Rebuilt features — yes, black-box logic first. Every feature on the rebuild list is characterised. Priority goes to the ones whose behaviour is buried in the package's own logic, because for those, observation is the only source of a specification — Marlex's own configuration can be read, but the package's can't.
  • Features kept as-is — no. A feature that is not being rebuilt needs no new specification; it keeps behaving as it does. Characterising it produces nothing anyone will build against.
  • Features being dropped — no. Behaviour that is deliberately not carried forward is not worth capturing.
  • The package's standard behaviour — only where it stays and matters. Where users rely on out-of-the-box package functionality that is in scope to reproduce natively, characterise it like any other rebuilt feature. Where it is going away, don't.

Which features sit in which bucket is the disposition already recorded on the scope page; the characterisation backlog follows that list rather than inventing its own.

How this differs from the rehearsal harness

This page is upstream of the continuous-proof harness, and the two shouldn't be confused. Characterisation captures what the old system does and turns it into the acceptance criteria — the specification. The harness then runs the native tests written from those criteria, continuously, to prove the dormant build stays correct. One writes the exam; the other sits it every night.

A question every acceptance criterion has to answer

Prove it as one user, or as several?

A given/when/then criterion is only unambiguous once it also says who is running the "when." Most business logic gives the same outcome regardless of who triggers it, so proving it once, as a single user, is a complete proof. But a feature whose outcome depends on sharing, org-wide defaults, role hierarchy, profile or permission-set grants can pass every single-user test and still be broken for the roles that actually need it — a recruiter test account with full visibility proves nothing about what a real, narrower-access recruiter can see. So this is not a thoroughness dial to turn up everywhere; it's a per-feature question with a specific trigger.

Single user is enough

Calculations, validations, data transformations, record-triggered automation whose outcome does not vary by who is logged in. One persona — the primary one for that feature — proves the behaviour completely; testing it again as other users adds cost without adding confidence.

Multiple personas are required

Anything gated by OWD, a sharing rule, role-hierarchy visibility, a profile's object/field permissions, or a permission set — record visibility across regions or teams, queue membership, the app/record-page switch itself (see Keep the standard objects standard). Here "does it work" and "does it work for this role" are different questions, and only a multi-persona test answers the second.

The trigger, so this is a checklist item, not a judgment call each time

The test is textual, not a matter of guessing: if a feature's behaviour specification (captured above) names sharing, OWD, role, profile or permission set as a factor in the outcome, it needs the multi-persona case, run as each affected persona rather than as one privileged test account. If it doesn't name any of those, single-user is sufficient and further personas are wasted effort. Writing this into the given/when/then itself — "given a recruiter in Team A" rather than just "given a recruiter" — is what makes the trigger checkable during the capture, not discovered as a gap during rehearsal.

This is a testing-method rule, not a sharing-model rule — it belongs here because it decides how a captured criterion is proven, not how sharing itself is designed (see Security for that). It does mean the sharing-dependent slice of the rebuild list carries a real, named testing cost: every capability whose behaviour varies by role needs as many proof-runs as personas that matter, not one.

How the options compare

A written behaviour spec versus building to the requirements

The choice is best judged by where the missing behaviour is found — in a cheap sandbox before the build, or in production after go-live. The two aren't points on a thoroughness dial; they decide when the black box's surprises arrive.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%.

Characterise what's being rebuilt Chosen

Capture each rebuilt feature's behaviour into written acceptance criteria before building; opaque package logic first; the criteria become the native regression suite.

  • Hidden package behaviour is found in a sandbox, before the native component is designed, when a fix is cheap. probably
  • Each capability reaches a written, agreed go/no-go instead of a subjective "looks right". almost certain
  • The capture effort partly lands on behaviour that turns out trivial — some over-investment on the easy features. possibly

What it buys, and how big A specification for something unreadable, and a regression net that outlives the package — bought with sandbox time before the build, the cheapest place to spend it.

Build to the requirements, verify later Rejected

Treat the inventory and requirements as the spec; confirm behaviour when the native build is exercised.

  • A behaviour that lived only in the package is missed, and surfaces first as a recruiter-reported regression after go-live. probably
  • Each feature's "is it right?" becomes a judgment call at cutover with no written baseline to check against. almost certain
  • Effort looks saved up front — until the post-go-live defect and re-work costs land during hypercare. more likely than not

What it buys, and how big Time saved before the build, repaid with interest as production incidents on the irreversible side of the cutover.

The whole value is moving the discovery of hidden behaviour from after go-live to before the build. A black box only reveals what it does when you run it; the question is whether it runs first in a safe sandbox or first in production. Characterising what's being rebuilt buys a written specification for an unreadable system and a regression net that survives it — at the cost of sandbox time spent at the cheapest possible moment. The preference it serves is a rebuilt feature that provably does what the old one did, verified before the switch, not discovered after it.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Characterisation is almost pure upside on reversibility — it is discovery in a sandbox on fabricated data, so it can harm nothing live and there is no production risk to unwind. The one thing it can't guarantee is completeness: observation shows what it triggers, never the absence of a behaviour it didn't think to trigger. The fail-back rule for that is honesty about confidence — where a feature's behaviour is only partly pinned down, its acceptance criteria are marked provisional, and the black-box probe and the volume rehearsal (see Rehearsal) stand behind them as the later, coarser nets. The only real failure mode of this page is skipping it — rebuilding to a requirements list and meeting the package's surprises in production — which is exactly the false economy the pass exists to prevent.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Observation can't prove a negative. A characterisation pass reduces the black-box unknown sharply but can't demonstrate the absence of every hidden reaction — so behaviour verdicts on package-driven features stay marked provisional even after it, the same caveat the probe carries.
  • Equivalence depth is a judgment call. "Same business outcome" is provable on the criteria that were captured; "identical in every edge case" is not — the capture bounds that gap without closing it.
  • Screen-level scripts are brittle by nature. The durable net must sit at the code level; a browser-driven layer is kept deliberately thin, or its maintenance cost quietly overwhelms the value it adds.
  • The sandbox is a dependency. The pass needs a sandbox with the package installed and configured and enough fabricated data to exercise each feature — availability and refresh to confirm with Marlex IT, alongside the full-copy dependency the rehearsal already carries.
  • AI-assisted capture needs a human check. Automated exploration accelerates the capture but doesn't ratify it; a captured criterion is only as trustworthy as the review that signs it off, so review is part of the loop, not an optional extra.
  • The disposition can move. A feature's kept / rebuilt / dropped status can change; the characterisation backlog tracks the scope list rather than a private copy, so a re-classification re-scopes the pass instead of silently orphaning work.

Return to the overview, see how these criteria are run continuously against the dormant build in Rehearsal, or how each feature is built to meet them in Rebuild approach. The scope that decides what gets characterised is on the scope page.

Decision 13 · Rehearsal

Prove it continuously, then rehearse the flip both ways

One story in two halves: a continuous-proof harness keeps the dormant build correct through the months it sits switched off, and full-copy dress rehearsals then prove it at real volume and drill the activation and the flip back to the old package — until both are timed.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 13 July 2026

Why this earns its own page. By the time we rehearse, every component is already deployed to production, switched off, waiting. So there is nothing left to rehearse about deploying it. Two different things have to be proven instead. First, that the switched-off build stayed correct while the live system kept changing underneath it for months. Second, that the switch itself works — that turning the new system on lands cleanly, and that turning it back off returns to the old package cleanly — both timed, both drilled. This page decides how each is proven, and the two are one story: keep it correct continuously, then prove it at volume and prove the flip in both directions.

What we chose. A continuous-proof harness runs through the whole build — a shared test-data factory, a paired-component register with a same-change rule, a quality bar above the platform floor, the suite executed nightly in production so any drift surfaces within a day, and scripted walkthroughs for the structural layer that has no unit tests. Then at least two full-copy dress rehearsals at the end: refresh a sandbox from production (so it already holds the released dormant build, the real data and the real config), then practise the activation — including the data-load wall-clock at real volume — and practise the deactivation and rollback, the timed flip back to native, each run on a clean baseline. A separate low-cost behavioural probe answers the one thing no rehearsal can: how the black-box package reacts when records change.

Alternatives considered

Test once, test only at the end, or prove continuously

The dormant build's biggest residual risk isn't the cutover weekend — it's the quiet months before it. Two things rot switched-off software: drift (a fix or rule change made to the live system is not mirrored into its dormant counterpart, so go-live silently regresses it) and staleness (a dormant component passed its tests the week it was written and was never exercised again). At the scale of this rebuild — more than six hundred components sitting dormant for months — measured drift is a certainty, not a risk to accept: over that many components and that long a window, the live system will change in ways the dormant twin doesn't follow. The only question is whether it is caught the day it happens or discovered on go-live morning.

Test at build time, then trustRejected

Each component is tested when written and assumed good thereafter. This is the default that happens when nothing is decided — and it fails on arithmetic. Every unmirrored live fix becomes a regression scheduled for go-live morning, discovered by recruiters rather than by the team.

Prove everything only at the rehearsalsRejected

Lean on the dress rehearsals as the sole quality gate. The rehearsals are essential — they prove the data move and the activation at real volume — but as the only proof they arrive far too late: months of accumulated drift would surface in one weekend, a pile of failures too large to fix before the window closes. Rehearsals verify; they cannot be the feedback loop.

A continuous-proof harness, then rehearse at volumeChosen

Make "still correct" a property that is re-verified automatically and often, all the way through the build — then use the dress rehearsals for what only they can do: prove the volume and drill the flip both ways. This costs real discipline, and that cost is the point: it converts a silent, compounding risk into visible, scheduled work that a mid-level and junior team can actually keep up.

Proving the build before the rehearsal

The continuous-proof harness — five parts, each with a job

This runs for the whole build, long before any dress rehearsal. It is what keeps the dormant build trustworthy month after month, so that when the rehearsals arrive they are measuring timing and parity on a build already believed correct — not discovering bugs.

  1. The test-data factory — the foundation everything stands on

    A single shared factory that generates realistic test data in the new model: candidates, applications, processes, placements, in the shapes and volumes the real business produces. It is the first thing built — nothing merges without tests, and no test of a native component is possible until the factory can supply native data. It is also what keeps tests honest over time: fixtures evolve in one place as the model evolves.

  2. The paired-component register and the same-change rule

    Every live component that has a dormant counterpart is listed in a register — one row per pair, with an owner. The change-control rule is one sentence: a change touching a registered live component must change its counterpart in the same change, or explicitly record why not. Enforced at code review from day one, and by the deployment pipeline as its automation matures. This is the direct answer to drift: the mirror obligation attaches the moment the divergence is created, when the context is fresh — not months later from archaeology.

  3. A quality bar above the platform minimum

    The platform demands 75% of code lines be executed by tests before deployment — a floor famously satisfiable by tests that assert nothing. The bar for new code in this rebuild is stricter and about substance: every new class ships tests that assert business outcomes on factory data (the placement got created with the right rates; the application landed on the right process), with review rejecting coverage-without-assertions. The numeric target for new code will be set above the floor when the delivery standard is agreed; the honest statement is that the assertion discipline matters more than the percentage.

  4. The suite runs nightly, in production, against the dormant build

    Salesforce test runs never touch real data — everything a test creates is rolled back — so the full suite can safely execute in production on a nightly schedule, exercising the dormant components in the one org that counts. A live-side change that breaks a dormant counterpart surfaces as a red run the next morning, with the causing change a day old and easy to find. This turns the drift risk from "discovered at cutover" into "detected within a day," which is the whole harness in one sentence.

  5. What the tests can't reach, walkthroughs cover

    Screens, page layouts, apps, reports and permissions — the structural layer — have no unit tests to run. They are proven by scripted walkthroughs in the build sandboxes on a recurring cadence, by the training programme itself (every training session on the dormant screens is also a test of them — see Change management), and finally by the enumerated activation checklist at the rehearsals.

What a dormant counterpart must pass to count as "done"

Three gates, in order: its own outcome-asserting tests on factory data pass; it is equivalent to its live counterpart on the behaviours the business depends on (same inputs, same business outcome, demonstrated on shared fixtures); and it survives the rehearsal parity checks at real volume. "Deployed" is none of these — deployed is just present.

For the developersThe factory emits MA_-model fixtures (candidate + application + process graphs against the shared spine) and is the first Platform Foundation enabler, ahead of the flag framework and the reference service pattern. Scheduled runs = full local test execution (package tests excluded) nightly in production; failures page the owning developer, not a mailbox. The escalating change freeze near cutover (progressively stricter rules on touching old-system-coupled components) is the harness's end-game.

Proving it at volume — and drilling the flip

The dress rehearsals — refresh from production, then practise on and off

Because the build is already released to production dormant, the rehearsal does not rehearse a deployment. It rehearses the switch. The setup is a single move: refresh a full-copy sandbox from production, so it comes up already holding the released dormant build, the real data at real volume, and the real configuration — an honest stand-in for the live org on cutover morning. Everything the rehearsal then does is exercised against that copy, with no risk to the business.

Two things are practised, timed, and repeated at least twice — each run starting only from a clean baseline, once the previous run's findings are fixed or clearly understood. The discipline is borrowed from Salesforce's own package-to-native migration guidance: never run a migration until the assessment is clean or clearly understood — skipping that is the most common reason migrations fail. A rehearsal on a dirty baseline proves nothing.

Practise the activation

Turn the new system on end to end: rehearse both halves of the data move — the full pre-load (extract → transform → load → validate) timed at real volume, and the far smaller delta that runs inside the freeze window — then flip the switches that make the new model live and walk the enumerated activation checklist. Two wall-clock numbers are the headline outputs: the pre-load hours (run ahead, off the critical path) and the delta-window hours (what actually sizes the freeze the business commits to) — counted numbers, not estimates.

Practise the deactivation and rollback

Then flip it back: turn the new system off, return to native and the old package, replay any delta, and time the flip both ways. This is the drill that turns the way back (see Rollback) from an assertion into a timed, proven procedure — it is central to the rehearsal, not an add-on at the end.

Check parity, each run

Row-count parity per object, zero orphaned children, and — beyond counts — a sampled record-by-record comparison on the migrated fields — the same parity the go/no-go gate applies (see Data migration). A run that doesn't produce a wall-clock, a proven flip-back, and a parity check doesn't count.

The flip in both directions is the deliverable

The single most valuable output of the full-copy rehearsal is not a deployment — that already happened. It is a timed activation and a timed rollback, both proven on real volume and confirmed stable across two runs. The activation gives the freeze-window wall-clock the cutover date rests on; the rollback gives a way back that has actually been exercised. Until a full-copy run produces both, the cutover date cannot honestly be set (see Cutover).

The one thing rehearsal can't answer

The black-box behavioural probe

A dress rehearsal proves the data move and the flip. It can't prove how the package reacts, because the package's logic is unreadable. That's a separate, cheap experiment, worth doing early: in a sandbox, with Marlex's own automation switched off, create, change and delete the records in question and watch what the package does on its own — does a candidate-status change ripple somewhere, does a portal surface something, does a write into a package object fire hidden logic?

This probe is the narrow, gating end of a wider practice: capturing the black box's behaviour as a written specification before rebuilding it, covered on the Testing behaviour parity page. Here it is scoped to the few questions that gate the sequence; there it runs across the whole rebuild list. It is rehearsal-adjacent, not a full run, and it clears several decisions at once:

  • The first slice — whether interviews are truly a self-contained leaf, or the package quietly reacts when an interview changes (which would make the slice core-coupled).
  • The candidate re-stamp — whether the package reacts to the record-type change on a million Contacts.
  • The rollback replay — whether replaying records back into the package during the flip-back fires hidden logic inside the way-back path.
Do it early

The probe is cheap and it gates the sequence — it should run before the core design is frozen and before the first slice is committed. Several "this is provisional on the black box" caveats across the other pages collapse the moment the probe clears.

The environment, and the constraint it sets

Full copy is single-purpose and temporary — and it sets a floor under the date

A full copy of the org is a limited, slow-to-refresh resource. Scheduling two rehearsals — plus room for a retry if the first surfaces something big — against that refresh cadence puts a real lower bound on how soon the cutover can happen. This isn't a detail to sort out later; it's a hard dependency on the date itself.

Full copy is needed for one thing only

The full copy exists for the volume-timed rehearsal — the activation-and-rollback drill — and only for the window in which those runs happen; it can be spun down afterwards, so it is a temporary need, not a standing environment. Everything else uses lighter sandboxes: the build and the continuous-proof harness run on partial or developer sandboxes, and user training runs in its own dedicated training sandbox (see Change management), never the full copy. The programme should hold full-copy usage to that single purpose rather than treat it as an always-on resource.

A dependency to confirm with Marlex, not an assumption

Full-copy sandbox availability and its refresh cadence must be confirmed with Marlex IT and treated as a dependency in the plan. If a full copy isn't available when needed, the rehearsal can't run at real volume — and without that, the wall-clock, the proven flip-back, the parity evidence and the go/no-go are all missing. The rehearsal plan is only as real as the sandbox it depends on.

How the options compare

Two full rehearsals versus one versus partial-only

Rehearsal is best judged by what each run buys — the value of the information it produces — against what it costs. Two full-copy runs, one, or partial-only aren't three points on a quality dial; each buys a different amount of certainty about the wall-clock and the flip the whole cutover rests on.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

At least two full-copy rehearsals Chosen

Two dress runs on a sandbox refreshed from production, each on a clean-or-understood baseline; each practises activation and the timed flip back to native.

  • Buys a measured activation wall-clock — the freeze-window number the business commits to — confirmed stable across two runs. almost certain
  • Buys a proven, timed rollback: the flip back to the old package exercised twice, not asserted. almost certain
  • The first run surfaces something big enough to need a third — schedulable because a retry was planned for. possibly

What it buys, and how big The only honest cutover date, plus a rollback that has actually been flipped. The cost is a limited sandbox window — cheap against a mis-sized freeze on the live business.

One run, or partial-only Rejected

A single full run, or rehearsing only in a partial sandbox.

  • One run: a first-run finding contaminates the only wall-clock and the only flip-back timing you have, with no clean second run to confirm either. probably
  • Partial-only: a sample activates and loads in minutes and tells you nothing about the real hours or the real flip-back time. almost certain
  • Partial-only: bulk-load chunking, row contention and referential failures across 1.4M records (measured) never surface until go-live. almost certain

What it buys, and how big Time and a scarce sandbox saved now — paid back as false confidence: an estimated window and an un-drilled rollback carried into the irreversible weekend.

The second run isn't redundancy — it's the confirmation that the first activation and the first flip-back were real. A single run can't tell a clean baseline from a lucky one; partial-only can't produce a volume number or a real flip-back time at all. Each full-copy run buys a specific, decision-grade fact — the activation wall-clock, the parity evidence, the timed rollback — that nothing cheaper can. The preference this serves is an honest, committable cutover date and a rollback that has actually been exercised in both directions. The asymmetry to guard against: if go-live later goes smoothly, that does not retroactively prove a rehearsal could have been skipped — the smooth go-live is what the rehearsals bought.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

Rehearsal is the one decision that is all upside on reversibility — it happens in a sandbox, so it costs no production risk at all, and its entire purpose is to make everything else reversible: it's where the flip back to native is drilled and where a failed run simply informs the next attempt instead of harming the business. The continuous-proof harness fails back just as gently: a failing dormant test blocks nothing live — that's the luxury of testing switched-off software, so the nightly red run is a to-do item, not an incident. The one piece that needs a fail-back rule is the same-change gate: when a genuine emergency fix can't wait for its counterpart, the rule bends — the fix ships, and the register records an explicit, dated mirror-debt entry that the escalating pre-cutover freeze forces to zero. Bent, never silently broken. The only real failure mode of the whole page is skipping it — going to cutover on an estimated wall-clock, an un-proven flip-back and months of un-checked drift — which is exactly the false confidence the harness and the two-run, clean-baseline discipline exist to prevent.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Rehearsal is only as representative as the copy. A full copy captures today's volume and shape; the real cutover runs against a drifted org, so counts are re-checked before the pre-load and again before the delta window is scheduled.
  • The probe can only show what it triggers. It reduces the black-box unknown sharply but can't prove the absence of every hidden reaction — so package-behaviour verdicts stay marked provisional even after it.
  • Pipeline automation is not yet chosen. Deployment and gate enforcement are review-based today; the tooling decision that automates them is open with Marlex IT. The harness is designed to start manual and harden — but the longer enforcement stays manual, the more the same-change rule depends on vigilance.
  • Suite duration at full scale. Six hundred-plus components' tests must finish overnight; suite speed becomes a maintained property (parallelisation, fixture reuse), or the daily drift signal degrades to weekly.
  • Equivalence depth is a judgment call. "Same business outcome" is provable; "identical in every edge case" is not — the walkthroughs and rehearsals bound that gap but don't close it.
  • The structural layer has no automated net. Screens, reports and permissions are proven by people on a cadence — the weakest link of the harness, mitigated by the activation checklist being enumerated rather than remembered.
  • Open: full-copy sandbox availability and refresh cadence — the dependency that sets the floor under the cutover date, still to confirm with Marlex IT.

Return to the overview, revisit Rollback — the flip back to native that this page's rehearsal drills and times — or read how the switch itself is run in Cutover. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and the closest documented package-replacement programmes.

Decision 14 · Delivering while rebuilding

Keeping the business moving without letting the two systems drift apart

The rebuild does not get a quiet org to work in. Marlex keeps shipping features to the business the whole time — so the plan needs a rule for how new and changed work is handled while the old system and its dormant replacement both exist.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 13 July 2026

The fact this page answers. There is no feature freeze during the rebuild. The recruitment operation keeps receiving new and changed functionality on its normal cadence — on the order of a delivery team's usual weekly throughput — and some of that work lands on the very objects the rebuild is replacing (contract handling reaching into the ERP link, the placement and candidate objects, is a live example). A rebuild that assumed a still target would silently fall behind the business it is meant to replace.

What we chose. Two rules, working together. New work is built native from day one — on the new model, reading old data through the shared seam, never extending the package — so it advances the rebuild instead of enlarging it. And changes to already-rebuilt old components are tracked: every to-be-retired component that has a dormant twin is registered, and if it is touched after its twin was built, the same change is carried into the twin in the same stroke. New work grows the new system; changed work never lets the twin fall behind.

Why this is a decision, not a footnote

A moving target is the dormant build's core risk

The whole strategy rests on building the new system ahead of time, switched off, and proving it correct before one rehearsed cutover (see Rebuild approach and Rehearsal). That bet is only safe if the thing being copied holds still long enough to be copied. It does not: the business keeps changing the live system underneath the dormant twins. Left ungoverned, that produces two failures at once — the new system grows (every new feature built on the package is one more thing to rebuild before the package can leave) and the twins drift (a fix applied to a live component but not its dormant counterpart silently regresses at go-live). Both are certainties at a real delivery pace, not risks to hope against. This page is the rule that turns continuous delivery from a threat into fuel.

The drift tax, stated plainly

The cost of running two systems in parallel is the change-rate of the coupled surface multiplied by how long the twins live. The business's ongoing delivery is the first term; it is real and cannot be wished away. The plan attacks both terms — the intake rule keeps most new work off the coupled surface entirely, and short twin-lives (see Deployment) keep the multiplier small — rather than relying on discipline to absorb an unbounded load.

The two rules

New work grows the new system; changed work never lets the twin fall behind

New work

Built native from day one, never on the package. A new feature is written on the new model and reads any data still living on old objects through the one shared seam — it does not add fresh automation, fields or logic onto the package's objects. A feature born native needs no twin, because there is no old counterpart to keep in step: it simply is the rebuild progressing. This is what keeps the disconnection scope shrinking rather than growing (the scope boundary it protects is on What's in scope).

Changed work

Touched-after-rebuilt is caught and mirrored. Every to-be-retired component that already has a dormant twin is listed in a register with an owner. The rule is one sentence: a change to a registered live component carries the same change into its twin in the same stroke — or records, dated, why it can't yet. The mirror obligation attaches at the moment the divergence is created, when the developer has the context fresh, not months later by archaeology.

Who does it

Ideally the same developer, in the same change. The cheapest way to keep a twin in step is for the person changing the live component to change its counterpart at the same time — they already understand the change. Where that isn't possible, the register makes the debt visible (a listed, dated, owned item) rather than silent, and the tightening freeze near cutover forces every open item to zero before go-live.

For the developersNew Apex is written on the canonical model behind a plain domain-service layer — no per-feature ACL. Where it needs data still on AVTRRT__ objects during coexistence, it reads through the single shared TR-read seam, never a direct package reference and never its own port/adapter/DTO stack (that is reserved for integration edges and any coexistence that survives cutover — see Rebuild approach). The paired-component register and the same-change rule are the enforcement mechanism, checked at code review from day one and by the deployment pipeline as it matures (the harness that runs them is on Rehearsal). Hard prerequisite: the native foundation — model, shared read seam, shared services — must be stood up early, before the bulk of new delivery, or new work has nowhere native to land and reaches for the package by default.

Classified at intake

The rule only works if every ticket is triaged as it arrives

A rule stated once and forgotten lets the coupled surface creep back up one ticket at a time. So the governance is at intake: as each piece of work enters the backlog it is classified — does it touch the coupled surface, and if so is it being built native? Three buckets cover everything, and each has a settled answer:

How each kind of incoming work is handled during coexistence
Incoming work How it's handled
New feature, needs only new/native data Built native on the new model. No twin — it is the rebuild advancing.
New feature, needs old data during coexistence Built native, reads old data through the shared seam. Still no per-feature twin; at cutover the seam re-points and the feature is unchanged.
Change to an already-rebuilt old component Applied to the live component and mirrored into its dormant twin in the same change — or logged as dated, owned mirror-debt.

The classification is deliberately coarse — three buckets a mid-level or junior developer can apply without an architect in the room. That is the point: a rule that needs expert judgement on every ticket will not survive a real delivery pace, so the rule is simple enough to hold under pressure and simple enough to be the same answer every time.

How the options compare

Govern delivery at intake, or freeze / let it run

There were three ways to handle a business that keeps delivering: freeze new work entirely (the assumption the plan originally leaned on), let it run wherever it lands and reconcile later, or govern it at intake so it advances the rebuild. The middle option is the trap; the freeze is off the table because Marlex confirmed there won't be one.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Govern delivery at intake Chosen

New work native-first; changes to rebuilt components mirrored in the same stroke; every ticket triaged as it arrives.

  • Continuous delivery advances the rebuild instead of enlarging it — new features are born on the new model. probably
  • The disconnection scope shrinks over time rather than growing under the business's own delivery. probably
  • The intake discipline erodes under deadline pressure and some work lands on the package anyway. possibly

Upside, and how big The business never stops delivering and the rebuild never chases a growing target — the one rule that makes no-freeze survivable.

Worst case Some coupled work slips through — but the register makes it visible and the tightening freeze forces it to zero, so the failure degrades gracefully rather than silently.

Let delivery run, reconcile later Rejected

Keep delivering on the old system as normal; sort out the divergence when the rebuild catches up.

  • No new process for the delivery team to learn — work continues exactly as today. almost certain
  • The coupled surface grows every sprint, so the rebuild is rebuilding a target that keeps moving away from it. almost certain
  • Months of unmirrored change surface as a reconciliation pile too big to clear before the window. probably

Upside, and how big Zero friction now — real, but borrowed against a reconciliation bill that arrives all at once, at the worst time.

Worst case The rebuild never converges: new coupled work is added faster than it can be replaced, and the deadline arrives with the package still un-removable.

The only way a no-freeze rebuild converges is to make new delivery part of it. Letting delivery run untracked is comfortable now and fatal later — the coupled surface it feeds grows faster than the rebuild can close it. Governing at intake turns the same delivery into progress: new work is born native, changed work is mirrored while the context is fresh, and the register catches what slips. The preference this serves is a business that never stops shipping and a rebuild that still finishes — and the rule is deliberately coarse enough for the team that has to apply it every day.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

This is a process decision, not a cutover step, so it has no runtime rollback — but it fails safe rather than silent. An intake slip surfaces as visible, dated items on the paired-component register (see Rehearsal), not a go-live surprise; and because new work is native-first, the worst realistic slip is a bounded handful of coupled changes to catch up, never an open-ended reconciliation. The rule bends under a genuine emergency — ship the fix, log the debt — but is never allowed to break silently.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The native foundation must be early. Native-first intake assumes there is somewhere native to build — the model, the shared read seam and the shared services stood up before delivery ramps. If the foundation lags, new work reaches for the package by default. This is a hard sequencing prerequisite, not a nicety.
  • Intake enforcement is a live process, not a one-off. Classifying every ticket needs to be owned by someone in the delivery flow (product owner or tech lead), not left to memory — an unenforced rule lets scope creep back one ticket at a time.
  • The in-flight contract-automation build is the first test. A feature reaching the ERP link and the placement and candidate objects is in flight as of this strategy (July 2026); whether it is redirected native-first or finishes on the package is the earliest and cheapest proof of whether the rule holds. It is a decision for the programme's technical governance.
  • Capacity is finite. The same team both delivers to the business and builds the rebuild; native-first reduces double-work but does not eliminate the load. How much can be built while still shipping is a capacity question that bounds the rebuild window (see Deployment).

Continue to Operating the new system for what watches the build once it's live, or revisit Rebuild approach for the native-first build rule this depends on. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and the delivery reality Marlex confirmed.

Decision 15 · Operating the new system

What watches the new system

On activation morning the native build runs real volume for the first time. This page decides how failures announce themselves, what the first weeks watch, and the written criteria the final removal of the old package must meet.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

The gap this closes. The strategy's other pages engineer how the new system arrives; this one engineers how it is heard. Today's operational visibility is thin, and — the sharper problem — most of what exists belongs to the old package and leaves with it. We verified the baseline in production: unhandled code failures route to a single shared mailbox, the only error-log objects in the org are the package's own, and 46 scheduled background jobs run with no consolidated view of their health.

What we chose. Build targeted, Marlex-owned observability into the rebuild itself: one native error log every new automation writes to, alerting routed by severity to named owners, a small set of go-live dashboards watching the flows that matter, a scheduled write-probe verifying the fallback stays untouched, and a technical hypercare period whose thresholds connect directly to the rollback criteria. Plus the end-game this enables: the package uninstall gets written go-criteria, so the final removal is a scheduled, evidenced change — not a leap.

Today's baseline

Measured: what would watch the new system if we built nothing

1shared mailbox receives every unhandled code failure in the org — no severity, no routing, no trend
46scheduled background jobs active, with no consolidated health view
0Marlex-owned error-log objects — the only error logs in the org belong to the old package and die at uninstall

That last number is the quiet trap: even the modest logging that exists today is package furniture. The programme that removes the package removes the org's error log with it. Observability isn't an optional polish on the rebuild — a slice of it is rebuild scope by necessity.

Alternatives considered

Inherit the mailbox, buy a platform, or build the targeted slice

Keep today's model — the mailboxRejected

Go live with unhandled failures flowing to one shared inbox. On an ordinary Tuesday that's survivable; on activation morning it's blindness — the one week when failure volume, novelty and stakes all peak is the week a mailbox saturates first. And the package-owned logs vanish at uninstall regardless, so "keep today's model" isn't even stable — it decays to less than today.

Adopt a full monitoring platformRejected (for now)

License the platform's event-monitoring add-on or a third-party observability suite. Real capabilities — and a new cost, a new tool to learn, and a scope far beyond what go-live needs, landing on a two-person IT team in the busiest year it will ever have. Nothing in the chosen design blocks adopting one later; the targeted slice below is what such a platform would consume, not a substitute it fights.

Build the targeted slice into the rebuildChosen

A native error log, severity-routed alerting, a handful of dashboards on the flows that matter, and a probe cadence on the fallback — each item earning its place by answering a specific go-live question. Small enough to build alongside the rebuild without ceremony; owned by Marlex, so it outlives the package it replaces.

The build

Four pieces, each answering a question

Error log

"What failed?" — One Marlex-owned log object; every new automation, integration entry and background job writes structured failures to it (what ran, what record, what error, how severe). It replaces the package-owned logs, and it ships dormant with the rest of the build. One place to look is the whole point — the org has never had one.

Alerting

"Who finds out, and how fast?" — Severity routes: a failed nightly job posts to the team channel by morning; a failing integration edge or a spike of load errors pages a named owner now. The shared mailbox stops being a monitoring strategy. Alert thresholds are set conservative for hypercare and relaxed deliberately afterwards — tuning down from loud is safer than tuning up from silent.

Dashboards

"Is it healthy?" — A small go-live set, built before the first rehearsal so the rehearsals exercise them too: inbound application flow (the job board's ~27,000-a-week feed arriving and landing), integration edge health (ERP, e-signature, credit check — successes, failures, retries), background-job completion, and error-log trend. Each dashboard exists because a rollback criterion or a hypercare threshold reads it — no vanity metrics.

The fallback probe

"Is the way back still clean?" — After go-live the old system must stay frozen and untouched to remain a valid fallback. That is enforced with permissions, and verified with a scheduled write-probe: daily in the first week, weekly after, confirming nothing and nobody still writes into the old objects. The probe reports onto the same dashboards; a detected write is a hypercare incident, because it means the known-good copy is silently diverging.

For the developersBaseline verified read-only in production: unhandled-exception notifications route to a single integrations mailbox; the only error-log entities are the package's (AVTRRT__Log__c, AVTRRT__Metric_Log__c — both destroyed at uninstall) plus other packages' internals; 46 scheduled jobs active. The native log = one MA_ object + a shared logger invoked from every new trigger handler, service, queueable and REST entry; platform unhandled-exception email stays on as the net under the net, re-pointed to a routed address.

Hypercare

The technical counterpart of the floor support

Change management puts people on the floor for the first weeks; this page puts numbers under them. Technical hypercare is a defined period — go-live to roughly week four, extended by evidence, not vibes — during which: the dashboards are watched on a rota with named daily ownership; alert thresholds run at their conservative settings; the fallback probe runs daily; and a short daily triage reviews the error-log trend against agreed thresholds. The thresholds are the important part: they are written down before go-live, and crossing the severe ones is what feeds the rollback invocation criteria on the rollback page — the go/no-go-back decision runs on dashboard evidence, not on the loudness of the person reporting the problem. Hypercare ends by review: error rates at agreed norms, no severe threshold crossed for two consecutive weeks, support ticket volume decayed to routine.

The end-game

Written go-criteria for removing the package

The uninstall is the programme's one true one-way door, and the fallback's dated expiry (set on the rollback page) puts it on a schedule. What was still missing is what must be true on that date. The gate's criteria, written now so nobody negotiates them during the event:

Nothing references it

Zero remaining references to any package component — verified with the platform's own dependency check, which blocks the uninstall while it detects a remaining reference. That check doesn't catch every kind of coupling, so the rebuilt-or-re-pointed component list is the real plan; the platform is only the backstop under it.

Nothing still writes it

The write-probe shows zero old-system writes for the agreed run of consecutive weeks — the fallback wasn't just unused, it was provably untouched.

Nothing is lost with it

All data in package-held fields verified extracted and checksummed — reconciled counts, not assumed. The platform's 48-hour post-uninstall export exists but is a safety net the plan never relies on.

The new system has earned it

Hypercare formally closed; error rates at norms; no severe threshold crossed since; the agreed clean-operation period completed.

The fallback has expired

The dated rollback expiry passed and forward-fix formally adopted — removing the package and retiring the fallback are the same decision, taken once, in writing.

Signed, scheduled, ordinary

Business sign-off recorded; the uninstall executed as a scheduled change with its own runbook — an administrative act, boring by design, because everything that made it dangerous was retired beforehand.

One reference already checked — and it's bounded

The "nothing references it" gate has a specific worry behind it: a set of small configuration records that point at the old package's fields by name, used to drive the switch between old and new. These have been reviewed against the platform's own capacity for holding such configuration, and both the period where the old and new versions sit side by side and the eventual clean-up stay well within it — so this part of the removal is bounded and carries no capacity risk. The one real action it leaves is a re-home: a single piece of switch-control configuration currently lives inside the package's own settings and would disappear with it, so it moves to a Marlex-owned setting before the gate. That's a relocation, not a limit problem.

The other teardown — the human accounts, not the components

Reclaiming user licenses and TargetRecruit-object permissions

The go-criteria above retire the package's own components. A separate teardown is easy to forget because it lives on the user accounts, not the metadata: every recruiter today holds a TargetRecruit user entitlement and read/edit permission on the package (AVTRRT__) objects. The rebuild's persona-based permission sets, granted at cutover, give them their access to the new model — but they do not remove the old access. Deciding when the old access comes off is a real timing decision, because it is coupled to the fallback.

The rule, and why the timing is not free

At cutover: grant the new, keep the old. Users get their persona permission sets on the native model, but their TargetRecruit license and AVTRRT__-object permissions are left in place — because the old system is held as the dated fallback, and a rollback would need those users able to work in it again. Stripping their access at go-live would quietly disable the fallback for the people who'd have to use it.

At the fallback expiry: reclaim the old. The user-entitlement teardown runs on the same gate as the uninstall — once forward-fix is adopted and the fallback retired: reclaim the TargetRecruit user licenses (a cost recovery, and often a contractual step), and remove the AVTRRT__-object permissions from the persona permission sets and profiles. A license or object permission left assigned is also one more reference that can complicate the uninstall.

What this depends on — the open question

Whether the TargetRecruit licenses can be held through the fallback window at all is the same unresolved question the deadline raises: if the license lapses at the vendor's end-of-support date, the fallback window is capped by it, and the reclaim is forced early rather than chosen. That question is owned by Marlex IT with the vendor, and until it closes this teardown's timing is provisional. If the licenses can be held cheaply, keep them for the full fallback window; if not, the fallback window shortens to the license horizon.

How the options compare

The targeted slice versus the inherited mailbox

The monitoring-platform purchase isn't the live fork — it's deferred, not opposed. The real comparison is between building the slice and going live with what exists.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Targeted native observability Chosen

One error log, routed alerts, go-live dashboards, the fallback probe — built with the rebuild, owned by Marlex.

  • A go-live defect is detected by a dashboard or an alert before it's reported by a recruiter. probably
  • The rollback decision, if it comes, is made on threshold evidence rather than anecdote. probably
  • Alert thresholds start mis-tuned and the first weeks include noise to calm down. more likely than not

Upside, and how big The highest-stakes weeks of the programme are observed by design — and the org keeps an error log after the package takes its own away.

Worst case Some build effort spent on dashboards that stay green — the cheapest bad outcome on any page of this set.

Inherit the mailbox Rejected

Go live with the shared inbox and the package's logs; add monitoring later if it hurts.

  • Nothing extra is built, and quiet first weeks make the gamble look retrospectively fine. possibly
  • A real go-live defect burns for hours because its only signal is an email in a shared inbox. possibly
  • At uninstall, the package's logs vanish and the org's observability drops below even today's baseline. almost certain

Upside, and how big The build effort saved — modest, because the slice is deliberately small.

Worst case A rollback-worthy incident recognised late and argued on anecdote — the exact failure the whole gated-rollback design exists to prevent.

Every other page of this strategy assumes someone can see. The rollback page's invocation criteria, the freeze's parity checks, the fallback's cleanliness, hypercare's exit — all of them read instruments this page builds. A quiet go-live does not retroactively prove the mailbox would have sufficed; it proves nothing was there to hear. The slice is small, it is rebuild scope anyway (the package takes the org's only error log with it), and it is what makes the strategy's own promises checkable.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

The observability build carries no rollback exposure of its own — it ships dormant like everything else, watches rather than acts, and a broken dashboard or mis-tuned alert is fixed as an ordinary change with nothing at stake. Its failure mode is subtler: instruments that lie — a probe that reports clean while something writes, a dashboard reading the wrong object. That is why the instruments themselves are exercised in the rehearsals (the rehearsal's known outcomes double as the instruments' expected readings), so by go-live the gauges have been seen working, not just installed.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The rota is two people deep. Marlex IT's operating capacity is small; the hypercare rota needs the partner's engineers in it, agreed in advance — a staffing commitment, not a technical one.
  • Threshold numbers need owners. The severe/normal thresholds that feed the rollback criteria must be agreed with the business before go-live — this page defines that they exist; it can't invent the numbers.
  • The job-board side is only half visible. The inbound dashboard sees what arrives; a failure on the partner's side (nothing sent) needs the partner's own confirmation channel during hypercare.
  • Alert fatigue is the known decay path. Conservative thresholds create noise, noise breeds muting, muting defeats alerting — the deliberate post-hypercare re-tuning pass exists precisely so relaxation is a decision, not an accumulation of ignored pings.

Continue to Integrations & middleware for how the rebuilt edges connect to the outside world. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and live monitoring-surface queries.

Decision 16 · Integrations & middleware

How the rebuilt edges meet the outside world

Every rebuilt integration ships behind a Marlex-owned interface with a direct connection first. The integration platform is adopted per edge class, additively, on its own timeline — the cutover never waits for it.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

The question. The group has selected MuleSoft as its integration platform, with licensing not yet in place as of this strategy (July 2026). The rebuild, meanwhile, touches every integration edge the recruitment system has — every point where it meets an outside system, whether Salesforce calls out or the outside system calls in — ERP, job board, e-signature, credit check, training certificates, the customer data platform — and adds one of its own: the bought matching-and-parsing engine. Do the rebuilt edges route through the new platform from day one, or stay point-to-point? Answered per-edge, ad hoc, later, this question quietly recreates the tangled coupling the rebuild exists to remove — so it gets one rule, decided here.

The rule. Every rebuilt edge ships behind a Marlex-owned interface (a port) with a direct connection as its first implementation. Routing an edge through the platform later is an additive change — a second implementation behind the same port, switched by configuration, no redeployment — taken per edge class when reuse across countries or systems earns it. The cutover has zero dependency on the platform's licensing, build-out or the team's capacity to operate it.

The principle

The port is the decision; the transport is a detail

What makes an integration expensive to change is never the wire — it's business logic knowing vendor specifics. The old package's deepest cost was exactly that coupling, and the org's one genuinely modern corner proves the alternative: the ERP client is already a cleanly separated family — the business logic calls a Marlex-owned interface and has no idea what sits behind it. The rebuild applies that shape to every edge. Once the port exists, "direct or via the platform?" stops being an architecture decision and becomes a configuration choice, reversible either way.

Alternatives considered

Platform-first, ports-first, or point-to-point forever

Route everything through the platform at cutoverRejected

The architecturally pure endpoint — reached by making the riskiest weekend of the programme depend on a platform whose licensing isn't final, whose build-out hasn't started, and whose day-two operation lands on a two-person IT team already carrying the cutover. Any platform incident becomes a recruitment-system incident, at the exact moment nobody can tell the difference from a rebuild defect. Purity is not worth a coupled go-live.

Point-to-point forever, no seamRejected

Rebuild each edge as a hard-wired connection and skip the port discipline. Cheapest per edge today — and it re-plants the coupling being paid down: the next platform, country or vendor swap becomes another round of open-heart surgery on business logic. The whole lesson of the package removal is what hard-wired edges cost to undo.

Ports with direct connections now; the platform per edge class, laterChosen

The seam is built where it's permanent (in the org, Marlex-owned); the transport behind it stays direct until the platform is licensed, operable and earning its hop for that class of edge. Decoupled timelines: the rebuild ships on the cutover's clock, the platform on its own. The cost: ports are a discipline every edge must actually follow, and the platform migration remains real future work — deferred deliberately, not forgotten.

The edges

The rule applied, edge class by edge class

Edge class What it does At cutover Platform route
ERP (NET4) Placements, offers and invoice traffic to and from the ERP Preserved, not rebuilt — already behind a Marlex-owned, vendor-free client; only its reads of the old model re-point Planned — first candidate once the platform is ready; multi-country ERP traffic is the reuse case that earns the hop
Job board (InfoJobs) Publishes vacancies out; ~27,000 applications a week in Outbound publishing behind a switchable service that can move ahead of cutover; inbound entry re-pointed with the core (see the guest door) Strong later candidate — its legacy protocol mediation and any second job board both argue for the platform
E-signature (Signaturit) Contract signature requests and signed-event callbacks Re-pointed direct — low coupling, single consumer Only if a second consumer or orchestration need appears; explicitly open, revisited per the rule, not by default
Credit check (Iberinform) Client solvency lookups Re-pointed direct — low volume, request-response Unlikely to ever earn the hop; stays direct behind its port
Training certificates (Humact / Lectio) Inbound certificate and enrolment documents Kept on its existing inbound endpoint; its rigid data attachment to the old model is restructured beforehand (see Data migration) No — direct inbound, low volume, single purpose
Customer data platform (Data Cloud) Feeds candidate and client data to marketing Its data streams re-point to the new objects at cutover — a standard-connector reconfiguration, not code Never — the platform-native connector is the seam here, so no port applies; a middleware hop would add cost and remove nothing
Matching & CV-parsing engine Parses CVs into structured profiles and ranks candidate–vacancy matches — the one new edge the rebuild adds, bought rather than built (worked in full on its own page) New — feed and query behind a Marlex-owned port with a direct adapter first; parsing-and-matching parity is a written go/no-go criterion Possible later — its high-volume feed could earn the hop once the platform is proven; behind the port it's a configuration choice
The long tail (connected apps) A dozen authorised external consumers — BI extracts, security-log shipping, assessment and sourcing tools, an AI screening intermediary Audited at cutover: each consumer confirmed live, re-pointed only where its objects moved, retired where dead Case by case, by the same rule — most never justify a hop

The last row hides the discipline that makes the audit possible: these consumers hit the platform's standard interfaces where the object name is the contract — there is no port to swap on Marlex's side, so each must be discovered from usage logs and re-pointed by its owner in step with cutover. The bought tools among them (the sourcing and screening connectors) carry a second, harder constraint: they ship a fixed connector that logs in through Salesforce's own authentication and reads through its standard interface, with no setting to aim them at the platform instead. So the "route it behind the platform later" option in the table above does not apply to them — not by choice, but because the platform can't stand in for Salesforce to a packaged connector. Their only lever is the Salesforce side: which data each is allowed to reach. The measured evidence for all of this — who calls, who writes, who only reads — is laid out on the measured surface; the per-caller cutover routes are on the cutover page; this page only fixes their long-term rule.

Naming & the seam's future

What the interface layer becomes after the old system is gone

During the transition, the port layer has a second job: it is the switch between old and new for the handful of capabilities that cross gradually. That job ends at uninstall — and the layer's naming should never memorialise it. Three naming rules, small now, priceless in five years:

  • Ports are named for the business capability, permanently — the placement service, the job-board publisher — never for the era ("new", "V2", "transition") or the vendor behind them. A name that encodes the transition outlives it as noise.
  • Implementations are named for what they talk to — the direct job-board adapter, the platform adapter, the old-system adapter. The old-system implementations are deleted at uninstall; because they sit behind ports, their deletion touches no business logic.
  • After uninstall, the layer's role shrinks and stays. From "the switch that made coexistence possible" to its permanent job: the org's integration boundary — the one place vendor and transport specifics are allowed to exist. It is not scaffolding to demolish; it is the load-bearing wall the scaffolding revealed.

How the options compare

Ports-first versus platform-first

Point-to-point-forever fails on the programme's own founding lesson and isn't compared. The live fork is timing: seam now and transport later, or both at once at cutover.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Ports now, platform per class later Chosen

Marlex-owned seam at cutover; direct transport first; platform adopted additively where reuse earns it.

  • The cutover proceeds on its own clock regardless of platform licensing, build-out or operating capacity. almost certain
  • A later platform migration happens behind the ports as configuration, invisible to business logic. probably
  • "Later" never comes for some edges and the platform's footprint stays smaller than envisioned. possibly

Upside, and how big The riskiest weekend of the programme sheds an entire external dependency — and loses nothing permanent, because the seam is what's permanent.
