Worst case Deferred work deferred too long — visible on a roadmap, cheap to restart, and behind ports either way.

Route everything through the platform at cutover Rejected

The target architecture delivered in one step, at the same weekend as the rebuild activation.

  • The end-state arrives in one move, with no second migration ever needed. possibly
  • Platform licensing, build-out or operations slip — and the cutover date slips with them. more likely than not
  • A go-live incident is ambiguous between rebuild and platform, doubling diagnosis time in the worst week to be slow. possibly

Upside, and how big One migration instead of two — genuine, but the second "migration" under the chosen option is a configuration switch, so the saving is small.

Worst case The recruitment system's go-live held hostage by another programme's procurement — a dependency nothing in this strategy needs to accept.

Buy the option, not the obligation. The port layer is cheap, permanent and needed under every future; the platform hop is valuable for some edges, later, on evidence of reuse. Coupling the two means the weekend that can least afford dependencies inherits one that contributes nothing to its success. The chosen shape gets the same end-state — it just refuses to pay for it with cutover risk.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

This is the one page whose rollback is genuinely a two-way door: the port-and-configuration design means any transport switch — direct to platform, or back — is a configuration change, rehearsable and reversible in minutes, per edge. A platform route that misbehaves falls back to its direct implementation without a deployment. The only thing to protect is the seam discipline itself: one hard-wired "temporary" bypass around a port, kept under pressure, and the two-way door starts rusting shut — which is a code-review rule, not a runbook step.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • Platform licensing and operating capacity remain open — deliberately outside this decision's blast radius, but the per-class adoption plan can't start until both resolve.
  • The e-signature routing call stays open by design — the rule says direct until a second consumer appears; someone must notice when it does.
  • A few consumer unknowns remain for Marlex to close. The usage census identified the live callers; what's left is a short list of dormant or in-development external apps whose owner and purpose Marlex must confirm, plus the AI screening intermediary — a new, not-yet-built path that runs alongside the job-board feed rather than replacing it — whose timing may still reshape what that feed carries.
  • Port discipline is cultural, not structural. Nothing physically prevents a hurried edge bypassing the seam; the review rule and the register keep it honest.
  • The inbound surface has now been measured. Six months of the platform's own usage records were sampled — nearly nine hundred thousand calls — to find every caller touching the retiring objects. Only the job-board feed writes to them; two smaller tools only read (one needs its query re-pointed, one just inspects the schema); everything else already runs on the new model. The per-caller routes are on the cutover page; the only residual is Marlex confirming a few dormant connections before the window.

This closes the decision set: back to the overview, or continue to Change management for the people side of the switch. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and the group's integration inventory.

Integrations · reference

The measured integration surface

The complete evidence behind the integrations decision: every edge where the recruitment org meets an outside system, in both directions, and what each one does at the core cutover. This is the census, not the argument.

Measured live · production org · 13 July 2026 · refined 16 July 2026 · living document

Read this if you need to see, edge by edge, exactly which outside connections touch the retiring data and which are already on the new model. The decision page sets the rule for how rebuilt edges connect; this page is the line-by-line evidence that tells us how many edges the cutover actually has to move. It closes the long-standing "the inbound surface isn't fully mapped" worry with counted fact.

The finding, in one line: of every outside system that touches the recruitment records, the job board clearly writes to the old objects, and a deeper measurement on 16 July found that a second connector may write to them too — so the edges the cutover must move are one confirmed, possibly two, not the single edge first reported. Everything else already runs on the new native or standard model and survives the cutover untouched.

Refined 16 July 2026 — a deeper read of the same logs. The first pass grouped the usage logs by the account that made each call. Because one integration account serves several different connections, that hid what each connection actually did. Re-reading the same logs grouped by connection corrected two edges and surfaced a class of connection the first sweep had not fully counted:

  • The reporting connection writes, it does not read. The connection previously described as a read-only reporting pull is in fact a data feed that writes into the platform — it pushes calculated figures (incentives, monthly margin per client) onto native objects, far more writes than reads. It runs under the finance/ERP integration account, not a reporting account, so it may be a data pipeline rather than the reporting tool it was labelled as. It touches only new native objects, so it is not coupled to the retiring model — that part of the earlier verdict holds — but the direction and nature were wrong.
  • The talent-assessment connector writes too. Over 30 days it both reads and creates application records on the old model. The earlier "reads only" was understated. One caution: this connector signs in as named people, so its own writes can't yet be told apart from those users working in the screen — the connector's owner needs to confirm. If it writes, it is a second edge the cutover must move.
  • A class of modern connection was under-counted. The standard connected-app lists do not show the platform's newer "external client app" connections; only the usage logs do. Reading those, five such connections are active — the two above, a security-log reader, a browser add-in, and internal developer tooling (not a real integration).

None of this changes the shape of the cutover, but it changes the count of write-coupled edges from one to possibly two, and it is on the technical agenda for the client's IT team to confirm.

How it was measured

Six months of the platform's own usage records, read-only

The platform keeps a detailed log of every programmatic call made against it. Those logs were read — never written to — to find every non-human caller that touched the recruitment objects being retired. The window is wide enough that a once-a-month integration could not hide in it.

194 daysof call logs retained and searched — six months, wide enough to catch monthly and quarterly jobs
888,709individual calls scanned across the sampled window — every caller touching the retiring objects
1write to the old objects across the whole window — and it was an administrator working by hand, not an integration

Of the scanned calls, roughly fifty thousand touched the retiring objects. The first sweep, grouping by calling account, found all but one to be reads. The 16 July re-read, grouping by connection, revised that: a talent-assessment connector both reads and writes those objects (see the note above). No other hidden write-coupled system surfaced in the six-month window. The result: the edges the cutover must actively re-point are few and now known — one confirmed, possibly two.

For the developersEvidence gathered read-only from EventLogFile (194-day retention; 21-day sample = 14 consecutive days + 7 monthly checkpoints back to December, 888,709 rows), plus ListMetadata, Tooling ApexClass bodies, LoginHistory, User, Site, ConnectedApplication and ExternalClientApplication. 50,391 rows touched AVTRRT__ objects; exactly one was a write, by an interactive admin. 418 custom Apex classes scanned. No writes to any Marlex org.

The callers that touch the old model

The job board writes; a connector reads and may write; one only looks

A small set of non-human callers touch the retiring objects, in cleanly different shapes — which is what keeps the cutover work per edge small and predictable. The 16 July measurement changed the middle row: the talent-assessment connector reads heavily and appears to write.

Caller What it does to the old objects What happens at cutover
Job board (InfoJobs) Writes. Applications land through the public site and create records in the old objects — the one genuinely write-coupled edge. Re-pointed to the new model, in step with the core cutover. The only re-point the programme itself controls end to end.
Data connector (Taleva) Reads, and appears to write. A live connector that reads the application object heavily and, on the 16 July measurement, also creates application records on the old model. Because it signs in as named people, its own writes can't yet be separated from those users' screen activity — the owner must confirm. Re-pointed on the connector owner's side, not ours — the mapping lives with them. If it does write, it is a second edge the cutover must move, in step with the job board.
Document tool (DocsMadeEasy) Only inspects the schema. It asks what fields exist to build merge templates; it never reads or writes a record. Re-map its merge templates to the new fields. No integration change at all.

Everything else reads or writes the native or standard model and is untouched by the cutover — including a recurring bulk job that refreshes roughly a million contact records, which writes the standard contact object, not the retiring ones.

For the developersInfoJobs writes via the guest-user site (MA_InfojobWebHook, /infojobsHook), targets confirmed from Apex — 84,002 calls in the 7-day attribution sample. Taleva (revised 16 Jul, attributing EventLogFile by CONNECTED_APP_ID over 30 days): ~110k reads and 392 writes — Contact (201), AVTRRT__Job_Applicant__c (99 POST creates), ContentVersion (92). Named-user OAuth, so connector-vs-human writes are not separable from logs alone. DocsMadeEasy: describe calls only. The ~1M-row recurring job writes Contact, not AVTRRT__. Prior by-user attribution reported Taleva as 119 reads / 0 writes; the by-app read supersedes it.

Inbound

Outside systems calling in — only the job board touches the old model

Customer data platform / marketing Standard objects — contact, account, opportunity, campaign (not the recruitment objects) Keep

Outbound

Salesforce calling out — all on the new model except the job board

The outbound edges follow the port rule set on the decision page: a Marlex-built edge gets a direct connection first and moves behind the integration platform later, per edge class, on its own schedule — never blocking the cutover. "Later lane" means direct until its turn comes, not direct forever. Only the CV-parsing engine is proposed to stay direct permanently (the connection once labelled a reporting pull turned out to be an inbound data feed — see the note above). Commercial data connectors are exempt from the platform hop for a product reason, not a preference: these are bought SaaS tools that ship a fixed Salesforce connector — they log in through Salesforce's own authentication and read through its standard interface, with no setting to aim them at a middleware address instead. There is no Marlex-owned edge to route them behind, and the platform cannot impersonate Salesforce for them. The only lever on these is the Salesforce side — which data each connector is allowed to reach.

Edge Platform sequencing State
Job board (InfoJobs) — vacancy publishing + CV pull, out Platform — next lane Re-points
ERP (NET4) Platform — first lane (multi-country reuse earns the hop) Preserved
E-signature, credit check, training certificates Platform — later lane Preserved
CV-parsing engine — replaces the old package's document reader Proposed stay-direct (latency-sensitive) Live
Finance data feed — writes incentives and monthly-margin figures in (was listed as a Power BI reporting pull) Inbound write, not outbound — identify owner Native model — preserved
Old package's bundled job-board and e-signature callouts Rebuilt per capability, then a platform candidate Retires with the package
For the developersCV parsing = CVParserCtrl → Anthropic_API (Claude + Groq), already live in prod, replacing the package's OCR. NET4 = MA_Net4Client, OAuth 12h token. Iberinform/Signaturit/Humact = named-credential callouts on MA_*. The "Power BI" PowerBiEmbedManager embed plumbing exists, but the live API Marlex Data Source external client app is an inbound upsert/delete feed onto MA_Incentivos__c / MX_Margen_Mensual_Por_Cliente__c (636 writes / 22 reads, 30d), running as the ERP-integration user — owner to identify. Package-bundled outbound (TRINTG__* — Daxtra, Idibu, SEEK, LinkedIn, Docusign…) retires with the package.

Rollback classes

Three ways an edge rolls back — and one Marlex does not control

If the cutover has to be reversed, an edge falls back differently depending on who owns the switch. This is the same reversibility logic the rollback decision turns on, applied to integrations: two classes are ours to flip, one is not.

Class Who owns the switch How it rolls back
Custom Salesforce web service (the job board) Marlex An interface-level switch added in the rebuild refactor — full rollback control, ours.
Platform-routed edges Marlex / integration platform Re-point the platform layer. Routing an old-model edge behind the platform before cutover is what earns this control.
Commercial data connectors (bought tools with a fixed Salesforce connector) External owner — not Marlex The external owner re-points their connector back to the old objects — the mapping and the connection both live in their product, and it can't be fronted by the platform. A path the programme does not control — so who re-points, in what window, on what signal must be agreed before the fallback window opens.

The one integration risk that is not ours to flip

A few external data connectors are expected to be reading — and, once the ERP work lands, editing — the old objects by the time the cutover comes. These are commercial tools with a fixed Salesforce connector: the connection and the field mapping both live in the product, so Marlex cannot roll them back or route them through the platform — their owners must re-point them. The mitigation is a living substitution inventory: for every old object and field left behind, the native object and field that replaces it, kept current and shared, so any owner — including an external connector's — knows exactly which old object to point back at. That map already exists; the discipline is keeping it current.

What's left to confirm

The census is complete on evidence; a few owners must confirm liveness

Everything measurable has been measured. What remains needs operational knowledge the logs cannot supply — the owner, liveness and purpose of a handful of connections — and belongs to Marlex IT to close, not to the architecture:

  • The planned job-board pre-filter is a separate, not-yet-built service with no traffic today; it does not replace the existing job-board feed. Its build date, and whether it will change what that feed still needs to carry, are open — as is who re-points it on a rollback, since it will connect as a caller-side data connector.
  • Two connected apps show no usage at all. If they are genuinely dead, they are dropped before the rebuild; if not, their owner and purpose need confirming.
  • One connector authenticates with full administrator access. It should be locked down to least privilege, and its owner must confirm they can re-point its query on a rollback.
  • The new CV parser is already live in production. Whether everything has switched to it, or the old package's reader is still partly in use, decides whether that is one fewer package dependency at cutover.
  • A final check for anything invisible. This census is built from what the platform's own logs reveal; Marlex IT confirms nothing writes in from outside that channel — an internal script, an external middleware, a scheduled bulk load. The recurring million-contact job proves mass inbound processes do exist, so the question is worth asking explicitly.

This is the evidence behind the integrations decision; the rule it supports — ports first, the platform per edge class later, the cutover never waiting — is argued there. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and read-only usage logs; no writes to any Marlex org.

Decision 17 · The archive

Where the records that don't board go

Half a million never-active candidates and seventy thousand finished placements and closed jobs don't earn a seat in the live system — this page decides where they live instead, and how they come back if needed.

Cutover strategy · draft for peer review · 12 July 2026

What this decides — and what it doesn't. Whether the never-active candidates are archived at all is a business call, staged with a recommendation on the data-migration page. This page decides the engineering half: if records are archived, where do they physically live, how are data-protection erasures executed against them, and what is the path back for the rare record that turns out to be needed. Deciding the destination now is what makes the business call safe to take.

Why it is now measured, not just argued. The record store is three-quarters full, and the data move temporarily holds the old records and their new copies at once. Counting that peak against the limit turns the archive from a quality preference into a sizing constraint — the numbers are below, and they set how much must go before the move.

What we chose. A governed cold store outside the live system, built from the cutover extract itself. The data move already extracts every record to a checksummed staging store before loading; archived records simply stay there, under governance, instead of boarding. Recall is exact and safe because every record carries its old-system identity key — recalling a record is the same idempotent load the migration itself uses.

The pool

What would be archived

460,847candidates who never applied to anything — 43% of the pool, the archive call staged with the business
~37Kfinished or cancelled placements — terminal history, never worked again
35,441closed jobs — and the closed applications hanging off them ride along

Two very different mechanics hide in those numbers, and conflating them is the classic mistake. The candidates already exist as person records in the org — "archiving" one means exporting it and then removing or retiring the record. The placements, jobs and applications are loaded fresh into new, empty objects — "archiving" one simply means not loading it, and keeping the extract. The second kind is nearly free; the first is a real operation with a retention-policy dimension.

Why the pool has a hard edge, not just a soft one

The storage headroom the data move consumes

Archiving the never-active tail has always been argued on quality — a clean search index, faster duplicate checks, a smaller data-protection surface. There is a second, harder reason, and it can be counted. The record store is three-quarters full today, and the data move temporarily makes it fuller: for the dormant-twin period the old recruitment records and their new native copies both exist at once, because the old package is removed only much later. That double-holding is a one-time peak the plan has to fit under.

75%of the record-storage limit already used before the move — measured on the live system
+2.6Mnative rows added while the old ones still stand — mostly applications and the candidate work-and-study histories
~92%worst-case peak if nothing is archived first — under the limit, but with little room to spare

The candidate records themselves add nothing to this peak — a candidate keeps the same record and only its fields move across, so no second copy is created (see Keep the standard objects standard). The peak is driven almost entirely by the applications and the work-and-study histories, which are loaded fresh into new, empty objects while the old ones remain in place.

The finding, stated plainly: even the do-nothing case fits — but only just. Migrating every record with no archiving lands the peak at roughly nine-tenths of the limit. That clears the bar, but it leaves almost no margin for the ordinary growth that happens during a rebuild that runs for months, and no margin for a rehearsal that loads a second full copy into the same org. So archiving first stops being only a quality choice and becomes the margin the move runs on: removing the rejected candidates and holding back the never-active tail brings the peak comfortably down into the low eighties, turning a tight fit into a safe one. Buying more storage is the fallback if the archive call slips — a couple of gigabytes would restore a comfortable ceiling on its own — but it is a fallback, not the plan.

The single largest confirmed lever is the roughly 195,000 candidates who declined data-protection consent — counted on the live system. They carry no business value and are deleted outright rather than archived, which frees live storage immediately and removes them and their dependent records from the move altogether. Because the recruitment records have almost no parent-child enforcement, that deletion is a manual sweep object by object, not a single cascade — a real task to plan into the cleanup, not a checkbox.

Alternatives considered

Three places an archived record could live

Keep everything in the live system, flagged as archivedRejected

Migrate every record, stamp the dead ones with an archived marker, hide them from default views. The easiest recall imaginable — the record is right there — but it pays, forever, every cost the archive exists to avoid: the search index carries half a million dead profiles, every report and duplicate-check runs over them, storage is billed at live-system rates, and the data-protection retention surface spans records nobody will touch again. If this were acceptable, the answer to the business call would simply be "migrate everything" and no archive would exist.

The platform's built-in cold storage ("big objects")Rejected

Salesforce offers a native archive tier for billions of rows. It keeps data inside the platform's compliance boundary — but archived records become second-class citizens: no ordinary reports or screens, retrieval only by pre-declared keys, and every consumer of the archive becomes custom work. For this programme's volumes (under a million rows, not billions) the tier's strengths never engage, while its awkwardness is paid on day one. It also does nothing for the candidate case, where the point is to remove records from the org, not re-house them within it.

A governed cold store built from the cutover extractChosen

The migration already produces the archive for free: a complete, checksummed extract of every record, taken at the freeze, keyed by old-system identity. Archived records stay in that store — governed, access-controlled, queryable for reference and audit — instead of boarding the load. Erasure requests delete by key; recall re-imports through the same load machinery the migration rehearsed twice. The store lives with Marlex IT's data platform, outside the org, at cold-storage cost.

Deleting the never-active candidates outright — no archive at all — was considered and set aside as a default: for finished placements and closed jobs it would destroy financial history, and even for never-active candidates, erasure is a retention-policy decision the business and legal owners must make deliberately. The chosen design keeps outright deletion available as a later retention sweep, executed against the cold store on a schedule the policy defines.

The mechanics

How the archive actually works

Getting in

At the cutover freeze, everything is extracted and checksummed — that step exists regardless (see Data migration). Records the business decided not to migrate are marked archived in the store and skipped by the load. For candidates, the equivalent records in the org are then retired per the retention policy — exported, verified, and removed or anonymised in a scheduled post-cutover sweep, never in the cutover window itself.

Living there

The store is governed: named owners, access on request, read-only. It serves reference lookups ("did this person ever apply?"), audits, and historical analysis. It is not a shadow system — nothing operational reads it, and no report presents it as live truth.

Erasure

A data-protection erasure request executes against the store by the person's identity key — delete the rows, log the erasure. Once the post-cutover retirement sweep has run, that leaves one place to erase, instead of a live system plus an archive. The scheduled retention sweep runs the same machinery in bulk.

Getting back

Recall is the migration machinery reused: the record's rows are re-imported through the same idempotent, external-key load path — it lands with its history intact, exactly once, even if recalled twice. A sample recall can be exercised in a migration rehearsal, so the path is proven, not asserted.

For the developersThe store = the ETL staging layer promoted to a retained artifact (same extract discipline: Bulk API, checksums, per-object row counts). Keys: MA_TR_Migration_Id__c values are reserved for archived records too — recall upserts on the same key. Candidate retirement respects the person-record subtlety: files and activities on retained Contacts are untouched; removed candidates are exported with their related rows (histories, memberships) as one keyed bundle.

How the options compare

Cold store versus flagged-in-place

The platform cold-storage tier falls out early — wrong volume class, and useless for the candidate case. The live fork is between keeping dead records in the org with a flag, and moving them out to the store the migration builds anyway.

Likelihoods below are educated guesses, not measured facts — an honest estimate beats refusing to give one. The scale: rarely under 10% · unlikely 10–30% · possibly 30–50% · more likely than not 50–70% · probably 70–90% · almost certain over 90%. A measured tag marks a counted figure, not a guess.

Governed cold store from the extract Chosen

Archived records stay in the checksummed migration store; recall by identity key through the rehearsed load path.

  • The live system starts clean — search, reports and duplicate checks run over working data only. almost certain
  • An archived record is genuinely needed and is recalled through the rehearsed path. rarely
  • Someone needs archive data in a hurry and finds the request-based access slower than a live lookup. possibly

Upside, and how big The archive is a by-product of work already committed — near-zero build cost for a clean live system and a single, governable place for retention and erasure.

Worst case A recall path defect — bounded, because recall reuses the load machinery the rehearsals already prove.

Flagged-in-place in the live system Rejected

Everything migrates; dead records carry an archived flag and hide from default views.

  • Recall is instant — the record never left. almost certain
  • 461K dead profiles (measured) sit in the search index, dedup checks and retention surface permanently. almost certain
  • Flag discipline erodes — automation, reports or users start touching "archived" records as if live. more likely than not

Upside, and how big Instant recall of records that, by definition, have had no activity in years — a large convenience for a rare event.

Worst case The new system inherits the old one's dead weight on day one, and "archived" becomes a polite fiction enforced by nothing.

The store already exists; the only question is whether the dead records board. Flagged-in-place optimises for an event the data says is rare (a never-active profile suddenly mattering) by paying a permanent, compounding cost — the same payoff-magnitude asymmetry that drives the archive recommendation on the data-migration page. The cold store collects the clean-system benefit every day, and its one real weakness — slower access — is priced as a request process, not a redesign.

Rollback for this choice

How this decision fails back

The archive is the rare decision here that is reversible by construction. Nothing is destroyed at cutover: every archived record sits in the store with its identity key, and the recall path is the same load the migration rehearsed. If the business call itself proves wrong — the never-active pool turns out to matter — the whole cohort can be loaded after the fact as an ordinary bulk import, off the critical path. The genuinely irreversible act is the retention sweep (actual erasure), which is why it runs on the policy's schedule, after go-live has settled, and never inside the cutover window.

Residual risk & open items

What we still carry

  • The retention schedule needs an owner. How long archived candidates are kept before erasure is a business-and-legal policy, not an engineering default — the store makes any answer executable, but someone must give it.
  • The store's home. Which Marlex data platform hosts it (file store, warehouse) is Marlex IT's call; the design constrains it only to governed access, keyed rows and checksums.
  • Historical reporting check. Confirm no live report needs record-level access to finished placements — invoicing history survives independently in the live system, but any report found reading terminal placement records must be re-pointed to an aggregate or the archive before cutover.
  • The archive-or-migrate call itself. Still with the business, due before the first migration rehearsal — this page only guarantees that either answer has a safe destination.

This is the last of the decisions — return to the overview for the whole set, or revisit Data migration, which decides which records board the new system and which come here. Grounded in the July 2026 production-org audit and live volume queries.
